ABSTRACT 
This study presents a comparative analysis of corporate social responsibility (CSR) and their communication in the states of Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand as a retrospective study over the financial years 2018–19 to 2022–23. The research examines how companies, operating in resource-rich and industrially significant regions, implement CSR programmes aimed at improving the socio-economic well-being of local communities. It focuses on key dimensions of societal betterment, including educational development, healthcare accessibility, environmental sustainability, and economic empowerment. The study further investigates how CSR interventions are communicated to beneficiaries through multiple channels, including community meetings, print and digital media, radio, television, conferences, and social media, assessing both the reach and effectiveness of these communication strategies. Employing a mixed-method approach, the research integrates quantitative data from 400 beneficiaries and qualitative insights from 40 stakeholders, including corporate representatives, implementing agencies, and community leaders. The findings reveal variations in CSR implementation and communication between the two states, highlighting the influence of socio-economic conditions, industrial composition, and cultural contexts on the design and effectiveness of CSR initiatives. This comparative analysis provides a nuanced understanding of how CSR contributes to societal development, emphasising the importance of strategic communication, stakeholder engagement, and alignment with community needs. The study offers actionable insights for policymakers, corporate decision-makers, and civil society actors seeking to enhance the impact and transparency of CSR programs in developing regions.

CHAPTER 01: INTRODUCTION
Corporate Social Responsibility (CSR) has increasingly become a central aspect of corporate governance, especially in resource-rich states of India, such as Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand, where industrial operations intersect with socio-economically vulnerable communities. This study adopts a retrospective approach covering the financial years 2018–19 to 2022–23, aiming to assess how CSR initiatives undertaken by companies have contributed to the betterment of society, and how these interventions have been communicated to beneficiaries.
CSR, as defined under India’s Companies Act, 2013, encompasses not only the implementation of social, environmental, and economic development programs, but also the processes through which such initiatives are systematically communicated to stakeholders, including community members, local authorities, and implementing agencies. In the selected states, CSR programs often target tribal and rural populations, focusing on education, healthcare, environmental sustainability, and economic empowerment. Communication channels for these interventions include community meetings, print and digital media, radio, television, conferences, and social media campaigns, thereby maximizing the reach and impact of CSR initiatives. 
The retrospective nature of the study allows for a detailed comparative analysis of five consecutive years of CSR activity, highlighting trends, best practices, and gaps in both implementation and communication. By integrating socio-economic indicators, sector-specific CSR data, and beneficiary feedback, this research provides an empirically grounded understanding of how CSR contributes to societal development while aligning with corporate objectives and legal mandates. The comparative focus on Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand enables an evaluation of state-specific variations in development outcomes and CSR effectiveness, offering insights into the design and dissemination of CSR initiatives in regions with similar industrial potential but differing socio-economic realities.
Corporate Social Responsibility (CSR)
In recent decades, corporate practices worldwide have drawn sustained public attention due to recurring contradictions between economic growth and social well-being. Instances of large-scale layoffs occurring alongside record corporate profits, widening income disparities, environmental degradation, unethical advertising, child labour, financial manipulation, and corporate fraud have raised serious concerns about the role of businesses in society. These developments have intensified debates within the domains of sustainability, business ethics, and Corporate Social Responsibility (CSR), particularly regarding justice, accountability, and responsibility toward both present and future generations .
Corporate Social Responsibility has emerged as a response to these challenges, positioning corporations not merely as economic entities but as socially accountable institutions. CSR refers to companies' responsibility to conduct their business operations in an ethically sound, socially inclusive, environmentally responsible, and economically viable manner. Unlike charity or philanthropy, CSR is concerned with how profits are generated, the processes adopted in production and service delivery, and the nature of engagement with stakeholders across the value chain .
The relevance of CSR becomes especially evident when examining cases of corporate misconduct. High-profile financial scandals, such as those involving falsified accounts, shell companies, and misuse of authority, have demonstrated how weak governance and unethical practices can undermine public trust, harm employees, and destabilize economic systems . Such incidents underscore the need to embed responsibility within corporate structures rather than rely solely on post-crisis corrective measures.
In contemporary corporate practice, CSR is often operationalized through mechanisms such as risk management, compliance frameworks, and codes of conduct to prevent legal violations and reputational damage. While these mechanisms play an important preventive role, they represent only a limited dimension of corporate responsibility. Compliance-based approaches focus primarily on avoiding wrongdoing, without necessarily questioning whether core business activities themselves contribute positively to societal development . Therefore, CSR must extend beyond regulatory adherence to examine how business models, communication strategies, and stakeholder relationships influence social outcomes.
A central feature of modern CSR discourse is integrating ethical considerations into a company’s core business activities. This includes fair labour practices, environmental stewardship, responsible sourcing, transparent governance, gender equity, human rights protection, and ethical supply chain management . Importantly, CSR cannot rely solely on individual moral conduct or leadership integrity. It requires institutional systems, organizational accountability, and effective internal and external communication mechanisms to translate ethical commitments into practice .
The role of the state in promoting corporate responsibility remains significant; however, its regulatory capacity is increasingly constrained in a globalized economy where corporations operate across national boundaries. While legal frameworks are largely territorial, economic activities are transnational, making enforcement complex and uneven . Consequently, corporate self-regulation, industry standards, and voluntary initiatives—often described as “soft law” mechanisms—have gained prominence. Instruments such as international guidelines and multilateral frameworks encourage corporate adherence to social and environmental norms beyond statutory obligations .
Within this evolving landscape, CSR communication becomes critical. The effectiveness of CSR initiatives is closely linked to how they are communicated to stakeholders, including employees, beneficiaries, communities, regulators, and the public. CSR communication shapes awareness, trust, participation, and perception, thereby influencing both social impact and corporate legitimacy . In the Indian context, particularly after the enactment of Section 135 of the Companies Act, 2013, CSR has transitioned from a voluntary practice to a regulated obligation, further intensifying the need for transparent, accountable, and culturally sensitive communication strategies .
This study situates itself within this broader theoretical and regulatory framework, examining CSR communication as a key determinant of societal betterment. By focusing on Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand, the research addresses a critical regional gap in CSR scholarship, especially in areas characterized by tribal populations, resource extraction industries, and socio-economic disparities. The comparative approach enables an assessment of how corporate communication practices influence beneficiary perceptions, stakeholder engagement, and developmental outcomes across different social and administrative contexts.
In recent decades, corporate activity has increasingly been marked by contradictions that have intensified public debate around accountability and responsibility. Large-scale layoffs alongside record corporate profits, widening executive–worker income disparities, environmental degradation, and climate-related risks have drawn attention to how business decisions affect society beyond financial outcomes . Simultaneously, ethically questionable advertising, child labour within supply chains, repeated corporate frauds, and global protest movements such as Occupy Wall Street have exposed gaps between corporate claims and social realities. These developments have positioned Corporate Social Responsibility (CSR) as a critical framework for examining not only corporate conduct but also how such conduct is communicated to stakeholders.
CSR has evolved as both a governance and a communication process through which corporations disclose information, justify decisions, and seek legitimacy in the public sphere. CSR does not operate in isolation from communication; rather, it is largely constituted through sustainability reports, media statements, stakeholder consultations, digital platforms, and public disclosures. The credibility of CSR initiatives therefore depends on the consistency between corporate actions and the narratives constructed around them .
The importance of ethical communication becomes evident when examining cases of corporate misconduct sustained through misinformation and lack of transparency. The Parmalat scandal in Italy revealed how falsified disclosures, fabricated entities, and controlled information flows enabled large-scale financial fraud to persist over time . Such cases demonstrate that ethical failure is often preceded by communication failure, where stakeholders are misled through manipulated reporting and selective disclosure.
In response to reputational and regulatory risks, many corporations have adopted compliance and risk-management communication frameworks. These include codes of conduct, internal guidelines, and standardized disclosures aimed at protecting corporate image and reducing legal exposure . While such measures play a role in signaling responsibility, they often prioritize reputational control over meaningful stakeholder engagement, thereby limiting their contribution to genuine CSR.
A communication-centered understanding of CSR extends beyond compliance and crisis management. It requires critical reflection on how core business activities are explained, justified, and evaluated through corporate messaging . CSR communication should not merely highlight philanthropic initiatives or social investments, but should focus on transparency in value creation, labour practices, environmental impact, and supply-chain governance. In this sense, CSR concerns how profits are earned and communicated, rather than how they are distributed after accumulation.
Corporations function not only as economic actors but also as producers of meaning through advertising, reporting, lobbying, and media engagement . CSR communication must therefore be integrated with everyday business operations, including employment relations, production processes, marketing strategies, and supplier management. Inconsistencies between operational practices and communicated commitments risk undermining trust and stakeholder confidence.
Effective CSR communication requires more than ethical leadership statements or formal disclosures. It depends on institutional structures that facilitate dialogue, feedback, and participation from stakeholders . Over-reliance on standardized reporting or codes of conduct may reduce CSR to procedural compliance rather than reflective engagement. Ethical communication demands continuous interpretation, responsiveness to critique, and openness to stakeholder perspectives.
The role of the state in regulating corporate responsibility remains important, yet its capacity is limited in a globalized economy where corporate operations transcend national borders . As a result, voluntary disclosure mechanisms, transnational reporting standards, and self-regulatory frameworks have gained prominence. Civil society organizations and media institutions act as intermediaries that scrutinize CSR claims, challenge inconsistencies, and amplify stakeholder voices .
In this context, so-called soft laws, including voluntary codes, industry agreements, and international initiatives such as the United Nations Global Compact, rely heavily on communication, self-reporting, and reputational accountability . While critics argue that CSR communication is often used for image management or greenwashing, evidence also suggests a gradual shift toward more structured and transparent reporting practices among some firms .
The CSR is inseparable from communication processes that shape how corporate behavior is understood, assessed, and contested. It is not equivalent to post-profit philanthropy, but a communicative practice embedded within value creation, disclosure, and stakeholder interaction. For societies seeking equitable and sustainable development, responsible corporate communication plays a decisive role. While corporations alone cannot create a just society, such a society cannot be achieved without transparent, accountable, and participatory CSR communication practices.
Corporate Social Responsibility (CSR) in India
Corporate Social Responsibility (CSR) refers to a company's commitment to operate in an economically, socially, and environmentally sustainable manner while balancing the interests of diverse stakeholders. In India, CSR has evolved from a voluntary philanthropic gesture to a legally mandated aspect of corporate governance, as outlined in Section 135 of the Companies Act, 2013. This legislation requires companies that meet specific financial thresholds to allocate at least 2% of their average net profits over the preceding 3 years to CSR activities in areas such as education, healthcare, environmental sustainability, and rural development. This regulatory framework represents a significant policy innovation, positioning India as the first country to legislate CSR spending and transforming it into a strategic tool for national development.
The regulatory framework for CSR in India has evolved from voluntary guidelines to a structured mandatory regime. While the national framework provides the foundation, states like Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand have developed complementary mechanisms to align CSR spending with local development priorities. Both states have established sophisticated systems for CSR governance, though implementation challenges remain. The comparative analysis reveals that while Chhattisgarh has focused on systematic planning and aligning with the SDGs, Jharkhand has emphasised needs assessment and tribal development. Both approaches offer valuable lessons for other resource-rich states seeking to leverage CSR for development objectives.
The regulatory landscape for Corporate Social Responsibility (CSR) in India is predominantly shaped by national legislation, with state governments playing a complementary role in implementation and facilitation.
The Companies Act, 2013 (Section 135)
The cornerstone of India's CSR regime is Section 135 of the Companies Act, 2013, which came into effect on April 1, 2014. This landmark legislation mandated that companies meeting any of the following criteria must constitute a CSR Committee and spend at least 2% of their average net profits of the immediately preceding three financial years on CSR activities:
1.	Net worth of ₹500 crore or more
2.	Turnover of ₹1,000 crore or more
3.	Net profit of ₹5 crore or more
  
Figure 1 Criteria for a company to have a CSR Committee and spend on CSR Activities
 

Evolution of Corporate Social Responsibility (CSR) 
Corporate Social Responsibility in India has evolved from informal philanthropic traditions to a structured, legally mandated framework integrated within corporate governance. Historically, Indian business houses such as the Tata Group, Birla Group, and Bajaj Group engaged in social welfare activities rooted in ethical values, community development, and trusteeship principles advocated during the pre-independence period . These early practices, though voluntary, laid the foundation for contemporary CSR by emphasizing the social role of business beyond profit generation.
The formalization of CSR in India gained momentum in the post-liberalization era, particularly after the 1991 economic reforms. As  participation expanded and multinational corporations entered the Indian market, expectations for corporate accountability, transparency, and social engagement increased. During this phase, CSR remained largely voluntary, guided by international frameworks, sustainability reporting standards, and corporate self-regulation . However, the lack of uniform implementation led to uneven CSR performance across sectors and regions.
A significant shift occurred with the enactment of the Companies Act, 2013, which made India the first country to mandate CSR expenditure through statutory provisions. Section 135 of the Act requires companies meeting specified financial thresholds to allocate at least two percent of their average net profits from the preceding three financial years toward CSR activities . This legislative intervention transformed CSR from a discretionary initiative into a structured corporate obligation, accompanied by formal governance mechanisms, including CSR committees, policy disclosures, and reporting requirements.
Schedule VII of the Companies Act outlines permissible CSR activities, including initiatives related to education, healthcare, sanitation, poverty alleviation, environmental sustainability, gender equality, rural development, and the welfare of marginalized communities . This framework aligns CSR objectives with national development priorities, enabling corporations to complement public welfare efforts while retaining operational autonomy. Importantly, CSR activities must be implemented directly by companies or through registered implementing partners, such as non-governmental organizations, to reinforce accountability and the traceability of funds.
In practice, CSR implementation in India reflects considerable variation across industries, ownership structures, and geographical regions. Public Sector Undertakings (PSUs), private corporations, and multinational enterprises differ in their approach, scale, and focus areas. Resource-intensive industries such as mining, power, and steel—prominent in states like Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand—tend to concentrate CSR investments in health, education, skill development, and environmental management due to their operational impact on local communities . Despite substantial financial allocations, disparities persist in project quality, beneficiary participation, and long-term outcomes.
CSR communication has emerged as a critical component of the Indian CSR framework. Effective communication determines stakeholder awareness, trust, and engagement, influencing both perceived and actual impact. Indian companies employ a range of communication channels, including annual CSR reports, sustainability disclosures, digital platforms, community meetings, and local partnerships. However, studies indicate that CSR communication in many regions remains one-directional, focusing on disclosure rather than dialogue, thereby limiting beneficiary feedback and participatory decision-making .
Regulatory oversight of CSR in India has been strengthened through amendments and guidelines issued by the Ministry of Corporate Affairs. Recent provisions emphasize impact assessment, unspent CSR funds, and greater transparency in reporting, particularly for large-scale projects . These measures reflect a gradual shift from expenditure-based evaluation to outcome-oriented assessment, reinforcing the need for systematic monitoring and communication strategies.
Despite regulatory advancements, several challenges continue to shape CSR practice in India. These include limited community involvement, inadequate capacity of implementing agencies, regional imbalances in CSR spending, and the tendency to treat CSR as a compliance exercise rather than a development-oriented process . Addressing these challenges requires stronger alignment between corporate strategy, stakeholder engagement, and communication practices.
Within this context, CSR in India represents a complex interaction between law, corporate governance, societal expectations, and communication processes. Understanding how CSR initiatives are designed, implemented, and communicated at the regional level is essential for evaluating their contribution to social development. This study examines these dynamics through a comparative analysis of CSR communication practices in Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand, with particular attention to beneficiary perceptions and sectoral outcomes.
 Companies (CSR Policy) Rules, 2014
The Ministry of Corporate Affairs (MCA) notified the Companies (Corporate Social Responsibility Policy) Rules, 2014, which provided detailed guidelines on:
•	Composition and functions of the CSR Committee
•	Formulation of CSR Policy
•	Implementation of CSR projects
•	Reporting and disclosure requirements
•	Monitoring mechanisms
Subsequent Amendments and Clarifications (2015-2025)
The MCA (Ministry of Corporate Affairs) issued multiple circulars and amendments to address implementation challenges:
•	2015: Clarification on treatment of surplus arising from CSR projects
•	2018: Guidance on registration of implementing agencies
•	2019: Amendment Rules requiring unspent CSR amounts to be transferred to specified funds within six months of the financial year end
•	2020: COVID-19-related activities included as eligible CSR expenditure; PM CARES Fund recognized for CSR contributions
•	2021: Comprehensive CSR Rules amendment strengthening governance and monitoring, including:
1.	Impact assessment requirements for projects exceeding ₹1 crore
2.	CSR Committee composition and responsibility clarifications
3.	Enhanced disclosure requirements in Board reports
•	2023: Further amendments to Schedule VII expanding eligible CSR activities, including sports training for national and international representation
•	2024: Clarifications on CSR implementation through corporate foundations and trusts; guidance on administrative overheads capped at 5% of total CSR expenditure
•	2025: Continued focus on monitoring and compliance mechanisms; enhanced digital reporting requirements through MCA-21 portal (as of January 2025)
National CSR Portal
In 2021, the Ministry of Corporate Affairs (MCA) launched the National CSR Portal (csr.gov.in) as a unified platform for:
•	CSR spending disclosure
•	Project monitoring
•	Implementing agency registration
•	Best practices sharing
Corporate Social Responsibility (CSR) Communication
Corporate Social Responsibility communication refers to the systematic process through which companies convey information about their CSR initiatives, their implementation processes, and the resulting impacts to relevant stakeholders. While CSR focuses on what companies do for social and environmental development, CSR communication addresses how these actions are shared, interpreted, and understood by beneficiaries, communities, institutions, and the wider public. In this sense, CSR communication serves as a critical link between corporate intent, social intervention, and stakeholder perceptions.
Operationally, CSR initiatives are typically conceptualized and approved by a company’s CSR committee or designated CSR team in accordance with statutory and strategic objectives. These initiatives may be implemented directly by the company or through external implementing agencies such as non-governmental organizations or specialized foundations. Following implementation, social, educational, health-related, environmental, or livelihood (Economic empowerment)-related impacts emerge at the community level. CSR communication encompasses the entire process through which these initiatives and their outcomes are conveyed to stakeholders before, during, and after implementation.
CSR communication operates across multiple stages of the CSR cycle. At the initial stage, communication is used to raise awareness among target communities about the proposed interventions, objectives, eligibility criteria, and participation mechanisms. During implementation, communication facilitates engagement, trust-building, and coordination between companies, implementing agencies, and beneficiaries. In the post-implementation phase, CSR communication serves to report outcomes, document impact, and demonstrate accountability. Thus, CSR communication is not limited to publicity or disclosure but functions as an ongoing interaction between corporations and society.
A wide range of communication channels is employed in CSR interventions, particularly in community-based development contexts. These include interpersonal and participatory methods such as community meetings, village-level consultations, focus group discussions, door-to-door outreach, and stakeholder workshops, often facilitated by implementing agencies. Such direct communication channels are especially relevant in rural and tribal regions, where literacy levels may be uneven, and trust is built through face-to-face interaction.
Alongside interpersonal communication, mediated channels play a significant role in CSR communication. These include print media reports, banners, posters, wall paintings, brochures, newsletters, conferences, and public events. Electronic and digital media—such as television coverage, radio programmes, corporate websites, social media platforms, and digital storytelling—are increasingly used to amplify CSR narratives and reach broader audiences beyond immediate beneficiary communities. Each channel serves distinct purposes, ranging from local mobilization to reputational positioning and regulatory disclosure.
The effectiveness of CSR communication depends not only on channel selection but also on message framing, cultural relevance, language, and feedback mechanisms. One-way promotional communication focused solely on corporate image may limit stakeholder trust and participation. In contrast, dialogic and participatory communication models encourage mutual understanding, beneficiary voice, and shared ownership of CSR initiatives. Therefore, CSR communication must be evaluated not merely by visibility but by its ability to facilitate inclusion, transparency, and responsiveness.
In the Indian context, CSR communication has gained increased importance following the statutory mandate under the Companies Act, 2013. Companies are required to disclose their CSR policies, expenditures, and project details in annual reports and on digital platforms. However, formal disclosure alone does not ensure effective communication at the community level. Gaps often exist between corporate reporting and beneficiary awareness, particularly in regions with limited media access and socio-economic vulnerability.
From a communication research perspective, CSR communication directly influences stakeholder awareness, trust, participation, and satisfaction. It shapes how communities perceive corporate intent, how beneficiaries engage with interventions, and how social impact is interpreted and sustained over time. Consequently, CSR communication should be understood as an integral component of CSR practice rather than a post-implementation activity or image-management exercise.
Within this framework, CSR communication can be defined as the structured use of interpersonal, organizational, and mediated communication channels through which CSR initiatives, implementation processes, and social impacts are conveyed to stakeholders, particularly beneficiary communities. This study adopts this understanding to examine how CSR initiatives in Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand are communicated, how beneficiaries receive and interpret these messages, and how communication practices influence the perceived effectiveness of CSR interventions.
Conceptual Framework of the CSR Communication Process
Corporate Social Responsibility (CSR) is not complete without effective communication of its initiatives and impacts. While CSR defines what a company does to contribute to social, environmental, and economic development, the CSR communication process explains how these actions are conveyed, perceived, and interpreted by stakeholders, particularly beneficiaries . The conceptual framework of CSR communication integrates the design, implementation, impact, and dissemination of CSR initiatives into a structured model that reflects both managerial strategy and stakeholder engagement.
The framework begins with the CSR initiative phase, where a company, guided by its CSR committee or dedicated CSR team, identifies social, environmental, or developmental interventions aligned with strategic objectives, regulatory requirements, and stakeholder needs. Initiatives may focus on sectors such as education, healthcare, skill development, environmental sustainability, or community welfare. These initiatives can be implemented either directly by the company or through an external implementing partner, typically a registered non-governmental organization (NGO), foundation, or community-based organization . Selecting an implementing agency is crucial for ensuring local knowledge, community trust, and efficient delivery of CSR activities.
Following implementation, the CSR process generates measurable and observable social impact at the community level. Impacts may include improvements in literacy rates, health outcomes, employment opportunities, environmental conservation, or community infrastructure . These outcomes form the evidence base for CSR communication and are essential for demonstrating accountability, transparency, and social responsibility.
The communication component of the framework focuses on how initiatives and their resulting impacts are transmitted to stakeholders. CSR communication employs multiple channels tailored to the target audience and context. These channels can be broadly categorized into:
1.	Community-based and participatory methods: Direct engagement through village or community meetings, workshops, beneficiary consultations, awareness campaigns, and on-site demonstrations. These methods are particularly effective in rural or marginalized communities where trust and interpersonal relationships are key .
2.	Print and visual media: Reports, brochures, banners, posters, newsletters, and public exhibitions that provide tangible information about CSR activities and progress. Print media allows for documentation and archival of CSR practices .
3.	Electronic and digital channels: Television, radio, social media, corporate websites, and mobile-based communication platforms. Digital channels enable wider reach, including urban stakeholders, regulators, and civil society actors, and facilitate real-time updates and interactive engagement .
4.	Stakeholder and institutional communication: Conferences, seminars, industry forums, and multi-stakeholder dialogues help communicate CSR outcomes to policymakers, investors, and implementing partners while promoting best practices and collaborative learning .
Effective CSR communication is bidirectional, emphasizing not only dissemination but also feedback collection from beneficiaries and other stakeholders. This interaction enhances participation, builds trust, strengthens accountability, and improves the design and implementation of future CSR initiatives . Moreover, communication must reflect cultural, linguistic, and social sensitivities to ensure accessibility, comprehension, and relevance for diverse communities.
In summary, the conceptual framework of the CSR communication process can be represented as a cyclical model comprising four interlinked stages:
1.	CSR Initiative Design – Strategic planning, alignment with company goals, and identification of stakeholder needs.
2.	Implementation – Direct execution by the company or through an implementing agency/NGO.
3.	Impact Generation – Observable social, environmental, or economic changes in beneficiary communities.
4.	Communication – Transmission of initiative objectives, processes, and outcomes through multiple channels and feedback mechanisms.
This framework underscores the centrality of communication in bridging corporate actions and societal outcomes. In the context of Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand, understanding this process is critical to evaluating how CSR interventions are perceived by beneficiaries, how awareness is generated, and how trust, participation, and satisfaction are shaped by the communication strategies adopted by companies .  
Figure 2 CSR Communication Process — Cyclical Model 

Interpretation. This CSR communication model presents CSR as a continuous cycle where feedback, implementation, impact, and communication together drive societal betterment. The Betterment of Society
1. Conceptual Understanding
In the context of Corporate Social Responsibility (CSR), “betterment of society” refers to measurable improvements in the socio-economic well-being and quality of life of communities affected by corporate activities. It extends beyond mere charitable contributions to encompass systemic enhancements in education, health, environment, and livelihood (Economic empowerment). The concept operationalizes societal betterment by aligning corporate interventions with national development priorities and the United Nations Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs). This ensures that CSR initiatives contribute meaningfully to long-term social transformation rather than short-term relief.
2. Social Indicators
Societal betterment can be assessed using both objective and subjective indicators:
•	Educational Development: Enrollment rates, literacy levels, school infrastructure quality, reduced dropout rates, and access to vocational or skill-development programs.
•	Healthcare Accessibility: Availability of primary and preventive healthcare services, health infrastructure, immunization coverage, maternal and child health indicators, and sanitation facilities.
•	Environmental Sustainability: Measures of natural resource conservation, pollution reduction, adoption of renewable energy, waste management, and biodiversity protection.
•	Economic Empowerment: Household income, employment orlivelihood (Economic empowerment) generation, skill development outcomes, and participation in self-help or microfinance programs.
Subjective indicators include community awareness, participation, and satisfaction with CSR initiatives. These capture beneficiaries’ perceptions of relevance, effectiveness, and accessibility, offering insight into the social impact beyond quantitative data.
3. CSR Link
Corporate Social Responsibility directly contributes to the betterment of society by designing and implementing targeted interventions in the dimensions outlined above. Companies, guided by CSR committees, identify community needs, allocate resources, and ensure project delivery either directly or through implementing agencies/NGOs. For example:
•	Educational programs may involve school construction, scholarships, or teacher training.
•	Healthcare initiatives may include medical camps, hospital equipment support, or preventive health awareness campaigns.
•	Environmental projects may involve tree plantation drives, water conservation, or renewable energy projects.
•	Economic empowerment can include skill training, entrepreneurship programs, or livelihood (economic empowerment) support.
Through these structured initiatives, CSR transforms corporate resources into measurable improvements in community well-being, demonstrating the practical link between business activities and social development.
4. CSR Communication Link
The impact of CSR on societal betterment is significantly mediated by communication. Effective CSR communication ensures that stakeholders—including beneficiaries, local authorities, and wider society—are aware of, understand, and participate in initiatives. Channels of communication include:
•	Community-level engagement: Meetings, consultations, workshops, focus group discussions, and door-to-door awareness campaigns, often facilitated by implementing agencies.
•	Print and visual media: Banners, posters, brochures, newsletters, and annual reports.
•	Electronic and digital media: Television, radio, corporate websites, social media platforms, and mobile-based messaging.
•	Institutional communication: Conferences, seminars, and multi-stakeholder dialogues with local authorities, NGOs, and civil society actors.
Effective communication enhances trust, awareness, participation, and satisfaction, enabling communities to recognize and benefit from CSR interventions. Without structured communication, even well-designed CSR initiatives may fail to reach their intended social impact, highlighting the interdependence between CSR implementation and communication.
The Society
For the purpose of this study, “society” refers specifically to the community-level beneficiaries of Corporate Social Responsibility (CSR) initiatives in selected districts of Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand. This definition allows the research to focus on populations directly impacted by corporate operations and CSR interventions, enabling a precise examination of outcomes and communication effectiveness.
The study considers the following segments within society:
1.	Tribal and Rural Populations: Communities residing in areas affected by industrial operations, mining, or infrastructure projects, particularly in resource-rich regions where environmental and economic impacts are significant.
2.	Direct Beneficiaries of CSR Programs: Individuals and households participating in CSR initiatives in sectors such as education, healthcare, skill development, environmental management, and livelihood (Economic empowerment).
3.	Local Stakeholders: Representatives of community organizations, members of Panchayati Raj Institutions, local NGOs, and other actors engaged in facilitating or monitoring CSR activities.
4.	Women and Marginalized Groups: Specific attention is given to women, Scheduled Tribes, Scheduled Castes, and other vulnerable populations who often constitute primary targets of CSR interventions due to historical socio-economic disadvantages.
This focused conceptualization of society provides a framework to assess how CSR communication strategies influence awareness, participation, trust, and satisfaction among targeted community groups. By concentrating on clearly defined beneficiaries, the study can explore variations in communication effectiveness across different demographic, socio-economic, and geographical contexts, particularly in districts characterised by unique challenges such as limited literacy, infrastructural gaps, and socio-cultural diversity.
Socio-Economic Profile of Chhattisgarh
1. Historical and Political Context
Chhattisgarh was carved out of the southeastern districts of Madhya Pradesh and formally established as India’s 26th state on November 1, 2000 . The state was created to address regional disparities and ensure better governance, particularly in resource-rich areas with significant tribal populations. Its administrative capital is Raipur, with other important urban centers including Bilaspur, Durg, Korba, and Bhilai . The state's political landscape is characterized by a multi-party system, with governance alternating between regional and national parties. Local governance is strengthened through Panchayati Raj Institutions (PRIs), which play a crucial role in planning and implementing developmental programs, including CSR-related initiatives .
2. Geography and Natural Resources
Chhattisgarh is geographically located in central India, covering 135,192 square kilometers, and is endowed with rich natural resources . The state lies on the Chhattisgarh plain, surrounded by hilly and forested regions, which are home to a significant tribal population. Chhattisgarh is among the top mineral-producing states in India, with major reserves of:
•	Coal: Contributing nearly 15% of India’s total coal production.
•	Iron Ore: Supporting steel industries, particularly in Durg-Bhilai and Raipur.
•	Limestone: A key input for cement and steel industries.
•	Bauxite, dolomite, and manganese: Supporting aluminum and metallurgical industries .
Forests cover around 41% of the state’s geographical area, supporting biodiversity, minor forest produce, and traditional livelihood (Economic empowerment) for tribal communities .
3. Demographic Profile
As per the 2011 Census of India, Chhattisgarh has a population of 25.5 million, with an annual growth rate of approximately 1.6% . The population is distributed between urban (35%) and rural (65%) areas, with the majority engaged in agriculture and allied sectors. Key demographic features include:
•	Tribal Population: Constituting nearly 31% of the state population, including Gond, Baiga, Halba, and other tribes .
•	Scheduled Castes: Represent around 12% of the population.
•	Gender Composition: Sex ratio stands at 991 females per 1,000 males, reflecting relatively better gender balance than the national average.
•	Literacy Rate: Overall literacy is 71%, with a gap between male literacy (81%) and female literacy (61%) .
These demographic characteristics are particularly relevant to CSR interventions, as tribal and marginalized communities are often primary beneficiaries of corporate social responsibility programs in the state.
4. Economic Structure
Chhattisgarh has emerged as one of India’s fastest-growing economies, largely due to industrialization and mineral exploitation . The state’s Gross State Domestic Product (GSDP) has shown consistent growth, averaging 6–7% annually over the past decade . The economic structure can be described in terms of three sectors:
•	Primary Sector: Agriculture remains a significant employer, with rice as the principal crop, earning Chhattisgarh the title of “Rice Bowl of India.” Other crops include maize, pulses, and horticultural produce. Forests and minor forest produce contribute to tribal livelihood (economic empowerment) .
•	Secondary Sector: Industry and manufacturing form the backbone of the economy. Chhattisgarh hosts steel plants (Bhilai Steel Plant), cement factories, power plants, and aluminium units, supported by abundant mineral resources. Industrial clusters in Raipur, Durg-Bhilai, Korba, and Raigarh attract both domestic and foreign investment .
•	Tertiary Sector: Services, including banking, education, healthcare, and trade, are expanding, particularly in urban centers. Information technology and tourism are emerging sectors, though they still account for a small share of overall GSDP .
Despite strong industrial growth, a large proportion of the population continues to depend on agriculture and informal labor, highlighting the need for inclusive growth strategies.
5. Human Development Indicators
While Chhattisgarh demonstrates robust economic performance, human development indicators reveal significant challenges :
•	Health: Infant mortality rate stands at 38 per 1,000 live births, and maternal mortality rate is 221 per 100,000 live births, both above the national average. Malnutrition remains a critical concern in tribal and rural areas .
•	Education: The state faces high dropout rates, particularly among girls and tribal students. School infrastructure and teacher availability remain uneven across districts .
•	Poverty: Approximately 30% of the population lives below the poverty line, with rural and tribal households disproportionately affected .
These human development gaps make the state a key focus for CSR initiatives in education, healthcare, livelihood (Economic empowerment) promotion, and women empowerment.
6. Social and Cultural Context
Chhattisgarh is home to over 70 distinct tribes, each with unique languages, customs, and traditional knowledge systems . Social structures are strongly influenced by tribal governance, local community norms, and Panchayati Raj institutions. Festivals, folk arts, and traditional crafts are integral to social life, which provides both opportunities and challenges for CSR engagement. Culturally sensitive approaches in CSR planning and communication are essential to ensure community participation and sustainability .
7. Industrial and CSR Landscape
Given the state’s resource-rich regions, industrial operations often impact local communities, creating both opportunities and responsibilities for companies. Major industrial players in steel, power, cement, and mining sectors implement CSR programs addressing education, healthcare, environmental sustainability, and economic empowerment . These programs are particularly concentrated in districts such as Durg-Bhilai, Korba, Raigarh, and Bastar, where industrial and tribal communities intersect. The state’s legal framework, along with national CSR mandates under the Companies Act, 2013, guides corporate interventions, while CSR communication ensures awareness, participation, and accountability .
Socio-Economic Profile of Jharkhand
1. Historical and Political Context
Jharkhand was carved out of the southern districts of Bihar and formally established as India’s 28th state on November 15, 2000 . The state was created to address regional disparities, particularly for tribal populations in mineral-rich areas, and to enable focused governance and development. The capital city is Ranchi, with other major urban centers including Jamshedpur, Dhanbad, Bokaro, and Hazaribagh . Jharkhand has a multi-party political system, with governance alternating between national and regional parties. Local governance is reinforced through Panchayati Raj Institutions (PRIs) and tribal councils, which play a key role in implementing developmental programs and CSR interventions .
2. Geography and Natural Resources
Jharkhand covers an area of 79,710 square kilometers, located in eastern India, and is endowed with extensive mineral and forest resources . The state is part of the Chota Nagpur Plateau and is rich in minerals, making it one of India’s top mineral-producing states. Key resources include:
•	Coal: Jharkhand accounts for a significant proportion of India’s total coal production, supporting thermal power plants and industrial sectors.
•	Iron Ore: Supporting steel production, particularly in Jamshedpur and Bokaro.
•	Mica, Bauxite, and Copper: Providing raw materials for metallurgical and industrial units.
•	Limestone and Uranium: Supporting cement, energy, and strategic industries .
Forests cover nearly 29% of the state’s area, sustaining biodiversity, minor forest produce, and traditional livelihoods (Economic empowerment) of tribal communities . The terrain is largely plateaued, interspersed with hills, rivers, and mineral-rich zones, which shape settlement patterns and economic activity.
3. Demographic Profile
According to the 2011 Census, Jharkhand has a population of approximately 32.9 million, with an annual growth rate of 1.3% . The population distribution is predominantly rural (76%), with urban centers concentrated in mining and industrial belts. Key demographic characteristics include:
•	Tribal Population: Constituting 26% of the state population, including Santhal, Munda, Ho, and Oraon tribes .
•	Scheduled Castes: Represent around 12% of the population.
•	Gender Composition: Sex ratio stands at 948 females per 1,000 males, below the national average, highlighting persistent gender disparities.
•	Literacy Rate: Overall literacy is 67.6%, with a significant gap between male literacy (78%) and female literacy (57%) .
The large tribal and rural population forms the primary target group for CSR initiatives in education, healthcare, livelihood (Economic empowerment), and women's empowerment.
4. Economic Structure
Jharkhand’s economy is resource- and industry-driven, making it a key contributor to India’s mineral and industrial sectors . The Gross State Domestic Product (GSDP) has grown at an average annual rate of 6–7%, with primary, secondary, and tertiary sectors contributing variably to the state economy .
•	Primary Sector: Agriculture employs a significant proportion of the population. Principal crops include rice, maize, pulses, and oilseeds. Forest products and minor forest produce (such as lac, tendu leaves, and mahua) support tribal livelihood (Economic empowerment) .
•	Secondary Sector: Industrial activity forms the backbone of the state’s economy. Jharkhand hosts steel plants (Tata Steel, Bokaro Steel Plant), coal mining, power generation, and cement factories. Mining and metallurgical industries dominate the industrial landscape, particularly in Dhanbad, Jamshedpur, and Bokaro .
•	Tertiary Sector: Services, including trade, banking, healthcare, and education, are growing steadily. Emerging sectors include information technology and tourism, though their contribution remains limited compared to mining and industry .
Despite industrial development, a large proportion of the population remains dependent on agriculture and informal labor, indicating a need for inclusive economic growth.
5. Human Development Indicators
Jharkhand faces significant human development challenges despite its mineral wealth :
•	Health: Infant mortality rate is 38 per 1,000 live births, maternal mortality rate is 206 per 100,000 live births, and malnutrition remains prevalent among children and women .
•	Education: The state struggles with high dropout rates in rural and tribal areas, particularly among girls. School infrastructure and teacher availability are uneven across districts .
•	Poverty: Nearly 29% of the population lives below the poverty line, with tribal and rural households disproportionately affected .
These indicators make Jharkhand a key focus for CSR programs targeting education, healthcare, environmental sustainability, and economic empowerment.
6. Social and Cultural Context
Jharkhand has a rich tribal heritage, with over 30 recognized tribes, each with distinct languages, customs, and traditions . Social life is strongly influenced by community governance, local norms, and festivals. Tribal traditions, folk arts, and handicrafts provide opportunities for CSR initiatives that promote cultural preservation and livelihood (Economic empowerment) generation . Understanding these socio-cultural contexts is crucial for designing effective and sustainable CSR interventions.
7. Industrial and CSR Landscape
Jharkhand’s resource-rich districts, particularly Dhanbad, Bokaro, Jamshedpur, and Ranchi, host major industrial operations. These industries, including steel, coal, and power plants, implement CSR programs to address socio-economic challenges faced by local communities . CSR activities commonly focus on education, healthcare, skill development, environmental management, and women empowerment. Communication strategies, including community meetings, media outreach, and social campaigns, are critical to ensuring program visibility, participation, and impact .
The Companies Act, 2013 and Ministry of Corporate Affairs guidelines provide the legal framework for CSR implementation, ensuring that companies’ social interventions are structured, accountable, and aligned with community needs .
Rationale for State Selection
The selection of Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand as the focus of this study is driven by their strategic importance in India’s industrial and socio-economic landscape and their relevance to Corporate Social Responsibility (CSR) research. Both states are rich in mineral resources, including coal, iron ore, and limestone, which attract large-scale industrial operations and create significant opportunities and responsibilities for corporate intervention.
The study aims to investigate how CSR initiatives are planned, implemented, and communicated in regions with resource-driven economies but diverse social challenges, including tribal populations, rural settlements, and marginalized communities. Comparing these two states enables the identification of regional similarities and differences in CSR practices, the socio-economic impact of corporate interventions, and the effectiveness of CSR communication strategies.
Furthermore, both states are subject to the Companies Act, 2013, which mandates CSR for eligible companies, ensuring that CSR activities are legally structured and reported. The comparison enables a context-sensitive analysis of CSR implementation and communication, offering insights into state-specific challenges and best practices that inform both policy and corporate decision-making. 
These two states, located in central and eastern India, respectively, were carved out of larger states in 2000 to promote regional development and address historical disparities, particularly in areas inhabited by tribal and marginalized communities. Both states are endowed with rich mineral resources, including coal, iron ore, and limestone, making them hubs of industrial activity and, consequently, sites of significant corporate social responsibility (CSR) engagement. The presence of extensive industrial operations alongside socially vulnerable populations provides a unique context to examine how CSR initiatives are designed, implemented, and communicated, and how they contribute to the betterment of society.
In addition to their resource-based industrial similarity, Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand share several common characteristics that make them suitable for a comparative study. Both have substantial tribal populations with distinct socio-cultural identities, face challenges in human development indicators such as literacy, health, and gender equity, and have a largely rural demographic where CSR interventions can have a significant social impact. The states also operate under similar legal and policy frameworks for CSR, particularly following the Companies Act, 2013, which mandates corporate responsibility initiatives. These shared features enable meaningful comparison, while differences in industrial concentration, urbanization, and governance offer opportunities to analyze variations in CSR practices and their outcomes across different socio-economic and cultural contexts.
By selecting Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand, this study ensures a balance between similarity and diversity, creating a context that highlights both best practices and gaps in CSR planning, implementation, and communication. This approach facilitates a comprehensive understanding of how to tailor CSR initiatives to regional needs, ensuring that the comparative analysis yields actionable insights for corporations, policymakers, and civil society stakeholders.
Common Characteristics
Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand share several structural and socio-economic characteristics that make them suitable for comparative analysis:
1.	Resource Endowment: Both states are mineral-rich, hosting significant reserves of coal, iron ore, limestone, and other industrially valuable minerals, which underpin their industrial and economic growth .
2.	Industrial Activity: The presence of steel, power, cement, and mining industries makes both states major hubs for corporate operations, thereby increasing the relevance of CSR interventions .
3.	Tribal and Rural Population: A substantial portion of the population in both states is tribal, with unique socio-cultural practices and livelihoods (Economic empowerment) linked to agriculture and forest resources .
4.	Developmental Challenges: Despite economic growth, both states face human development gaps, including lower literacy rates, limited healthcare access, high rural poverty, and gender disparities .
5.	Legal and Institutional Framework: Both states operate under the Companies Act, 2013, with CSR mandates, and rely on local governance structures, including Panchayati Raj Institutions, to facilitate community-level development initiatives.
6.	Emerging CSR Focus Areas: Education, healthcare, environmental sustainability, and economic empowerment are common priority sectors for CSR programs in both states, reflecting national development objectives and Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs) .
These shared characteristics make Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand ideal candidates for a comparative study, enabling a systematic investigation of how CSR initiatives are implemented and communicated, and how they contribute to the betterment of society in similar socio-economic contexts.
Rationale for Comparative Analysis
The comparative analysis of Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand is essential for understanding the implementation and impact of corporate social responsibility (CSR) initiatives in resource-rich regions that are socio-economically diverse. Both states were formed in 2000 to address regional disparities and have abundant mineral resources such as coal, iron ore, and limestone, which attract large-scale industrial activity and create opportunities for CSR interventions. Additionally, both states have substantial tribal populations and rural communities that rely heavily on agriculture and forest-based livelihoods (Economic empowerment), making them critical targets for corporate developmental programs.
Despite these shared characteristics, Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand differ significantly in their human development indicators, industrial structure, and infrastructure, which influence CSR design and effectiveness. Chhattisgarh exhibits relatively higher literacy rates, better gender parity, and more developed urban infrastructure, while Jharkhand faces higher levels of rural poverty, malnutrition, and lower female literacy. Industrially, Jharkhand’s economy is dominated by mining, steel, and energy sectors, whereas Chhattisgarh has a more diversified industrial base that includes power generation and cement production. These differences affect both the scope of CSR initiatives and the methods used to communicate them to communities.
Comparing the two states allows the study to examine how CSR programs are planned and implemented, whether through internal company teams or via implementing agencies and NGOs, and to analyze sectoral priorities such as education, healthcare, environmental sustainability, and livelihood (Economic empowerment) enhancement. The comparison also facilitates an evaluation of the impact of CSR initiatives on socio-economic indicators and community satisfaction, as well as the effectiveness of CSR communication through various channels, including community meetings, print and electronic media, social media, radio, television, and conferences.
A comparative approach not only identifies state-specific best practices and gaps but also contributes to theoretical and practical knowledge of CSR. It provides insights into how corporate interventions can be tailored to regional socio-economic contexts and highlights the importance of effective communication in enhancing societal outcomes. This framework ensures that CSR initiatives go beyond philanthropy to contribute meaningfully to the betterment of society in mineral-rich, industrially active, but socially vulnerable regions of India.

The statistical profile therefore establishes the developmental context within which CSR communication operates. While economic indicators differ between the two states, the study argues that perceived societal betterment is shaped less by expenditure levels alone and more by the effectiveness of communication strategies used to engage beneficiaries.
