Rationale for the Study
Corporate Social Responsibility (CSR) in India has shifted from a voluntary philanthropic practice to a statutorily mandated corporate responsibility following the enactment of the Companies Act, 2013. While extensive literature exists on CSR expenditure patterns, sectoral focus, and compliance reporting, relatively limited academic attention has been given to how CSR initiatives are communicated to intended beneficiaries, especially in resource-rich and socially sensitive regions. This gap is particularly evident in tribal-dominated states such as Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand, where communication plays a decisive role in shaping awareness, participation, and long-term social outcomes.
Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand are among India’s most mineral-rich states and host significant public and private industrial operations. Despite sustained industrial growth, both states continue to face challenges related to education, healthcare access, environmental stress, and livelihood (Economic empowerment). CSR initiatives are expected to respond to these concerns; however, the effectiveness of such interventions depends not only on financial allocation or programme design but also on how CSR activities are communicated, understood, and experienced by local communities.
CSR communication functions as the primary link between corporations and society. Community meetings, interpersonal engagement by implementing agencies, print media, banners and posters, conferences, electronic media, social media, and radio serve as channels through which information about CSR initiatives is disseminated. In regions marked by low literacy levels, cultural diversity, and geographical dispersion, inappropriate or inadequate communication can significantly limit programme reach and impact, reducing CSR activities to formal compliance rather than meaningful social engagement.
Existing CSR research in India predominantly concentrates on urban or corporate perspectives, with limited emphasis on community-level communication processes in rural and tribal contexts. Moreover, comparative studies that examine CSR communication across states with similar industrial structures but differing social environments remain scarce. A comparative analysis of Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand therefore provides a suitable framework to examine variations in CSR implementation models, communication strategies, and beneficiary responses under comparable legal and economic conditions.
The study is further justified on theoretical grounds, as it integrates stakeholder-oriented perspectives and communication-based approaches within the CSR discourse. By focusing on communication as a process rather than an outcome, the research positions CSR as a relational practice that involves dialogue, trust-building, and social inclusion. This approach enables a deeper understanding of how CSR communication influences community participation, perception, and satisfaction.
From a policy and practice perspective, the findings of this study are expected to offer valuable insights for corporate decision-makers, implementing agencies, and policymakers. By identifying effective CSR communication practices suited to resource-intensive and socially diverse regions, the study aims to contribute to improved transparency, inclusiveness, and accountability in CSR implementation, thereby strengthening its role in the betterment of society.
The Comparative Study Approach
This research adopts a comparative methodology, which involves systematically analyzing similarities and differences across two or more cases to identify patterns, test hypotheses, and generate theoretical insights. In the context of CSR research, the comparative approach is particularly valuable as it enables a nuanced understanding of how corporate initiatives operate across distinct regional and socio-economic settings.
The comparative approach provides several advantages for this study:
•	Contextual Analysis: It allows examination of how local and regional factors influence the design, implementation, and impact of CSR initiatives, including socio-cultural characteristics, governance structures, and infrastructural capacity.
•	Identification of Best Practices: By comparing cases, the study can highlight successful strategies and approaches that may be transferable to other contexts or regions.
•	Controlled Evaluation: While national-level CSR policies, such as the Companies Act, 2013, remain constant, state-level differences in regulation, monitoring, and stakeholder engagement can be systematically analysed.
•	Insights on Regional Variations: The approach facilitates understanding of how differences in governance, socio-economic conditions, and community dynamics shape the effectiveness and communication of CSR interventions.
By employing a comparative framework, this research moves beyond single-case descriptions to generate findings that are both theoretically rigorous and practically relevant. It enables a systematic evaluation of CSR practices across Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand, highlighting patterns, challenges, and opportunities applicable to similar socio-economic and industrial contexts.
Significance of the Study
The significance of the present study lies in its focused examination of Corporate Social Responsibility (CSR) communication as a critical determinant of societal outcomes, rather than viewing CSR merely as a financial or compliance-driven activity. By concentrating on how CSR initiatives are communicated to community-level beneficiaries, the study addresses an underexplored dimension of CSR research in India, particularly within resource-rich and socially diverse regions.
From an academic perspective, the study contributes to CSR and communication research by extending existing theoretical discussions beyond corporate reporting and reputation management. It positions CSR communication as a process involving awareness creation, participation, trust, and feedback at the community level. By integrating communication-oriented approaches with CSR analysis, the research provides empirical evidence from rural and tribal contexts that are often absent from mainstream CSR literature.
The study holds substantial regional significance for Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand, two states characterised by intensive industrial activity alongside persistent socio-economic challenges. By examining CSR communication practices across these states, the research highlights how contextual factors such as literacy levels, cultural diversity, and local governance structures influence the reach and effectiveness of CSR initiatives. This region-specific focus allows for the identification of communication gaps and locally relevant practices that can enhance community engagement and programme outcomes.
From a practical standpoint, the findings of this study are valuable for corporate CSR teams and implementing agencies. Insights into effective communication channels—such as community meetings, interpersonal outreach, print and electronic media, and digital platforms—can support better planning and execution of CSR interventions. The study also offers guidance on aligning communication strategies with community needs, thereby improving beneficiary participation and satisfaction.
The research also carries policy relevance, as it provides evidence that can inform government agencies and regulatory bodies involved in CSR oversight. By demonstrating the importance of communication in achieving meaningful social impact, the study supports the need for clearer guidelines and evaluation mechanisms that go beyond expenditure reporting to include communication effectiveness and community response.
Finally, the study is significant in terms of societal relevance, as it foregrounds the voices and experiences of CSR beneficiaries. By examining how communities perceive, engage with, and respond to CSR initiatives, the research reinforces the role of CSR as a participatory and inclusive development mechanism. In doing so, it contributes to a deeper understanding of how CSR communication can support the betterment of society in regions marked by economic opportunity alongside social vulnerability.
Defining of Key Terms
For the purpose of this study, the following terms are operationally defined:
Corporate Social Responsibility (CSR)
Refers to a company’s commitment, as mandated under Section 135 of the Companies Act, 2013, to contribute to sustainable development by undertaking social, environmental, and economic welfare initiatives. In this study, CSR is examined not only as a set of activities but as a communication-driven process that links corporate intent with community impact.
CSR Communication
The systematic process through which companies convey information about CSR initiatives—including objectives, implementation, and outcomes—to stakeholders, especially beneficiary communities. It includes both interpersonal channels (e.g., community meetings, door-to-door outreach) and mediated channels (e.g., print, digital, radio).
Betterment of Society
Measurable improvements in the socio-economic well-being and quality of life of communities, assessed through indicators in education, healthcare, environmental sustainability, and livelihood (Economic empowerment). In this study, societal betterment is evaluated through both objective metrics and subjective beneficiary perceptions.
Society (Operational Definition)
Refers specifically to direct beneficiaries of CSR initiatives in Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand. This includes tribal, urban and rural populations, women, Scheduled Tribes (STs), Scheduled Castes (SCs), and other marginalized groups targeted by CSR programs.
Beneficiary
An individual or household that directly receives or participates in a CSR intervention. For this research, a beneficiary is defined as someone who has been part of a CSR initiative in education, health, the environment, or livelihood (economic empowerment) during the study period (FY 2018–19 to 2022–23).
CSR Initiative
A structured CSR activity or project implemented by a company—either directly or through an implementing agency—in alignment with Schedule VII of the Companies Act, 2013. Initiatives are categorized into four sectors: Health, Education, Environment, and livelihood (Economic empowerment).
Implementing Agency
A registered non-governmental organization (NGO), trust, or community-based organization that partners with a company to execute CSR projects on the ground. Excludes government agencies, as per the study’s delimitation.
Tribal and Rural Populations
Communities residing in tribal-dominated and rural areas of Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand, often characterized by distinct socio-cultural identities, reliance on forest/agrarian livelihood (Economic empowerment), and limited access to public services. These groups constitute the primary focus of CSR interventions in the selected states.
Districts and Sample Distribution
This study focuses on ten core districts—five each in Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand—alongside additional districts from both states to ensure comprehensive geographic and socio-economic representation. In Chhattisgarh, the core districts include Raipur, Dhamtari, Balodabazar, Kanker, and Bastar, with 30 respondents each, supplemented by 50 respondents from other active CSR districts across the state, for a total of 200 beneficiary respondents from Chhattisgarh. Similarly, in Jharkhand, the core districts comprise East Singhbhum (Jamshedpur/Tatanagar), West Singhbhum (Chaibasa), Ranchi, Ramgarh, and Hazaribagh, also with 30 respondents per district, along with 50 respondents from other CSR-active districts, bringing the total beneficiary sample from Jharkhand to 200. This distribution ensures that the study captures both concentrated CSR hubs and wider regional implementation patterns, enabling a balanced comparative analysis of CSR communication and impact across diverse administrative and socio-cultural contexts.
Comparative Analysis
A research approach that systematically examines similarities and differences between two or more cases—here, the states of Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand—to identify patterns, contextual factors, and best practices in CSR communication and impact.
Five-Pillar Framework
The analytical model developed for this study, comprising:
1.	Company Identification
2.	Implementation Mode (direct/through agency)
3.	Initiative Type (health, education, environment, livelihood-Economic empowerment)
4.	Impact Measurement
5.	Communication Process
Stakeholder
Any individual or group affected by or influencing CSR activities, including beneficiaries, implementing agencies, corporate CSR teams, and local government bodies. In this study, the primary stakeholders are beneficiary communities.

 CHAPTER 02: REVIEW OF LITERATURE
This chapter synthesises existing scholarly work, policy documents, and empirical studies relevant to the core themes of this research: Corporate Social Responsibility (CSR), CSR communication, societal betterment, and the specific contexts of Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand. The purpose is to establish the theoretical foundation, identify the research gaps, and justify the necessity of this comparative study. The review is structured to progress from global and national conceptualisations of CSR to specific regional analyses, culminating in the clear articulation of the knowledge gap that this thesis aims to fill. A systematic approach has been employed to survey literature from academic databases, including JSTOR, Sage Journals, SpringerLink, and Google Scholar, as well as government reports and policy documents.
The Evolution of Corporate Social Responsibility (CSR)
The concept of Corporate Social Responsibility has evolved significantly from its early roots in philanthropic charity to a complex, multi-stakeholder framework integral to modern business strategy. The seminal work of Bowen (1953), defined the social responsibilities of businessmen as "the obligations of businessmen to pursue those policies, to make those decisions, or to follow those lines of action which are desirable in terms of the objectives and values of our society."  This established the notion that corporations have a duty beyond profit maximisation. Carroll's (1991) subsequent CSR pyramid model categorised these responsibilities into four layers: economic (be profitable), legal (obey the law), ethical (be ethical), and philanthropic (be a good corporate citizen). This model provided a structured understanding that CSR is not merely charity but a layered obligation. In the 21st century, the concept has further evolved to include strategic CSR and Creating Shared Value (CSV), where Porter and Kramer (2011) argue that social and economic progress must be connected using CSR to identify and expand the connections.  The adoption of the Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs) by the United Nations in 2015 has provided a global framework for aligning CSR activities, pushing corporations towards more measurable and impactful social investments.
 
 
Figure 5 Carroll's Pyramid of Corporate Social Responsibility (1991)
 
CSR in the Indian Legislative Context
The Companies Act, 2013, fundamentally transformed the landscape of CSR in India. Section 135 of the Act mandates that companies with a specified net worth, turnover, or net profit must spend at least 2% of their average net profits of the preceding three years on CSR activities. This made India the first country to legally mandate CSR spending, shifting it from a voluntary gesture to a compliance-oriented activity. Mitra and Akhtar (2020) describe this as a "noble experiment" but highlight challenges in implementation, including a focus on spending rather than impact, and a lack of local community participation in planning.  The Ministry of Corporate Affairs (MCA) has subsequently released several circulars and amendments to the CSR Rules, most notably in 2021, to strengthen the framework by emphasising impact assessment, permitting CSR for COVID-19 relief, and requiring the registration of implementation agencies. Studies by Sahasranamam and Arya (2020) indicate that while the mandate has significantly increased CSR fund flow, its effectiveness is uneven across states and sectors, with a tendency for companies to invest in urban-centric, high-visibility projects rather than in critical but complex rural and tribal development. 
The Critical Role of CSR Communication
CSR communication is the strategic process of designing and disseminating information about a company's social and environmental commitments and actions. Morsing and Schultz (2006) identify three primary strategies: the stakeholder information strategy (one-way communication), the stakeholder response strategy (two-way asymmetric communication), and the stakeholder involvement strategy (two-way symmetric communication).  They argue that the involvement strategy, which fosters dialogue and engagement, is the most effective for building trust and legitimacy. Du, Bhattacharya, and Sen (2010) emphasise that effective CSR communication is crucial for maximising both business and social returns on CSR investments.  It helps in managing stakeholder attributions about the company's motives, which directly influences their scepticism or support. However, a significant challenge in the Indian context, as noted by Chaudhuri (2018), is the communication gap. Many companies, especially PSUs, focus on compliance reporting to the MCA rather than communicating in vernacular languages through culturally appropriate channels to the beneficiaries on the ground. This creates a disconnect where substantial investments remain invisible to the intended communities, undermining their perceived legitimacy and impact. 
Measuring Societal Betterment: From Output to Outcome
The "betterment of society" is an oft-stated but complex goal to measure. Traditional CSR reporting often focused on outputs (e.g., number of schools built, trees planted, people trained). However, contemporary literature emphasises the need to measure outcomes and impact—the tangible change in beneficiaries' lives. Sen's (1999) "capabilities approach" to development argues that the goal should be to expand people's capabilities and freedoms to lead the lives they value.  In the CSR context, this translates to measuring improvements in health, education, economic empowerment, and environmental well-being. The work of the Impact Management Project (IMP) and frameworks like the Social Return on Investment (SROI) provide methodologies for quantifying social impact. In India, the National CSR Portal collects data on spending and projects; however, there is a severe lack of standardised impact assessments. Studies by Dutta and Banerjee (2021) advocate for a stronger connection between CSR reporting and the SDGs, aiming to establish a unified framework for measuring contributions to national and global development priorities. This approach seeks to move beyond corporate-centric metrics to beneficiary-centric assessments of improvement. 
The Tribal Context of CSR in India
The implementation of CSR in tribal-dominated regions presents unique challenges and opportunities. India's Scheduled Tribes (STs), who constitute 8.6% of the population, are disproportionately represented among the poor and marginalised, often residing in resource-rich but economically underdeveloped regions. Xaxa (2016) outlines the historical context of exploitation and displacement faced by tribal communities due to development projects. The Fifth Schedule of the Indian Constitution provides special protections for tribal areas, emphasising community ownership and self-governance through the Gram Sabha.  However, as Kumar and Xaxa (2018) argue, CSR initiatives often bypass these democratic institutions. Their study in eastern India found that CSR projects in tribal areas are often designed with a top-down approach, lacking adequate consultation with the local community.  This leads to a mismatch between corporate offerings and community needs. For instance, providing computer education in a school that lacks qualified teachers and reliable electricity. Communication is often conducted in Hindi or English, often ignoring tribal languages and oral traditions, resulting in low awareness and ownership.
CSR in Chhattisgarh: A Review
Chhattisgarh's economy is heavily dependent on mining and mineral-based industries, leading to a significant concentration of CSR-spending companies. However, as Sinha (2018) notes in a case study, there is a stark contrast between the high CSR potential and the on-ground realities in districts like Bastar and Dantewada.  A significant portion of CSR funds is channelled into building physical infrastructure, such as schools and hospitals. However, a study by the IJSDR (2023) highlights that many such structures remain underutilised due to a lack of commensurate investment in teachers, doctors, and maintenance—a classic outcome of poor communication and community involvement during the planning stage. The communication strategy of central PSUs, such as SECL and NMDC, is often reported through their annual reports, with little evidence of localised, vernacular communication aimed at the tribal beneficiaries. This creates a scenario where companies "report" compliance but fail to "communicate" with the community, leading to a trust deficit and limiting the potential for societal betterment. 
CSR in Jharkhand: A Review
Jharkhand shares a similar trajectory with Chhattisgarh but has its own distinct challenges. Home to some of India's oldest industrial houses (e.g., Tata Steel in Jamshedpur), CSR has a more extended history here. The Tata Steel Foundation's approach, which includes programs like the "1000 Schools Project," is often cited as a model for long-term, participatory development. However, a report by Swaniti (2022) highlights the extreme regional disparity within Jharkhand.  While industrial hubs like Jamshedpur and Bokaro see concentrated CSR activity, the remote tribal districts of Palamu, Garhwa, and Latehar remain severely underserved. Lower literacy rates and greater linguistic diversity compound the communication challenge. Sharma (2019) studies CSR communication explicitly in Jharkhand and concludes that corporations' reliance on digital and print media falls short in these regions.  He advocates for the use of folk media, community radio, and direct interpersonal communication through trusted NGOs to bridge this gap.
The Comparative Research Gap
While there is a growing body of literature on CSR in India, and some studies on individual states, a significant gap exists in comparative, region-specific research that focuses explicitly on CSR communication and its link to societal betterment. Most studies are either macro-level analyses of CSR spending patterns or micro-level case studies of single companies or projects. Singh and Agrawal (2020) acknowledge the dominance of urban-centric research and the absence of contextualized studies on rural and tribal communication modalities.  There is no known study that systematically compares the communication strategies, beneficiary awareness, and perceived impact of CSR between Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand. This gap is critical because these two states offer a natural experiment of sorts: they share similar historical, demographic, and economic contexts but have diverged in their governance and development outcomes over the past two decades. Understanding how CSR communication operates in these two distinct yet similar environments is crucial for developing more effective, context-sensitive CSR policies and practices for resource-rich tribal regions across India.
CSR Communication: Theoretical Perspectives
CSR communication has emerged as a distinct area of study within the broader field of corporate communication. Scholars have examined how companies communicate their CSR activities, the motivations behind such communication, and the effects on various stakeholder groups.
Morsing and Schultz (2006) proposed a model of CSR communication that distinguishes between three strategies: stakeholder information strategy, stakeholder response strategy, and stakeholder involvement strategy.  The stakeholder information strategy focuses on one-way communication from the company to stakeholders, emphasizing transparency and disclosure. The stakeholder response strategy involves two-way asymmetric communication, where companies gather feedback from stakeholders to improve their CSR initiatives. The stakeholder involvement strategy represents the highest level of engagement, involving two-way symmetric communication that treats stakeholders as partners in CSR development and implementation.
Dawkins (2004) examined the role of corporate communications in managing stakeholder relationships, arguing that effective CSR communication can enhance corporate reputation, build trust, and create shared value. The study emphasized the importance of authenticity and consistency in CSR messaging, noting that stakeholders can detect and penalize greenwashing or insincere communication. 
In the Indian context, Kumar and Prasad (2018) studied CSR communication practices among top Indian companies, finding that while disclosure levels have improved, there is limited emphasis on communicating with beneficiaries at the grassroots level. The study highlighted the need for companies to adopt more participatory communication approaches that engage beneficiaries as active stakeholders rather than passive recipients.

  
Figure 6 CSR Communication — Stakeholder Flow
Interpretation. CSR communication links corporations, implementing agencies, communities, and regulators through two-way engagement that builds awareness, trust, accountability, and societal betterment.
 Key Interactions
1. Corporates ↔ NGOs:
•	Corporates → NGOs: Provide funding, strategic direction, and require compliance reporting.
•	NGOs → Corporates: Execute projects on the ground, mobilize communities, and provide crucial ground-level feedback and impact data. This is often the most critical link for effective CSR.
2. NGOs ↔ Beneficiaries:
•	NGOs → Beneficiaries: Deliver services (education, healthcare), facilitate participation, and act as the primary communicator and trusted intermediary.
•	Beneficiaries → NGOs: Provide authentic feedback, voice grievances, and participate in needs assessment. This is the key channel for community voice.
3. Government ↔ All Entities:
•	The government acts as a regulator, facilitator, and enabler for all other stakeholders.
•	→ Corporates: Sets the legal framework (Companies Act) and mandates compliance.
•	→ NGOs: Provides grants, permissions, and district-level support for implementation.
•	→ Beneficiaries: Provides public schemes and infrastructure that CSR complements.
4. Corporates ↔ Beneficiaries:
•	Corporates → Beneficiaries: Engage in direct implementation and corporate-branded communication (e.g., posters, camps). This builds brand legitimacy but can be less effective without intermediaries.
•	Beneficiaries → Corporates: Provide direct feedback (e.g., through grievance boxes) and their perception of the corporate's legitimacy directly impacts the company's social license to operate.
5. Beneficiaries ↔ Government:
•	Beneficiaries → Government: Influence policy through votes, advocacy, and social audits. Their satisfaction with CSR can impact political legitimacy.
•	Government → Beneficiaries: Provides the overarching development framework and welfare schemes within which CSR operates.
6. Corporates ↔ Government & NGOs ↔ Government:
•	These flows represent lobbying, policy advocacy, and reporting. Companies and NGOs both seek to influence policy and report on their activities to maintain legitimacy and access.
Central Principle: The model shows that effective CSR and societal betterment (the ultimate outcome) are not achieved by any single entity but through continuous, multi-directional engagement and communication between all stakeholders. Trust is the currency that enables every interaction in this model.
 Research Direction
The role of Corporate Social Responsibility (CSR) has undergone significant transformation in recent decades, transitioning from philanthropic intent to a strategic mechanism of social engagement and corporate accountability. Within this evolving discourse, CSR communication has emerged as a central pillar that determines not only the visibility and public awareness of CSR activities but also the extent of engagement and trust that communities place in corporate entities. In India, the integration of CSR into mainstream corporate governance through legislative instruments—most notably, the Companies Act, 2013—has mandated businesses to invest in social development, thereby institutionalizing CSR as both a legal and ethical responsibility.
The purpose of this literature review is to critically examine existing academic, policy, and industry literature related to CSR and its communication, with a particular focus on the Indian context and the specific regional dynamics of Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand. These two states, both carved out in the year 2000, share commonalities such as tribal populations, mineral wealth, and a history of developmental imbalance. However, they also exhibit differences in their socio-economic structure, industrial base, governance mechanisms, and communication ecosystems. These contextual variables are critical in determining the reach, acceptance, and effectiveness of CSR communication strategies adopted by corporations operating in these regions.
The significance of CSR communication becomes more pronounced in areas where traditional state welfare mechanisms struggle to penetrate, particularly in the rural and tribal belts of Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand. Several studies have noted that the success of CSR initiatives is often less about the scale of investment and more about the clarity, accessibility, and inclusivity of the communication that accompanies such initiatives. For instance, communities that understand the purpose, scope, and implementation mechanisms of CSR programs are more likely to participate and benefit from them in a sustainable manner .
Moreover, the evolution of CSR communication in India has not been uniform. While urban and industrialized areas may benefit from digital platforms and organized public relations mechanisms, rural and underdeveloped regions—like many districts in Jharkhand and Chhattisgarh—continue to depend on face-to-face interaction, vernacular media, and the mediation of non-governmental organizations (NGOs) or local leaders . In such regions, communication gaps may lead to misinterpretation, disinterest, or even resistance, reducing the potential societal impact of otherwise well-structured CSR programs.
The literature review thus aims to synthesize scholarly perspectives on the historical evolution, theoretical foundations, communication strategies, and sector-specific impacts of CSR, while identifying existing research gaps, particularly with respect to regional comparisons within India. By doing so, this section lays the groundwork for the present study’s contribution to academic knowledge and policy-oriented dialogue concerning how CSR communication influences societal betterment.
This review is structured to first explore the global and Indian evolution of CSR, followed by an analysis of key theories underpinning CSR communication. It then delves into sectoral reviews related to education, healthcare, and environmental sustainability, supported by empirical case studies. Special emphasis is placed on studies relevant to Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand, to contextualize the comparative approach undertaken in the thesis. The chapter concludes by highlighting gaps in existing literature and explaining how the current study seeks to address them.
Sectoral Review – CSR in Education, Healthcare, and Environment
The mandate of Corporate Social Responsibility (CSR) in India has increasingly emphasized not only the amount of money spent but also the strategic focus of initiatives, especially in critical sectors such as education, healthcare, and environmental sustainability. In the regional context of Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand, where infrastructural gaps, social inequality, and environmental degradation are significant, CSR programs in these sectors have become vital instruments for societal betterment. This section examines scholarly work and corporate practices in each of these three sectors, focusing on communication mechanisms and outreach strategies adopted by corporations to address community needs.
Education
Education has consistently been one of the top CSR spending areas in India since the enforcement of the Companies Act, 2013. As per data from the Ministry of Corporate Affairs (MCA), education-related CSR projects received nearly 36% of total CSR expenditure in 2020–21, highlighting its strategic and social importance .
In Chhattisgarh, education-focused CSR programs are especially relevant due to the high tribal population and limited school infrastructure in rural districts like Dantewada, Sukma, and Kanker. Companies such as NMDC Limited have invested in schools like the DAV NMDC Public School, Dantewada, and residential hostels for tribal students, often supplementing state efforts to reduce dropout rates and improve quality of learning . Communication strategies here involve school enrolment drives, community meetings with parents, and distribution of materials in local dialects to promote awareness.
In Jharkhand, corporations like Tata Steel Foundation run programs such as the 1000 Schools Project in partnership with the government and civil society organizations. These programs focus on teacher training, digital literacy, infrastructure support, and school governance improvement . Communication in these projects is structured through regular stakeholder workshops, school-level councils, and periodic impact newsletters in regional languages.
Studies have emphasized the need for culturally relevant and localized communication strategies in tribal and rural education initiatives to enhance participation and community trust .
Healthcare
The healthcare infrastructure in both Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand, especially in rural and tribal regions, continues to face challenges like under-staffing, poor connectivity, and lack of diagnostic facilities. CSR has played a supplementary role in bridging these service gaps. National data reveals that 22% of CSR spending in FY 2020–21 was allocated to healthcare initiatives, including sanitation and nutrition .
In Chhattisgarh, the BALCO Medical Centre in Naya Raipur, funded by Vedanta, has emerged as a premier oncology institution offering subsidized cancer treatment to the underserved population. Their communication campaigns have used patient story-telling, community screenings, and field health educators to promote health-seeking behavior .
Another successful example is Jindal Steel and Power Limited (JSPL) in Raigarh, which runs mobile health units, organizes regular village health camps, and leverages health awareness rallies to reach out to marginalized populations. These initiatives are communicated through local wall paintings, posters, and audio vans in Chhattisgarhi and tribal dialects .
In Jharkhand, Tata Steel’s health CSR programs include mobile medical units in West Singhbhum and immunization drives coordinated with ASHA workers. Awareness is communicated through printed materials, mobile messages, and community radio, enhancing the reach among non-literate beneficiaries .
Environmental Sustainability
Given the extensive mining and industrial activities in both Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand, environmental degradation remains a critical concern. CSR interventions in this sector often focus on afforestation, water conservation, waste management, and air quality monitoring.
In Jharkhand, companies such as Central Coalfields Limited (CCL) and Hindalco have undertaken tree plantation campaigns, implemented water harvesting structures, and promoted solar energy. These programs are often communicated through participatory planning meetings, visual campaigns in schools, and environmental days celebrated with local communities. .
In Chhattisgarh, SECL has collaborated with local forest departments to implement mine-site reclamation, groundwater recharge, and biodiversity protection initiatives in the Hasdeo Arand and Korba regions. Information about these CSR activities is shared with stakeholders through printed newsletters, village information boards, and collaborative meetings with forest committees .
Environmental communication in tribal areas is often complicated by low literacy and the abstractness of ecological issues. Hence, many corporations have adopted visual media, community rituals, and school eco-clubs to build awareness and participation.
CSR communication in the sectors of education, healthcare, and environmental sustainability is not merely about dissemination but about dialogue, engagement, and cultural contextualization. Literature strongly advocates for region-specific, inclusive, and participatory communication models, particularly in socially and linguistically diverse regions such as Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand. It is evident from reviewed studies and corporate practices that effective communication has a direct impact on the visibility, effectiveness, and sustainability of CSR initiatives.
Economic Empowerment and Livelihood Development
Economic empowerment and livelihood development have emerged as significant areas of Corporate Social Responsibility (CSR) engagement in India, particularly in regions where industrial activity intersects with poverty, unemployment, and limited access to sustainable income opportunities. In states such as Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand, where large segments of the population rely on agriculture, forest resources, and informal labor markets, CSR initiatives focused on livelihood enhancement, vocational training, and entrepreneurship development contribute to strengthening local economic capacity. National CSR assessments indicate that a considerable share of CSR investments is directed toward programs related to skill development, employment generation, and poverty alleviation under the activities listed in Schedule VII of the Companies Act, 2013. 
In Chhattisgarh, livelihood-oriented CSR programs often focus on vocational skill development for rural youth, promotion of self-employment, and capacity building among women’s groups in villages located near industrial regions. Training programs in trades such as tailoring, electrical repair, computer literacy, and small-scale entrepreneurship are commonly introduced to enhance employability and income-generating opportunities. Communication strategies in these initiatives frequently include village-level awareness meetings, participatory consultations with local governance institutions, and outreach through community facilitators who mobilize participants from marginalized and tribal communities. Such communication mechanisms help ensure that information regarding training opportunities and financial support reaches potential beneficiaries effectively. 
In Jharkhand, CSR interventions related to livelihood development often emphasize agricultural productivity improvement, micro-enterprise promotion, and the strengthening of self-help groups. Programs may include training in modern agricultural techniques, support for small-scale processing units, and assistance in developing local handicraft or forest-based products. Communication associated with these initiatives generally relies on field demonstrations, participatory workshops, and engagement with community-based organizations. These approaches help bridge information gaps and encourage community participation, particularly in rural areas where formal communication channels remain limited. 
Academic studies have indicated that livelihood-focused CSR programs achieve better outcomes when communication processes encourage participation and continuous interaction with local communities. In tribal regions of central and eastern India, the use of local institutions, women’s self-help groups, and cooperative networks has been found to strengthen awareness and foster collective participation in development initiatives. Such communication practices not only disseminate information about available opportunities but also enable feedback from beneficiaries, thereby improving the responsiveness and sustainability of CSR interventions. 
The inclusion of livelihood development within CSR strategies contributes to broader social outcomes such as reduced economic vulnerability, improved household income stability, and increased participation of women and youth in local economic activities. Effective communication plays a central role in ensuring that these initiatives are accessible and relevant to the communities they aim to serve. As a result, livelihood and economic empowerment initiatives represent an important dimension of CSR practice in Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand, complementing interventions in education, healthcare, and environmental sustainability while contributing to long-term societal development.
Gaps in Existing Literature and Justification for the Study
Despite the growing body of academic work on Corporate Social Responsibility (CSR) and its socio-economic impacts in India, a significant gap remains in region-specific, comparative studies that analyse the communication strategies and societal outcomes of CSR in less-researched states, such as Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand. This section identifies the limitations in existing literature and presents a rationale for the present research, highlighting its necessity and originality in the Indian academic and policy landscape.
Lack of Region-Specific Comparative Studies
Most available studies on CSR in India focus on corporate hubs such as Maharashtra, Delhi-NCR, Karnataka, and Gujarat, where CSR activities are often implemented with better infrastructural support and media access. As a result, the specific socio-cultural and economic dynamics of tribal-majority states like Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand have been largely overlooked. Even when CSR in these states is discussed, the emphasis is usually on expenditure patterns or broad outcomes rather than the strategies of communication, stakeholder perceptions, and beneficiary engagement mechanisms.
A study by Singh & Agrawal (2020) on CSR in India acknowledged the dominance of urban-centric research and the absence of contextualised rural and tribal studies, especially concerning communication modalities in linguistically diverse and low-literacy regions. .
Inadequate Focus on CSR Communication Processes
The majority of Indian CSR literature tends to concentrate on the quantitative aspect—how much was spent and where—rather than how information is conveyed, how communities perceive and respond, and what channels are most effective in building trust and participation. This gap is particularly critical in tribal belts, where oral culture, local dialects, and community traditions significantly influence access to and interpretation of information.
For instance, a national survey on CSR implementation by the Centre for Social Markets (CSM) noted that only 18% of companies studied used local-language communication strategies, and even fewer engaged in two-way communication with stakeholders in rural areas. .
Limited Integration of CSR Communication Theories in the Indian Context
While theoretical frameworks such as Stakeholder Theory, Legitimacy Theory, and Two-Way Symmetrical Communication Model are widely recognised globally, Indian CSR literature shows limited application or testing of these frameworks in the field, particularly in tribal and conflict-affected zones. There is a scarcity of studies that critically analyse how these models function in the socio-political realities of eastern India, where mining-related conflict, development-induced displacement, and governance gaps complicate CSR operations.
A paper by Sharma (2019) pointed out that “there is insufficient scholarship linking global CSR communication theory with the practical realities of communication in semi-literate, low-infrastructure zones in India” .
Neglect of Beneficiary Perception Studies
Most CSR assessments rely on company-reported outcomes, with slight inclusion of community voice or beneficiary feedback. The absence of qualitative data that reflects perceptions, satisfaction, and grievances limits the understanding of actual societal betterment and undermines the principle of stakeholder-centric CSR.
In states like Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand, where social inequality and institutional gaps persist, capturing the perceptions of tribal women, migrant workers, youth, and marginalised groups is essential to assessing the actual impact of CSR initiatives.
Research by Nair and Vyas (2021) on participatory evaluation methods in CSR emphasised the urgent need for field-based perception studies, particularly in sectors like education, healthcare, and the environment, where community interaction is continuous and direct. .
Justification for the Present Study
This research aims to fill these identified gaps by undertaking research in two mineral-rich but underrepresented Indian states. The study focuses specifically on how CSR communication strategies differ in design, outreach, and reception, and how these differences affect beneficiary perception and program impact in the domains of education, healthcare, and environmental sustainability.
Key reasons this study is justified:
•	It offers a region-specific comparison rooted in fieldwork and stakeholder interviews.
•	It incorporates theoretical communication frameworks in assessing CSR practices.
•	It uses a mixed-method approach to include both quantitative CSR data and qualitative perception insights.
•	It addresses tribal and rural populations, whose voices are often excluded from mainstream CSR assessments.
•	It supports evidence-based policymaking by providing grounded, actionable insights for corporate and government actors involved in CSR.
Through this work, the thesis intends to advance the academic discourse on CSR in India while providing practical recommendations for companies operating in socio-economically sensitive regions.
CSR and Tribal Communities – Contextual Analysis
The inclusion of tribal communities within the scope of Corporate Social Responsibility (CSR) is essential in the states of Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand, where tribal populations form a significant demographic. According to the Census of India (2011), tribals constitute approximately 30.6% of the population in Jharkhand and 30.6% in Chhattisgarh, much higher than the national average of 8.6% . These communities often reside in remote, underdeveloped areas where access to education, healthcare, livelihood opportunities, and environmental justice is critically lacking.
This section examines how CSR initiatives have historically engaged with tribal populations in these two states, the challenges of CSR communication in tribal contexts, and the gaps in the literature regarding tribal-specific CSR engagement strategies.
Historical Background and Socioeconomic Context
The regions now constituting Jharkhand and Chhattisgarh have a long history of tribal resistance to exploitation, land alienation, and displacement. Landmark tribal uprisings such as the Santhal Rebellion (1855) and Birsa Munda Movement (1895–1900) in Jharkhand, and the Bhopalpatnam and Bastar Revolts in Chhattisgarh, reflect a deep-rooted struggle against resource extraction and marginalisation. .
Despite the constitutional recognition under Schedule V of the Indian Constitution and the implementation of the PESA Act, 1996, and the Forest Rights Act, 2006, development indicators in tribal districts remain low. These structural disadvantages are compounded by mining and industrial activities that often lead to displacement without adequate rehabilitation. It is within this context that CSR interventions must operate and be critically examined.
CSR Engagement with Tribal Areas in Jharkhand and Chhattisgarh
CSR projects by companies in mining, banking and steel sectors such as Coal India, NMDC, Vedanta, HDFC Bank and Tata Steel often target tribal-majority districts like Bastar, Dhamtari, Balodabazar, Tata Nagar, and Ranchi. However, the literature reveals that much of this engagement has been compliance-driven rather than participatory or transformative.
For instance, Tata Steel Foundation’s tribal development program includes livelihood enhancement through Wadi models and skill training programs, but these are often implemented with limited grassroots consultation . Similarly, in Chhattisgarh, NMDC’s CSR activities in Bacheli and Kirandul involve health camps, educational scholarships, and construction of community halls—but questions remain on the sustainability and awareness of such projects among beneficiaries .
Studies by Kumar & Xaxa (2018) show that tribal communities often lack awareness about CSR schemes, largely due to ineffective communication strategies that fail to use local languages or cultural idioms .
Communication Challenges in Tribal CSR Implementation
Effective CSR in tribal regions requires a shift from top-down, one-way dissemination to participatory communication. Tribal communities are not passive recipients; they possess strong oral traditions, decision-making through gram sabhas, and a worldview centered on ecological balance and community welfare.
However, literature shows that:
•	Most CSR communication remains in Hindi or English, rarely in tribal languages such as Chhattisgarhi, Gondi, Halbi, Ho, Mundari, or Kurukh .
•	There is an over-reliance on printed media, which has limited penetration in oral societies.
•	Community media, including traditional folk performances, puppet shows, or tribal radio, are underutilized in CSR awareness drives.
These gaps lead to low trust, limited engagement, and perceived exclusion, especially among women, elders, and those living in forest interiors.
Recommendations from Literature
Academic recommendations include:
•	Localization of CSR communication tools through participatory theatre, tribal dialects, and oral storytelling.
•	Involving tribal leaders and panchayats in planning and evaluation stages.
•	Developing two-way communication models rooted in respect, reciprocity, and cultural competence.
A case study by Banerjee and Rout (2019) on the use of Nukkad Natak (street plays) by a steel company in Singhbhum successfully increased community participation in a sanitation project by over 60%, underlining the potential of culturally adapted methods .
The CSR-tribal interface in Jharkhand and Chhattisgarh is both complex and under-researched. While CSR funds are reaching tribal areas, the mechanisms of communication, perception, and community ownership remain underdeveloped. There is an urgent need for CSR frameworks that integrate local knowledge systems, linguistic diversity, and culturally grounded communication strategies to ensure that tribal development is not only inclusive but transformative.
This thesis builds on these identified gaps, proposing a framework that evaluates CSR communication specifically from the lens of tribal beneficiaries and stakeholders in Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand. 
Summary
This chapter reviewed the significant strands of literature related to Corporate Social Responsibility (CSR) communication and its societal implications, with a specific focus on India and the tribal-dominated states of Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand. Through thematic exploration, the chapter established the theoretical, empirical, and regional context for the research while identifying critical knowledge gaps that justify the necessity of this comparative study.
Summary of Literature Themes
The reviewed literature affirms that CSR, particularly post the enforcement of Section 135 of the Companies Act, 2013, has emerged as a legally mandated and strategically significant aspect of corporate operations in India. However, several interlinked themes surfaced throughout the review:
•	Legal and Policy Frameworks: While India’s statutory environment is robust, implementation varies significantly across regions, and CSR activities often reflect compliance rather than genuine community development goals .
•	Communication in CSR: The literature reveals a marked underemphasis on CSR communication practices. Most studies neglect two-way or culturally sensitive communication, particularly in linguistically and ethnically diverse regions like Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand .
•	Societal Impact Dimensions: Although there is recognition of CSR’s potential to contribute to education, health, and the environment, few studies comprehensively assess these dimensions together, especially in underrepresented states .
•	Theoretical Application: Theoretical frameworks such as Stakeholder Theory, Legitimacy Theory, and the Two-Way Symmetric Communication Model have not been sufficiently applied or tested in Indian tribal contexts .
•	Tribal Community Engagement: The intersection of CSR and tribal welfare remains underexplored. Literature on how CSR communication is perceived and internalized by tribal communities—who form a third of the population in both target states—is sparse and often anecdotal .
Identification of Research Gaps
From the literature, the following gaps are evident:

3.		Minimal beneficiary-focused evaluation of CSR initiatives	Justifies qualitative methods such as interviews and perception surveys
