Figure 29 Systematic Framework for CSR Initiative Documentation and Validation
Interpretation. CSR initiatives are documented across five parameters (corporate ID, implementation, initiative type, impact, communication) and validated through source verification, field evidence, and impact correlation. Study Area and Rationale
The present study focuses on a comparative analysis of Corporate Social Responsibility (CSR) communication and its contribution to societal betterment in two resource-rich states of India — Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand. These states, both carved out of larger states in November 2000, share similar socio-economic challenges, abundant mineral wealth, and a high proportion of tribal populations, while exhibiting distinct governance patterns and developmental trajectories. The selected districts from both states represent a cross-section of industrial, tribal, and rural settings where CSR interventions play a significant role in supporting community welfare.
Chhattisgarh: Raipur, Dhamtari, Balodabazar, Kanker, Bastar, and others
Chhattisgarh, located in central India, has one of the highest concentrations of mineral resources, contributing significantly to national coal, iron ore, and limestone production (Government of India, 2011). The selected districts of Raipur, Dhamtari, Balodabazar, Kanker, and Bastar represent diverse socio-economic and geographical profiles that are critical for understanding CSR dynamics:
•	Raipur serves as the state capital and industrial hub, hosting steel, power, and manufacturing sectors. CSR activities here focus on urban development, healthcare infrastructure, and education (Chhattisgarh Economic Survey, 2023).
•	Dhamtari, with a mix of agriculture and small-scale industries, has been a recipient of CSR programs targeting livelihood enhancement and healthcare access in rural pockets.
•	Balodabazar has seen significant CSR investment in educational infrastructure and community welfare initiatives due to its emerging industrial presence.
•	Kanker, classified as an aspirational district, faces development challenges related to poverty, malnutrition, and limited infrastructure, where CSR interventions aim to improve education and health indicators (NITI Aayog, 2021).
•	Bastar, with a large tribal population and forest-dependent communities, represents the region’s vulnerability to socio-economic exclusion. CSR initiatives here address healthcare outreach, educational inclusion, and sustainable livelihoods (Census of India, 2011).
•	Other district: there is more then 20 active CSR districts in Chhattisgarh. 
These districts collectively provide a representative sample of both accessible and remote areas where CSR programs are expected to mediate between corporate operations and community welfare.
Jharkhand: East Singhbhum (Jamshedpur/Tatanagar), West Singhbhum (Chaibasa), Ranchi, Ramgarh, Hazaribagh, and others
Jharkhand, situated in eastern India, is endowed with approximately 40% of India’s mineral reserves, particularly coal, iron ore, and copper (Government of India, 2011). The selected districts of East Singhbhum, West Singhbhum, Ranchi, Ramgarh, and Hazaribagh represent critical CSR intervention zones due to their industrial base and developmental challenges:
•	East Singhbhum, with Jamshedpur (Tatanagar) as its industrial nerve center, is home to major corporations like Tata Steel, making it a benchmark for CSR initiatives in urban-industrial environments.
•	West Singhbhum, predominantly tribal and forested, faces significant challenges in healthcare access and educational facilities, where CSR projects focus on basic services and capacity building.
•	Ranchi, the state capital, is a focal point for government-corporate collaboration, with CSR initiatives focusing on healthcare, education, and environmental awareness.
•	Ramgarh, known for coal mining activities, reflects a paradox where economic activity coexists with ecological degradation and limited community welfare outreach, making it a priority area for sustainable CSR interventions.
•	Hazaribagh, with agriculture as its economic backbone, receives CSR support aimed at skill development, educational access, and environmental conservation programs (Jharkhand Economic Survey, 2022).
•	Other dist. In Jharkhand: CSR practices in other district are do notable. 
These districts enable the study to explore CSR implementation in both industrialized centers and peripheral tribal regions, offering insights into the differential impact of communication strategies in contexts with varying governance and socio-cultural dynamics.
Rationale for District Selection
The selection of these five districts in each state is guided by the following considerations:
1.	Industrial Relevance: Districts like Raipur and East Singhbhum are among the most industrially developed, making them ideal for studying CSR practices in urban-industrial contexts.
2.	Tribal Concentration: Kanker, Bastar, West Singhbhum, and parts of Ramgarh are areas with high tribal populations and limited access to basic services, where CSR plays a crucial role in addressing gaps left by state welfare programs.
3.	Geographical Diversity: The inclusion of both centrally located and remote districts ensures that the study captures challenges related to accessibility, infrastructure, and communication.
4.	CSR Investment Patterns: These districts have been targeted by corporations for their CSR mandates, providing adequate scope for field-level engagement and data collection.
5.	Developmental Disparity: The contrast between districts such as Raipur and Bastar or East Singhbhum and West Singhbhum allows for comparative analysis across different socio-economic strata.
The multi-district approach facilitates a comprehensive understanding of how CSR communication strategies are adapted across varying local contexts, thereby contributing to broader policy recommendations for effective CSR governance in resource-rich but socio-economically challenged regions.
  
Figure 30 Comparative Analysis of CSR Communication in Chhattisgarh & Jharkhand
 Timeline 
This five-year study (2018–19 to 2022–23) captures CSR trends across changing economic conditions, assessing medium-term impacts, shifts in communication channels, program sustainability, and community feedback loops

Interpretation. The five-year retrospective captures medium-term CSR trends. CSR expenditure increased in both states, with Chhattisgarh (₹1,646 Cr) outspending Jharkhand (₹1,073 Cr) across the study period. Sampling Strategy
The sampling strategy is designed to ensure comprehensive representation across industries, beneficiary groups, geographical regions, and socio-economic categories. The selection of districts and sample units reflects a purposive yet structured approach aimed at addressing the research objectives while maintaining empirical rigor.
Selection of Districts	
The study covers five districts in each state, chosen based on their industrial significance, socio-economic diversity, and CSR engagement:
•	Chhattisgarh: Raipur, Dhamtari, Balodabazar, Kanker, Bastar, and other CSR active districts
•	Jharkhand	: East Singhbhum (Jamshedpur/Tatanagar), West Singhbhum (Chaibasa), Ranchi, Ramgarh, Hazaribagh, and other CSR active districts 
Sampling Units
The research identifies the following sampling units within each district:
1.	Corporate Entities: Companies undertaking CSR initiatives in sectors such as mining, steel, power, FMCG, and financial services.
2.	Beneficiaries: Individuals directly impacted by CSR interventions, including students, patients, women’s self-help group members, and farmers.
3.	Government Officials: Representatives from district administrations, planning departments, and CSR coordination cells.
4.	NGOs and Community-Based Organizations: Implementation partners involved in executing CSR projects and facilitating community participation.
Sampling Method
A multi-stage stratified sampling technique is employed:
•	Stage 1: District selection based on industrial presence, socio-economic profile, and CSR activity concentration.
•	Stage 2: Stratification of beneficiaries by sector (education, healthcare, environment) and socio-demographic characteristics (gender, age, tribal status).
•	Stage 3: Random sampling within each stratum to ensure balanced representation while controlling for selection bias.
Sample Size
The study targeted the sample size to ensure reliability and validity:
•	Quantitative Surveys: 400 direct CSR beneficiary respondents (200 from each state), evenly distributed across the five districts, with sector-wise proportional allocation.
•	Qualitative Interviews: 40 in-depth interviews (20 per state), including CSR heads, Implementing agency (NGO) coordinators, district officials, and community leaders.

 Data Collection Methods
This research employs a combination of primary and secondary data collection methods to comprehensively examine the effectiveness of Corporate Social Responsibility (CSR) communication in the selected districts of Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand. The mixed-methods approach ensures that both measurable trends and contextual realities are studied in depth. The data collection is structured around sector-specific CSR interventions in education, healthcare, and environmental sustainability and is supported by district-level governance frameworks and stakeholder participation.
Primary Data Collection
A structured questionnaire has been developed to quantitatively measure key dimensions related to CSR communication, including:
•	Awareness of CSR initiatives among beneficiaries.
•	Trust in corporate communication channels and messages.
•	Satisfaction with the services provided through CSR interventions.
•	Participation in CSR-driven programs and activities.
The questionnaire is divided into thematic sections and adapted for different beneficiary groups, including students, healthcare recipients, members of self-help groups, and farmers. Where applicable, it incorporates both closed-ended and Likert scale questions to facilitate statistical analysis. The survey is translated into local languages, such as Chhattisgarhi, Halbi, Ho, Mundari, and Kurukh, depending on the district, ensuring cultural and linguistic accessibility.
In-depth Interviews
Semi-structured interviews are conducted with key stakeholders to gain qualitative insights into CSR communication strategies, challenges, and best practices. The interviewees include:
•	CSR heads and corporate representatives from industries operating in each district.
•	Government officials involved in district planning, health, education, and environmental schemes.
•	NGO coordinators and field workers implementing CSR initiatives.
•	Community leaders, including Panchayat representatives, tribal elders, and women’s group leaders.
These interviews explore the formulation and execution of communication strategies, stakeholder engagement practices, and the perceived impact of CSR interventions at the grassroots level.
Beneficiary Impact Survey
Specialized survey tools were developed to measure the tangible benefits received in health, education, and livelihood sectors. These included indicators such as access to school infrastructure, immunization drives, maternal care, skill training outcomes, etc.
Secondary Data Collection
Secondary data is sourced from authentic, government-verified, and peer-reviewed publications to complement the primary data and ensure triangulation of findings. Sources include:
1.	Corporate Reports
o	Annual CSR reports submitted to the Ministry of Corporate Affairs.
o	Sustainability reports of prominent companies.
2.	Government Portals and Databases
o	National CSR Data Portal (csr.gov.in).
o	District-level health, education, and environmental reports from state government departments.
o	Census 2011 data for demographic insights and socio-economic indicators.
3.	Research Articles and Academic Journals
o	Studies on CSR communication, stakeholder engagement, and development metrics in rural and tribal contexts.
o	Reports from organizations like NITI Aayog, WHO, UNICEF, and academic databases like JSTOR and SpringerLink.
4.	Policy Documents and Frameworks
o	CSR rules and guidelines under the Companies Act, 2013.
o	State-specific CSR policies for Jharkhand and Chhattisgarh.
This secondary data supports the development of background context, validates survey findings, and offers historical insights into CSR trends and communication strategies.

Interpretation. Data collection combines primary sources (400 surveys, 40 interviews, impact surveys) and secondary sources (corporate reports, government portals, research articles, policy documents), merged through triangulation for comprehensive evidence.
Data Analysis Techniques
A combination of quantitative statistical tools and qualitative coding techniques was employed.
Quantitative Analysis:
•	Descriptive statistics (mean, percentage, frequency).
•	Cross-tabulation for comparative analysis.
•	Chi-square tests to determine association between variables.
•	SPSS will be used for data entry and analysis.
Qualitative Analysis:
•	Thematic content analysis of interview transcripts using manual coding.
•	NVivo software used to identify recurring themes such as "transparency," "inclusiveness," "cultural resonance," and "beneficiary engagement."
•	Narrative analysis for understanding impact stories shared by participants.
 Hypothesis Testing
Given that the five research hypotheses (H₁ through H₅) all examine the relationship between Corporate Social Responsibility (CSR) communication and societal betterment, a deliberate and methodologically sound decision was made to employ distinct statistical tests for each hypothesis. This approach was not arbitrary but was dictated by the fundamental differences in the structure of the variables and the specific research question each hypothesis sought to answer.
While the subject matter is similar, the statistical question being asked in each case is fundamentally different. Using a single analytical method (e.g., only regression or only chi-square) for all hypotheses would have resulted in statistical model misspecification, leading to invalid conclusions or an incomplete understanding of the data. The selection of each test was guided by the nature of the dependent variable (continuous, binary, or categorical) and the need to detect specific effects (e.g., differences, associations, interactions, or predictive strength).
 The following table and rationale justify the five distinct tests employed:
S. No.	Hypot
hesis	Research Focus	Statistical Question	Chosen Test	Justification for Test Selection
1.	H₁	State-level difference in communication effectiveness	"Is the mean Communication Effectiveness Score (CES) significantly different between Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand?"	Independent Samples t-test	This test is the standard for comparing the means of a continuous dependent variable (CES) across two independent groups (State). It directly quantifies the magnitude of the inter-state gap.
2.	H₂	Communication → Education	"How much does communication effectiveness increase the odds of perceiving educational improvement?"	Binary Logistic Regression	The dependent variable (Educational Improvement) is binary (Yes/No). Logistic regression is the appropriate method to calculate odds ratios and determine the predictive probability of a binary outcome based on one or more predictors.
3.	H₃	Communication → Healthcare	"Does the effect of the communication channel on healthcare perception depend on the state?" (Interaction effect)	Two-Way ANOVA	This test is uniquely suited to detect interaction effects between two categorical independent variables (State and Channel). A t-test or simple regression would have been unable to reveal that the effectiveness of a channel (e.g., verbal vs. print) varies significantly depending on whether the beneficiary is in Chhattisgarh or Jharkhand.
4.	H₄	Communication → Environment	"Is there a statistically significant association between the type of communication channel used and belief in environmental impact?"	Chi-Square Test of Independence	Both variables (Communication Channel and Environmental Impact Belief) are categorical. The Chi-square test is the non-parametric standard for determining whether two categorical variables are related or independent.
5.	H₅	Communication → Livelihood	"When controlling for state, what is the unique predictive power of communication effectiveness on livelihood outcomes?"	Multiple Linear Regression	This hypothesis required a model that could assess the relative contribution of multiple continuous and categorical predictors (CES + State) simultaneously on a continuous outcome (Sectoral Impact Score). Multiple regression provides beta coefficients (β) to compare the strength of each predictor.
`Table 12 Statistical Test Selection by Hypothesis

  
Figure 34 Statistical Test Selection by Hypothesis — Justification Matrix
 Interpretation. The study employs distinct statistical tests for each hypothesis based on variable structure rather than using a single test for all. H₁ uses an independent samples t-test to compare mean Communication Effectiveness Scores between Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand, as the dependent variable is continuous and there are two independent groups. H₂ uses binary logistic regression because the dependent variable (educational improvement perception) is binary (Yes/No), requiring odds ratios rather than mean differences.
H₃ uses a two-way ANOVA to test for interaction effects between state and communication channel on healthcare perception. This test is uniquely suited to reveal whether channel effectiveness varies by state—a nuance that t-tests or simple regression cannot capture. H₄ uses a chi-square test of independence because both variables (communication channel and environmental impact belief) are categorical, making this the standard non-parametric test for association.
H₅ uses multiple linear regression to assess the relative contribution of communication effectiveness and state on livelihood impact perception (a continuous outcome). This provides beta coefficients (β) to compare predictor strength. This methodological pluralism ensures construct validity—each hypothesis is tested with the most appropriate analytical tool for its specific variable structure and research question.
 Addressing the Specific Case of ANOVA (H₃):
It is important to clarify that ANOVA (Analysis of Variance) is a purely quantitative inferential statistical technique. It is frequently and correctly used to analyze numerical survey data, not qualitative interview data. In this study, ANOVA was selected for H₃ specifically to test for an interaction effect (State × Channel) on healthcare perception. This allowed the research to move beyond a simple "which state is better?" question to a more nuanced finding: the effectiveness of a given communication channel is conditional upon the state in which it is used. This level of analysis is impossible to achieve with a t-test, chi-square, or standard regression alone.
Methodological Pluralism
The research did not employ five different tests for redundancy, but rather to ensure construct validity and statistical conclusion validity. Each hypothesis required a specific analytical tool matched to the scale of measurement (nominal, ordinal, interval) and the specific research objective (difference, association, prediction, or interaction). This multi-method quantitative strategy, combined with the parallel qualitative analysis, provides the most rigorous and defensible testing of the study's core propositions.
Limitations of the Study
While this study employs a rigorous mixed-methods design and extensive field-based data collection, it acknowledges the following limitations inherent to its scope and context:
1.	Challenges in Isolating Individual Responses during Field Interviews: Data collection involved direct interactions with beneficiaries in community settings. Despite efforts to conduct separate interviews, the presence of family members, neighbours, or community groups during some conversations may have influenced individual responses due to social desirability bias or group consensus dynamics. This was particularly challenging when interviewing women, elders, and youth separately to capture diverse perspectives.
2.	Logistical Dependency on Company/Implementing Agency Coordination: Access to specific beneficiary communities and verification of CSR initiatives required coordination through corporate CSR teams or partner NGOs. While essential for fieldwork, the occasional presence of these representatives during initial interactions could have impacted the candidness of beneficiary feedback, especially regarding critical assessment of programs.
3.	Linguistic and Cultural Translation Complexities: The study covers regions with significant linguistic diversity (Gondi, Halbi, Chhattisgarhi, Sadri, Santhali, Kurukh, etc.). Although trained local interpreters and translated questionnaires were used, subtle nuances in communication, context-specific idioms, and oral narrative traditions in tribal communities may not have been fully captured in standardized data formats.
4.	Geographical and Temporal Boundaries: The research is focused on selected districts of Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand over a five-year period (2018–19 to 2022–23). While this allows for meaningful comparative analysis, the findings may not be directly generalizable to other states, urban corporate hubs, or longer-term impact assessments beyond the study timeline.
Delimitations of the Study 
The scope of this research is deliberately bounded as follows to ensure focus, feasibility, and contextual relevance:
1.	Geographical Focus: The study is confined to the states of Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand, encompassing both urban-industrial hubs (Raipur, Ranchi, Jamshedpur) and rural/tribal districts (Bastar, Kanker, West Singhbhum, etc.). This enables a within-state comparison of CSR communication across varied socio-geographical settings.
2.	Sectoral Concentration: Analysis focuses on CSR initiatives in four primary sectors: Education, Healthcare, Environmental Sustainability, and Livelihood Generation. These represent the majority of CSR expenditure and activity in the selected states, as evidenced by national and state-level CSR expenditure reports.
3.	Stakeholder Scope: The term "society" is operationally defined as direct beneficiaries of CSR initiatives—specifically tribal and rural populations, women, and marginalized groups. The study does not assess perspectives of investors, regulators, or corporate employees, unless directly relevant to communication processes.
4.	Communication Channels Analyzed: The research examines conventional, community-based, and digital communication channels used by companies or implementing agencies to reach beneficiaries. This includes interpersonal communication, print media, radio, television, social media, and digital platforms. Internal corporate communication, global reporting frameworks, and investor relations are excluded.
5.	Conceptual and Analytical Framework: The study employs a simple, self-constructed five-pillar analytical model focusing on: (1) Company, (2) Implementation Mode, (3) Initiative Type, (4) Impact, and (5) Communication Methods. It does not rely extensively on pre-existing theoretical models of development communication or CSR theory.
6.	Regulatory Context: The study is grounded in the Indian regulatory framework, primarily the Companies Act, 2013, and subsequent amendments. International guidelines are referenced only where they inform national policy or corporate practice in the selected states.
 CHAPTER 06: DATA ANALYSIS AND RESULTS 
PART A - QUANTITATIVE DATA ANALYSIS
Introduction to the Quantitative Analysis 
This chapter presents the analysis and interpretation of data collected from the quantitative survey of 400 beneficiaries and qualitative interviews with 40 key informants. The analysis is organized into several sections: profile of respondents, CSR awareness analysis, impact assessment analysis, communication effectiveness analysis, hypothesis testing, and qualitative findings. 
This chapter presents a comprehensive analysis of both quantitative and qualitative data to address the research objectives and hypotheses concerning Corporate Social Responsibility (CSR) communication and its contribution to societal betterment in Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand. The analysis integrates findings from 400 beneficiary surveys (200 per state) with insights from 40 in-depth stakeholder interviews (20 per state), following a mixed-methods approach. The chapter is structured in three main parts: quantitative data analysis (completed), qualitative thematic analysis (presented here), and an integrated synthesis of both datasets. This integrated approach enables a nuanced understanding of not only what patterns exist in CSR communication effectiveness, but why these patterns occur and how communication processes influence developmental outcomes in these resource-rich, tribal-majority states.
Q1-Q5: Demographic Information (Name, Age, Gender, Occupation, Address)
These questions were used for sampling and demographic profiling. The consolidated profile of 400 respondents shows an equal gender (50% Male, 50% Female) and state (50% Chhattisgarh, 50% Jharkhand) split, with most beneficiaries being between 31-45 years old, engaged in agriculture or daily wage labour, and having primary or secondary education.
The demographic data collected through Q1 to Q5 establishes the foundational profile of the 400 beneficiary respondents and confirms the representativeness of the sample. The equal gender distribution (50% male, 50% female) and equal state distribution (50% Chhattisgarh, 50% Jharkhand) were deliberately engineered to eliminate sampling bias, ensuring that any subsequent comparative findings between states or between male and female beneficiaries cannot be attributed to uneven group sizes. The age data (Q2) reveals that the majority of respondents fall within the 31-45 years bracket, indicating that the sample predominantly consists of economically active adults who are directly engaged in livelihood activities such as agriculture or daily wage labour (Q4). The occupation data (Q4) further confirms that the sample reflects the rural and tribal economic reality of both states, with minimal representation from salaried or formal sector employment. Finally, the address information (Q5) enabled district-level stratification across Raipur, Dhamtari, Balodabazar, Kanker, and Bastar in Chhattisgarh, and Ranchi, East Singhbhum, West Singhbhum, Hazaribagh, and Ramgarh in Jharkhand, ensuring geographical diversity across industrial hubs, aspirational districts, and remote tribal blocks. Together, Q1 through Q5 establish that the sample is balanced, representative, and appropriately grounded in the socio-economic context of CSR implementation in both states.
Q6: State Distribution
S. No.	Response Option	Total (n=400)	Chhattisgarh	Jharkhand
1.	Chhattisgarh	200 (50.0%)	200 (100%)	0 (0%)
2.	Jharkhand	200 (50.0%)	0 (0%)	200 (100%)
 
Figure 35 Sample Distribution by State

 Interpretation. The sample for this study was deliberately constructed with equal representation from both states to ensure methodological rigor and enable unbiased comparative analysis. Chhattisgarh contributed 200 beneficiaries (50% of the total sample), and Jharkhand contributed 200 beneficiaries (50%). This balanced design eliminates sample size bias, meaning any observed differences in CSR awareness, communication effectiveness, or impact perception between the two states can be attributed to genuine state-level contextual factors—such as governance structures, industrial composition, or communication strategies—rather than to imbalances in sample composition.
Within each state, the sample was further stratified to ensure comprehensive geographic and socio-economic representation. Each state's 200 respondents were distributed across five core districts (30 respondents per district) plus additional CSR-active districts (50 respondents). For Chhattisgarh, the core districts included Raipur, Dhamtari, Balodabazar, Kanker, and Bastar—ranging from the industrial capital to remote tribal areas. For Jharkhand, the core districts included East Singhbhum (Jamshedpur), West Singhbhum (Chaibasa), Ranchi, Ramgarh, and Hazaribagh—capturing both urban-industrial hubs and rural tribal belts.
This multi-district, stratified approach ensures that the sample captures the full diversity of CSR implementation contexts within each state. The equal state distribution, combined with district-level stratification, provides a robust foundation for all subsequent comparative analyses. The 50/50 split is not arbitrary but methodologically essential—it allows for direct statistical comparisons using independent t-tests, chi-square tests, and regression models without requiring weighted adjustments for unequal group sizes. The balanced design is a key strength of this study's comparative methodology.
Q7: Are you aware of any CSR initiatives being implemented in your area?
S.No.	Response Option	Total (n=400)	Chhattisgarh (n=200)	Jharkhand (n=200)
1.	Yes	286 (71.5%)	156 (78.0%)	130 (65.0%)
2.	No	114 (28.5%)	44 (22.0%)	70 (35.0%)
. 
Figure 36 Comparative Awareness of CSR Initiatives by State

Interpretation. The data reveals a substantial difference in basic CSR awareness between the two states. In Chhattisgarh, 78.0% of beneficiaries reported awareness of CSR initiatives being implemented in their areas, compared to only 65.0% in Jharkhand. This 13-percentage-point gap is statistically significant (χ² = 8.34, p < 0.01) and represents a fundamental difference in how effectively communities are reached with initial information about CSR programs. Overall, 286 of 400 beneficiaries (71.5%) across both states demonstrated basic awareness of CSR activities.
This awareness gap is methodologically significant because awareness is the prerequisite for all other CSR outcomes. Beneficiaries cannot participate in CSR programs, access services, provide feedback, or perceive impact if they are unaware that such initiatives exist. The lower awareness in Jharkhand (65%) means that more than one-third of the intended beneficiary population may be excluded from CSR benefits simply because they have not been adequately informed. This foundational gap cascades into the lower impact perceptions documented across education, environment, and livelihood sectors in Jharkhand.
The disparity likely reflects differences in communication strategies between the two states. Chhattisgarh's verbal-dominant approach (58% of beneficiaries receive information through community meetings) is inherently more effective at generating initial awareness than Jharkhand's print-dominant approach (35% through pamphlets and newspapers). Community meetings enable direct information dissemination, question-answer sessions, and word-of-mouth diffusion through social networks. Print media, by contrast, requires literacy, active seeking of information, and may not reach remote or marginalized populations. The awareness gap underscores that initial outreach strategy fundamentally shapes the reach and inclusivity of CSR programs.
Q8: Which company is undertaking CSR activities in your area?
S.No.	Response Option	Total (n=400)	Chhattisgarh (n=200)	Jharkhand (n=200)
1.	Accurate Identification	180 (45.0%)	104 (52.0%)	76 (38.0%)
2.	Partial Identification	120 (30.0%)	60 (30.0%)	60 (30.0%)
3.	Incorrect / Do Not Know	100 (25.0%)	36 (18.0%)	64 (32.0%)
 
Figure 37 Accuracy of Company Identification by State

Interpretation. The ability of beneficiaries to accurately identify which company is implementing CSR initiatives in their area is a critical measure of communication clarity and corporate attribution. Overall, less than half of all beneficiaries (45%) could accurately name the implementing company. Chhattisgarh demonstrates significantly stronger performance, with 52% of beneficiaries providing accurate identification compared to only 38% in Jharkhand—a 14-percentage-point gap. Partial identification (knowing some but not all details) was equal across both states at 30%.
The most concerning finding is that 32% of Jharkhand beneficiaries either incorrectly identified the company or did not know, nearly double the rate in Chhattisgarh (18%). This means nearly one-third of Jharkhand's beneficiary population is unaware of which corporate entity is responsible for the CSR programs they experience. This attribution gap has significant implications for corporate legitimacy, trust-building, and the reputational returns that motivate CSR investment. When beneficiaries cannot connect social benefits to specific corporate actors, the accountability chain is broken.
The disparity reflects differences in communication strategies. Chhattisgarh's verbal-dominant approach (58% through community meetings) allows for explicit naming and reinforcement of the corporate entity during face-to-face interactions. Implementing agency staff can repeatedly state the company name, answer questions about corporate identity, and build associations between visible outcomes (schools, health camps, infrastructure) and specific corporations. Jharkhand's print-dominant approach (35% through pamphlets) may include corporate logos and names, but printed materials are less interactive and may not be retained or understood by all beneficiaries, particularly those with limited literacy.
Q9: Do you agree that CSR activities in your area have had a impact?
S.No.	Response Option	Total (n=400)	Chhattisgarh (n=200)	Jharkhand (n=200)
1.	Yes	324 (81.0%)	172 (86.0%)	152 (76.0%)
2.	No	76 (19.0%)	28 (14.0%)	48 (24.0%)
 
Figure 38 Perception of Visible CSR Impact by State
 Interpretation. A strong majority of beneficiaries across both states agree that CSR activities have created visible impact in their communities. Overall, 324 of 400 beneficiaries (81%) responded affirmatively, indicating widespread recognition that CSR initiatives produce tangible outcomes. However, the perception of impact is significantly stronger in Chhattisgarh, where 86% of beneficiaries agree compared to 76% in Jharkhand—a 10-percentage-point gap. Conversely, nearly one-quarter (24%) of Jharkhand beneficiaries do not perceive visible impact, double the rate in Chhattisgarh (14%). The 10-point gap aligns with the pattern observed in awareness (13-point gap) and communication clarity (26-point gap), suggesting that perception of impact is not independent but directly influenced by communication effectiveness.
The higher impact perception in Chhattisgarh likely results from two reinforcing factors. First, Chhattisgarh's verbal-dominant communication strategy enables beneficiaries to connect specific visible changes (new schools, health camps, infrastructure) directly to CSR activities through explicit attribution during community meetings. Second, higher rates of local language use (80% vs 54%) ensure that beneficiaries fully comprehend the relationship between corporate investments and community outcomes. In Jharkhand, print-dominant communication and lower local language usage may result in beneficiaries observing changes without connecting them to CSR, thereby underestimating impact. The finding suggests that improving communication alone—without changing project quality—could substantially enhance impact perception.

Figure 39 Sectoral Distribution of CSR-Implementing Companies
Interpretation. The sectoral composition of CSR-implementing companies reveals a diverse corporate landscape with Banking/Finance as the dominant sector. Banking and financial services companies constitute 40% of CSR implementation in the study regions, followed by Metal (25%), Energy (20%), and Cement (15%). This distribution is consistent across both Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand, indicating that banking sector CSR has extensive pan-regional reach beyond the specific industrial zones where metal, energy, and cement companies operate.
The prominence of the Banking/Finance sector is a notable finding. Unlike metal, energy, and cement companies whose operations are geographically concentrated near mines, plants, and industrial clusters, banking companies have branch networks spanning rural and urban areas across both states. This widespread physical presence enables banking sector CSR to reach communities that may not be in proximity to traditional industrial sites. Beneficiaries in this study primarily referenced HDFC Bank, Axis Bank, and Yes Bank as implementing entities, reflecting the active CSR engagement of these financial institutions.
The sectoral distribution has implications for understanding CSR impact patterns. The Banking/Finance sector's focus on livelihood development, agricultural training, and women's self-help groups—rather than physical infrastructure—may explain the higher satisfaction scores observed for this sector. Metal, Energy, and Cement sectors traditionally invest in school buildings, hospitals, and roads, which, while visible, may generate different patterns of beneficiary engagement and satisfaction. The diversity of sectors in the sample strengthens the study's external validity, as findings reflect CSR implementation across multiple industrial contexts rather than a single sector.

Figure 40 Type of Implementing Companies in the Study

No beneficiary reported CSR implementation by Public Sector Undertakings (PSUs). This finding confirms strict adherence to the study's delimitation, which deliberately focused on analyzing CSR communication and impact of private corporate entities, excluding government-owned public sector undertakings.
The exclusive focus on Non-PSU companies allows for concentrated analysis of CSR practices within a homogeneous category of corporate entities. Private sector companies operate under the same statutory mandate (Section 135 of the Companies Act, 2013) as PSUs but may differ in governance structures, implementation models, and communication strategies. By excluding PSUs, the study avoids conflating findings across potentially different operational contexts.
The companies represented include private sector entities across banking, metal, energy, and cement sectors. All are subject to CSR provisions of the Companies Act, 2013, and have established CSR committees, policies, and annual reporting mechanisms. The findings on communication effectiveness, beneficiary awareness, and impact perception are therefore specific to private sector CSR implementation in Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand.

Figure 41 Perception of Educational Facilities Improvement by State

Interpretation. Education is a primary focus area for CSR expenditure in both states, and beneficiary perceptions reflect substantial recognition of improvements. Chhattisgarh demonstrates stronger positive perception, with 82% agreement compared to 74% in Jharkhand—an 8-percentage-point gap. The gap, while smaller than those observed for other outcomes like livelihood or quality of life, remains statistically meaningful.
Educational CSR typically involves school infrastructure (classrooms, libraries, toilets), digital learning resources (smart classrooms, computers), scholarships, and teacher training. These interventions have varying degrees of visibility. Physical infrastructure like school buildings and toilets is highly visible regardless of communication. Scholarships and teacher training, however, require effective communication for beneficiaries to recognize and attribute them to CSR.
Chhattisgarh's advantage likely stems from its verbal-dominant communication strategy, which enables explicit discussion of educational programs during community meetings, parent-teacher associations, and school enrolment drives. The use of local languages (80% in CG vs 54% in JH) ensures that information about scholarships, vocational training, and school improvements reaches all community members, including those with limited literacy. In Jharkhand, print-dominant communication may not effectively convey information about educational programs, particularly scholarships and skill development initiatives that require active beneficiary participation. The gap indicates that communication strategy influences even highly visible sectors like education.
Q13: Do you agree that CSR projects have made healthcare more accessible to your community?

1.	Yes	318 (79.5%)	158 (79.0%)	160 (80.0%)
2.	No	82 (20.5%)	42 (21.0%)	40 (20.0%)
 
Figure 42 Perception of Healthcare Accessibility Improvement by State

Interpretation. Healthcare stands out as the only sector in this study where Jharkhand slightly outperforms Chhattisgarh, with 80% of Jharkhand beneficiaries agreeing that CSR projects have improved healthcare accessibility compared to 79% in Chhattisgarh. Overall, 318 of 400 beneficiaries (79.5%) acknowledged healthcare improvements, representing the highest overall agreement among all four developmental sectors examined. The near-parity (1-percentage-point difference) contrasts sharply with other sectors where Chhattisgarh shows advantages of 8 to 18 points.
This unusual finding suggests that healthcare CSR possesses inherent visibility that partially compensates for weaker communication strategies. Unlike education or livelihood programs whose benefits may require sustained engagement to become apparent, healthcare interventions such as medical camps, ambulances, hospital equipment, and mobile health units produce immediately observable and universally valued outcomes.
The near-parity also may reflect Jharkhand's particular emphasis on healthcare CSR, as documented in qualitative interviews. The Tata Steel Foundation's cancer hospital in Jamshedpur, mobile medical units in West Singhbhum, and immunization drives coordinated with ASHA workers represent substantial, visible healthcare investments. These interventions are communicated through institutional channels (hospitals, government health workers) that, while not as participatory as community meetings, still effectively convey the availability of services. The finding indicates that for healthcare, physical infrastructure and service visibility can partially substitute for strategic communication, though optimal outcomes require both.

Figure 43 Perception of Environmental Impact by State

Interpretation. Environmental sustainability records the lowest perceived impact among all sectors, with only 66% of beneficiaries agreeing that CSR initiatives have positively impacted their areas. Chhattisgarh (72%) significantly outperforms Jharkhand (60%), a 12-percentage-point gap. This represents the second-largest state disparity, surpassed only by livelihood.
The low recognition reflects the inherent communication challenge of environmental CSR. Unlike education or healthcare, environmental benefits such as pollution reduction or groundwater recharge are diffuse, long-term, and not immediately observable. This temporal gap makes environmental outcomes disproportionately dependent on sustained, participatory communication.
Chhattisgarh's advantage stems from integrating environmental messaging into existing community structures, such as gram sabhas and watershed committees, making abstract concepts locally relevant. In Jharkhand, institutional communication channels fail to build the sustained engagement necessary for communities to recognize environmental outcomes.
 Q15: Do you think CSR initiatives have contributed to poverty alleviation in your area?
S.No.	Response Option	Total (n=400)	Chhattisgarh (n=200)	Jharkhand (n=200)
1.	Yes	228 (57.0%)	124 (62.0%)	104 (52.0%)
2.	No	172 (43.0%)	76 (38.0%)	96 (48.0%)
 
Figure 44 Perception of Poverty Alleviation Contribution by State

Interpretation. Poverty alleviation represents one of the most complex outcomes to achieve through CSR. Overall, just over half of beneficiaries (57%) agreed that CSR initiatives have contributed to poverty reduction in their areas. Chhattisgarh shows a 10-percentage-point advantage with 62% agreement compared to 52% in Jharkhand. Notably, 48% of Jharkhand beneficiaries do not perceive poverty alleviation contributions, indicating significant room for improvement in both program design and communication.
The modest recognition reflects the intrinsic difficulty of poverty alleviation as a developmental goal. Unlike constructing a school or conducting a medical camp, poverty reduction requires sustained, multi-dimensional interventions addressing income generation, skill development, market access, and financial inclusion. Benefits may accumulate over years, and attribution to specific CSR programs is challenging when multiple actors influence economic outcomes. Strategic communication can help beneficiaries connect discrete interventions to longer-term economic improvements.
Chhattisgarh's 10-point advantage likely reflects its integrated livelihood programs in Dhamtari, Kanker, and Bastar, where village meetings explicitly framed activities within poverty alleviation narratives. Beneficiaries learned how agricultural training, market linkages, and self-help groups collectively contribute to household economic security. In Jharkhand, livelihood programs appeared more fragmented, with communication focused on individual project outputs rather than coherent poverty reduction outcomes, making it harder for beneficiaries to perceive cumulative economic impact.
 Q16: Has the quality of public infrastructure (roads, electricity, etc.) improved due to CSR activities?
S.No.	Response Option	Total (n=400)	Chhattisgarh (n=200)	Jharkhand (n=200)
1.	Yes	280 (70.0%)	150 (75.0%)	130 (65.0%)
2.	No	120 (30.0%)	50 (25.0%)	70 (35.0%)
 
Figure 45 Perception of Public Infrastructure Improvement by State

Interpretation. Public infrastructure improvements represent some of the most visible and tangible outcomes of CSR activities. Overall, 280 of 400 beneficiaries (70%) agreed that the quality of public infrastructure—including roads, electricity, water supply, and community buildings—has improved due to CSR initiatives. Chhattisgarh shows a 10-percentage-point advantage with 75% agreement compared to 65% in Jharkhand. The gap persists even for these highly observable physical developments.
Infrastructure projects are inherently visible by design. A newly constructed road, repaired school building, installed streetlight, or renovated community hall is physically present and continuously observable to community members. Unlike environmental benefits that may take years to manifest, infrastructure improvements are immediate and tangible. Given this visibility, the 10-point gap suggests that the difference between states is not merely about project existence but about attribution—whether beneficiaries correctly connect visible improvements to CSR activities rather than government programs or other actors.
Chhattisgarh's higher recognition likely stems from stronger attribution communication. Community meetings in Chhattisgarh explicitly name the corporate entity responsible for infrastructure projects, with signage, plaques, and public announcements reinforcing the connection. In Jharkhand's print-dominant approach, attribution information may be present in pamphlets but not effectively communicated to all beneficiaries, particularly those with limited literacy. The finding demonstrates that even for highly visible outcomes, strategic communication remains essential for ensuring beneficiaries correctly attribute improvements to CSR rather than other sources.

Figure 46 Perception of Sanitation Facilities Improvement by State

Interpretation. Sanitation improvements, including toilet construction, waste management systems, and hygiene infrastructure, represent highly visible CSR outcomes that directly impact daily life. Overall, 276 of 400 beneficiaries (69%) agreed that sanitation facilities have improved due to CSR initiatives. Chhattisgarh shows a 10-percentage-point advantage with 74% agreement compared to 64% in Jharkhand. More than one-third (36%) of Jharkhand beneficiaries do not perceive sanitation improvements from CSR activities.
The 10-point gap mirrors the pattern observed for public infrastructure, suggesting that even highly visible, tangible outcomes are perceived differently across states. Sanitation facilities—unlike abstract environmental benefits—are immediately observable and directly usable by community members. A new toilet block or waste management system is physically present and continuously available. Despite this inherent visibility, the gap persists, reinforcing that attribution communication is as important as project implementation. Beneficiaries may observe improvements but fail to connect them to CSR without explicit, repeated attribution.
Chhattisgarh's advantage likely stems from integrating sanitation messaging into broader health and hygiene campaigns communicated through community meetings, self-help groups, and ASHA workers. This multi-channel approach ensures that beneficiaries not only see the infrastructure but understand its connection to corporate CSR programs. In Jharkhand, sanitation improvements may be communicated primarily through print materials or corporate branding on facilities, which may not reach all community members or effectively convey the corporate-community partnership underlying the intervention.
 Q18: Have CSR initiatives contributed to providing livelihood opportunities in any of the following areas?
S.No.	Response Option	Total (n=400)	Chhattisgarh (n=200)	Jharkhand (n=200)
1.	Yes	236 (59.0%)	132 (66.0%)	104 (52.0%)

Figure 47 Perception of Livelihood Opportunities Creation by State

Interpretation. Livelihood generation represents the sector with the largest state disparity in this study. Overall, 236 of 400 beneficiaries (59%) agreed that CSR initiatives have contributed to livelihood opportunities. Chhattisgarh shows a 14-percentage-point advantage with 66% agreement compared to only 52% in Jharkhand. Notably, nearly half (48%) of Jharkhand beneficiaries do not perceive livelihood improvements from CSR activities—the highest non-recognition rate across all sectors examined.
The substantial gap reflects the unique communication challenges of livelihood interventions. Unlike building a school or conducting a medical camp, livelihood programs require beneficiaries to change existing productive behavior—adopting new agricultural techniques, forming self-help groups, accessing market linkages, or pursuing vocational training. These outcomes depend heavily on sustained, participatory communication that builds trust, demonstrates benefits, and provides ongoing support. Livelihood programs cannot succeed through one-way information dissemination alone.
Chhattisgarh's advantage is explained by its embedded communication architecture. In Dhamtari, Agricultural Production Clusters and women's self-help groups serve as standing forums for information exchange, collective decision-making, and accountability. Farmers learn new techniques through field demonstrations, not pamphlets. Women receive training through group meetings, not mailers. In Jharkhand, print-dominant communication and fragmented livelihood messaging fail to provide the sustained engagement necessary for behavior change, resulting in significantly lower recognition of livelihood opportunities.

Figure 48 Perception of Women's Empowerment by State

Interpretation. Women's empowerment represents a critical but challenging outcome for CSR interventions, requiring sensitive implementation and trust-building with female community members. Overall, 244 of 400 beneficiaries (61%) agreed that CSR initiatives have empowered women in their communities. Chhattisgarh shows a 14-percentage-point advantage with 68% agreement compared to 54% in Jharkhand. Nearly half (46%) of Jharkhand beneficiaries do not perceive women's empowerment from CSR activities.
The substantial gap reflects differences in both program design and communication strategies for women-centric initiatives. Successful women's empowerment programs—self-help groups, vocational training, financial literacy, and leadership development—require creating safe spaces for women to participate, discuss concerns, and build collective agency. These programs depend on word-of-mouth diffusion, trusted female facilitators, and community-level acceptance. Communication cannot be limited to pamphlets or announcements; it requires sustained, trust-based engagement.
Chhattisgarh's advantage stems from integrating women's groups into broader livelihood programs, as seen in Dhamtari and Kanker. Women's self-help groups function as both program delivery mechanisms and communication channels, enabling peer learning, collective decision-making, and visible leadership development. In Jharkhand, women's empowerment initiatives appear less systematically integrated, with communication relying on institutional channels that may not reach women effectively, particularly those in remote or conservative communities.

Figure 49 Perception of Leadership Development Inclusion by State

Interpretation. Leadership development is the only indicator in this study where overall beneficiary agreement falls below 50%. Only 196 of 400 beneficiaries (49%) agreed that CSR initiatives included leadership development components for local communities. Chhattisgarh shows a 14-percentage-point advantage with 56% agreement compared to 42% in Jharkhand. More than half (58%) of Jharkhand beneficiaries do not perceive leadership development from CSR activities—the highest non-recognition rate across all indicators.
The low overall recognition reflects that leadership development represents an advanced, capacity-building outcome that is less common than basic service delivery in CSR programming. Unlike school construction or medical camps, leadership development requires sustained investment in training, mentorship, and community organizing. It also requires beneficiaries to recognize and value less tangible outcomes such as decision-making skills, advocacy capacity, and community representation—capacities that develop gradually and may not be immediately attributed to CSR.
Chhattisgarh's 14-point advantage likely reflects its community-embedded program design, where water management committees, agricultural producer groups, and women's self-help groups explicitly develop leadership skills among members. These structures create visible leadership roles—secretary, treasurer, president—that community members recognize as outcomes of CSR-supported organizing. In Jharkhand, where community structures are less systematically developed, leadership development may occur informally without clear attribution to CSR, resulting in substantially lower recognition.
 Q21: Do you agree that the CSR projects improved your quality of life?
S.No.	Response Option	Total (n=400)	Chhattisgarh (n=200)	Jharkhand (n=200)
1.	Yes	284 (71.0%)	160 (80.0%)	124 (62.0%)
2.	No	116 (29.0%)	40 (20.0%)	76 (38.0%)
 
Figure 50 Perception of Quality-of-Life Improvement by State

 Interpretation. Quality-of-life improvement represents the most comprehensive and summative measure of CSR impact, capturing the cumulative effect of all interventions across education, healthcare, environment, and livelihood sectors. The 18-percentage-point gap between Chhattisgarh (80%) and Jharkhand (62%) is the largest observed for any direct impact indicator in this study. Overall, 284 of 400 beneficiaries (71%) agreed that CSR projects have improved their quality of life. However, four out of five Chhattisgarh beneficiaries feel their lives have improved, compared to only three out of five in Jharkhand.
This substantial gap is particularly significant because quality of life represents the ultimate goal of CSR interventions—the tangible, felt improvement in daily living conditions, economic security, health outcomes, and overall well-being. Unlike sector-specific indicators (school attendance, medical camp utilization), quality of life captures beneficiaries' holistic assessment of whether CSR has made a meaningful difference. The 18-point gap indicates that the cumulative effect of CSR, as perceived by beneficiaries, is dramatically different between the two states.
The finding encapsulates the core conclusion of this study: the entire CSR ecosystem in Chhattisgarh—from project selection to implementation to communication—combines to create a substantially stronger sense of community betterment. The gap is not explained by expenditure differences or state wealth but by systematic differences in communication architecture. Verbal-dominant, community-embedded, linguistically appropriate communication enables beneficiaries to connect discrete CSR interventions to their overall life circumstances. In Jharkhand, print-dominant, institutionally-mediated communication fails to build this holistic understanding, resulting in significantly lower perceived quality-of-life improvement. Figure 51 Primary Area of CSR Impact Perceived by State
Interpretation. When forced to select the single most significant area of impact from CSR projects, beneficiaries in the two states show markedly different response patterns. In Chhattisgarh, "Overall Development" dominates at 40%, followed by Economic Development (28%), Health Improvement (22%), and Educational Development (10%). In Jharkhand, responses are more evenly distributed: Overall Development (30%), Educational Development (22%), Economic Development (24%), and Health Improvement (24%). The most striking difference is Educational Development: 22% in Jharkhand versus only 10% in Chhattisgarh.
The Chhattisgarh pattern indicates that beneficiaries perceive CSR as contributing to integrated, holistic community progress rather than isolated sectoral improvements. When asked to choose one area, 40% selected "Overall Development"—a recognition that CSR benefits are interconnected and cumulative. This perception likely results from Chhattisgarh's communication strategy, which frames individual interventions (schools, health camps, livelihood programs) within an overarching narrative of community betterment discussed during village meetings.
The Jharkhand pattern reveals more fragmented perception, with beneficiaries viewing CSR impact through specific sectoral lenses, particularly education (22%). This suggests that in Jharkhand, CSR communication may focus on individual projects rather than integrated community development, making it harder for beneficiaries to perceive holistic impact. The finding has strategic implications: communication that connects discrete interventions to an overarching betterment narrative significantly increases beneficiaries' ability to recognize cumulative CSR value.
Q23: Was the information you received about CSR activities simple and in clear language?
S. No.	Response Option	Total (n=400)	Chhattisgarh (n=200)	Jharkhand (n=200)
1.	Yes	292 (73.0%)	172 (86.0%)	120 (60.0%)
2.	No	108 (27.0%)	28 (14.0%)	80 (40.0%)
 
Figure 52 Clarity of CSR Communication by State

Interpretation. Message clarity is the cornerstone of effective communication, and this indicator reveals the most dramatic disparity between the two states. In Chhattisgarh, an overwhelming 86% of beneficiaries found CSR information simple and clear, compared to only 60% in Jharkhand—a 26-percentage-point gap. This means two out of five Jharkhand beneficiaries (40%) found CSR communication complicated or unclear. Overall, 292 of 400 beneficiaries (73%) across both states reported clear communication.
The 26-point gap is foundational because clarity enables all other communication outcomes. Without clear information, beneficiaries cannot understand program objectives, access services, provide feedback, or develop trust in corporate actors. Chhattisgarh's high clarity rate (86%) reflects its verbal-dominant communication approach, where community meetings allow for real-time clarification, question-answering, and reinforcement of key messages in local languages. Face-to-face interaction enables communicators to adapt language, simplify complex concepts, and confirm understanding.
Jharkhand's low clarity rate (60%) likely stems from its print-dominant approach. Printed materials require literacy, assume standard language comprehension, and provide no mechanism for beneficiaries to seek clarification when confused. A pamphlet delivered to a village cannot answer questions, rephrase confusing sentences, or adapt to different learning needs. The 40% of Jharkhand beneficiaries who find communication unclear represent a substantial population effectively excluded from meaningful CSR engagement. Improving clarity requires shifting toward verbal, interactive communication channels.
 Q24: Did you receive information about CSR activities in your local language?
S. No.	Response Option	Total (n=400)	Chhattisgarh (n=200)	Jharkhand (n=200)
1.	Yes	268 (67.0%)	160 (80.0%)	108 (54.0%)
2.	No	132 (33.0%)	40 (20.0%)	92 (46.0%)
 
Figure 53 Use of Local Language in CSR Communication by State

Interpretation. Local language usage is a critical determinant of communication accessibility, particularly in linguistically diverse tribal and rural regions. Chhattisgarh demonstrates substantially stronger performance, with 80% of beneficiaries receiving CSR information in their local language compared to only 54% in Jharkhand—a 26-percentage-point gap. This means nearly half (46%) of Jharkhand beneficiaries do not receive CSR communication in a language they understand. Overall, 268 of 400 beneficiaries (67%) received local language communication.
The 26-point gap mirrors the clarity gap exactly, confirming that language accessibility is the primary driver of communication clarity. When beneficiaries receive information in their native language—Chhattisgarhi, Gondi, Halbi in Chhattisgarh; Santhali, Mundari, Kurukh, Ho in Jharkhand—comprehension improves dramatically. Chhattisgarh's high local language usage (80%) directly enables its high clarity rate (86%). Jharkhand's low local language usage (54%) directly contributes to its low clarity rate (60%). The relationship is causal: language accessibility determines clarity.
The disparity reflects different operational approaches. Chhattisgarh's implementing agencies employ field staff from local communities who speak tribal dialects, and community meetings are conducted in local languages. Materials are translated and oral communication adapted to linguistic contexts. In Jharkhand, greater linguistic diversity (over 30 tribal languages) creates complexity, but the lower performance suggests insufficient investment in translation, multilingual staff, and culturally adapted communication. Without local language access, CSR communication cannot reach, inform, or engage substantial portions of beneficiary populations.
Q25: Through which medium do you receive information about CSR projects?
S. No.	Response Option	Total (n=400)	Chhattisgarh (n=200)	Jharkhand (n=200)
1.	Verbal	180 (45.0%)	116 (58.0%)	64 (32.0%)
2.	Print Media	110 (27.5%)	40 (20.0%)	70 (35.0%)
3.	Electronic Media	80 (20.0%)	36 (18.0%)	44 (22.0%)
4.	Social media	30 (7.5%)	8 (4.0%)	22 (11.0%)
 
Figure 54 Primary CSR Communication Channels by State
Interpretation. The choice of communication channels represents the most consequential strategic divergence between the two states. Chhattisgarh is verbal-dominant, with 58% of beneficiaries receiving information through community meetings, village-level consultations, and door-to-door outreach. Jharkhand is print-dominant, with 35% relying on pamphlets, brochures, and newspapers, while verbal channels reach only 32% of beneficiaries—a 26-percentage-point gap in verbal channel usage. Electronic media (television, radio) shows modest use in both states (18-22%), while social media remains negligible (4-11%) due to limited digital access in rural areas.
This channel divergence explains the cascade of disparities documented throughout this study. Verbal channels enable dialogue, immediate feedback, local language adaptation, and trust-building through interpersonal relationships. When a company representative holds a village meeting, community members can ask questions, seek clarification, hear information in their native dialect, and develop personal connections with implementing agency staff. Print channels are one-way, literacy-dependent, and provide no mechanism for clarification or feedback. A pamphlet cannot answer questions or adapt to different comprehension levels.
The 58% verbal channel usage in Chhattisgarh directly produces higher clarity (86%), higher local language use (80%), higher awareness (78%), and higher impact perception across most sectors. Jharkhand's 35% print channel usage produces lower outcomes across all these metrics. The finding has direct policy implications: CSR communication guidelines should prioritize investment in interpersonal, community-embedded channels over mediated, one-directional formats. The negligible social media usage indicates that digital platforms cannot substitute for face-to-face engagement in these contexts.
RESULTS: QUANTITATIVE DATA ANALYSIS
CSR Awareness Analysis
This section examines the level of awareness among beneficiaries regarding CSR initiatives in their areas. Awareness is a prerequisite for accessing CSR benefits and is influenced by the effectiveness of communication strategies employed by companies and implementing agencies.
S.No.	Awareness Level	Chhattisgarh (n=200)	Jharkhand (n=200)	Total (n=400)
1.		Aware of CSR Initiatives	156 (78.0%)	130 (65.0%)	286 (71.5%)
2.		Not Aware of CSR Initiatives	44 (22.0%)	70 (35.0%)	114 (28.5%)
3.		Total	200 (100%)	200 (100%)	400 (100%)
Table 13 CSR Awareness by State
Interpretation. The data reveals that 78.0% of respondents in Chhattisgarh were aware of CSR initiatives in their areas, compared to 65.0% in Jharkhand. This 13-percentage-point difference suggests that CSR communication is more effective in Chhattisgarh, possibly due to differences in communication strategies, company presence, or community engagement approaches.
  
Figure 55 CSR Awareness by State — Comparative Analysis (n=400)
 Interpretation. The data reveals a substantial difference in basic CSR awareness between the two states. In Chhattisgarh, 78.0% of beneficiaries reported awareness of CSR initiatives being implemented in their areas, compared to only 65.0% in Jharkhand. This 13-percentage-point gap is statistically significant (χ² = 8.34, p < 0.01) and represents a fundamental difference in how effectively communities are reached with initial information about CSR programs. Overall, 286 of 400 beneficiaries (71.5%) across both states demonstrated basic awareness of CSR activities.
This awareness gap is methodologically significant because awareness is the prerequisite for all other CSR outcomes. Beneficiaries cannot participate in CSR programs, access services, provide feedback, or perceive impact if they are unaware that such initiatives exist. The lower awareness in Jharkhand (65%) means that more than one-third of the intended beneficiary population may be excluded from CSR benefits simply because they have not been adequately informed. This foundational gap cascades into the lower impact perceptions documented across education, environment, and livelihood sectors in Jharkhand.
The disparity likely reflects differences in communication strategies between the two states. Chhattisgarh's verbal-dominant approach (58% of beneficiaries receive information through community meetings) is inherently more effective at generating initial awareness than Jharkhand's print-dominant approach (35% through pamphlets and newspapers). Community meetings enable direct information dissemination, question-answer sessions, and word-of-mouth diffusion through social networks. Print media, by contrast, requires literacy, active seeking of information, and may not reach remote or marginalized populations. The awareness gap underscores that initial outreach strategy fundamentally shapes the reach and inclusivity of CSR programs.
