 Impact Assessment Analysis
This section presents a comparative assessment of perceived CSR impact across four key developmental domains: education, healthcare, environmental sustainability, and livelihood generation. The analysis is based on beneficiary responses from Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand, aiming to understand how CSR interventions are experienced and interpreted at the community level. Rather than measuring only program outputs, this section focuses on perceived outcomes, offering insight into how effectively CSR initiatives translate into visible social change. The comparison between the two states enables an evaluation of both sectoral performance and the role of contextual and communication factors in shaping public perception.
Across both states, CSR initiatives are widely recognized as contributing to community development, though the intensity of perceived impact varies by sector and location. Education and healthcare emerge as the most positively perceived areas, reflecting their relatively visible and service-oriented nature. Environmental sustainability and livelihood generation, by contrast, show lower levels of recognition and greater inter-state variation, indicating that long-term and less tangible outcomes are more difficult for beneficiaries to attribute directly to CSR activities. These variations highlight that visibility and immediacy of outcomes significantly influence perception.
This section establishes the analytical foundation for understanding sector-wise CSR impact differences and sets the stage for detailed interpretation of each developmental domain.

 
Figure 58 Sectoral Perceptions of CSR Impact — Chhattisgarh vs Jharkhand

Interpretation. The comparative analysis reveals that CSR initiatives are widely recognized across both states, though the intensity of perceived impact varies significantly by sector. Education (78% combined) and healthcare (79.5% combined) emerge as the most positively perceived areas, reflecting their visible and service-oriented nature. Environmental sustainability (66% combined) and livelihood generation (59% combined) show substantially lower recognition, indicating that long-term and less tangible outcomes are more difficult for beneficiaries to attribute directly to CSR activities. This visibility gradient demonstrates that the immediacy of outcomes significantly influences perception.
A consistent pattern across all four domains is the higher perception of impact in Chhattisgarh compared to Jharkhand, though the magnitude varies considerably by sector. Healthcare shows near-parity (79% CG vs 80% JH), suggesting that healthcare infrastructure possesses inherent visibility that partially compensates for weaker communication strategies. Livelihood generation shows the widest gap (14 points), followed by environment (12 points), education (8 points), and healthcare (1 point). This variation indicates that certain types of CSR interventions are more sensitive to communication and contextual factors than others.
The findings underscore that communication effectiveness acts as a central mediating factor between CSR implementation and perceived impact. States with stronger verbal engagement, local language usage, and participatory communication structures demonstrate higher levels of awareness and attribution across all sectors. Conversely, reliance on print-based or institutional communication channels corresponds with lower recognition of CSR contributions, even when similar interventions are present. Impact perception is not determined solely by expenditure scale or project quality, but by how effectively information is shared and internalized within communities. Educational Impact
Education is a major focus area for CSR initiatives in both states. The study examined beneficiary perceptions of improvements in educational facilities and opportunities resulting from CSR activities.
 
Figure 59 Education Impact of CSR Initiatives — Beneficiary perception of improved educational facilities
Interpretation. This figure presents beneficiary perceptions of educational improvements resulting from CSR initiatives across both states. The comparative bar chart reveals that 82% of respondents in Chhattisgarh acknowledge educational facility improvements, compared to 74% in Jharkhand, representing an 8-percentage-point gap. This difference suggests that CSR interventions in education are either more substantial, more visible, or more effectively communicated in Chhattisgarh.
The accompanying data table provides sectoral specificity, showing that school infrastructure improvements receive the highest recognition, followed by smart classroom installations and scholarship programs. Education consistently ranks as a top CSR expenditure category nationally, and these findings confirm that beneficiaries recognize these investments. The higher recognition in Chhattisgarh aligns with the state's stronger overall communication effectiveness scores documented elsewhere in this study.
The 8-point gap, while smaller than gaps observed in other sectors like livelihood or quality of life, remains statistically meaningful. It indicates that even in a priority sector with substantial corporate investment across both states, Chhattisgarh's approach—characterized by community meetings and local language communication—generates greater beneficiary awareness and acknowledgment of educational outcomes.
 Healthcare Impact
Healthcare is another critical area of CSR focus, with companies investing in health infrastructure, medical camps, and preventive health programs. The study assessed beneficiary perceptions of improvements in healthcare accessibility and quality.
 
Figure 60 Healthcare Impact of CSR Initiatives — Beneficiary perception by state with channel analysis
Interpretation. This figure presents an unusual finding in the study: near-parity in healthcare impact perception between Chhattisgarh (79%) and Jharkhand (80%). Unlike education, environment, and livelihood where Chhattisgarh consistently outperforms Jharkhand, healthcare shows virtual equality, with Jharkhand registering a marginal 1-point advantage. This suggests that healthcare CSR interventions possess inherent visibility that partially compensates for weaker communication strategies.
The inset box reveals critical nuance: communication channel choice significantly moderates healthcare impact perception. Verbal channels (community meetings, door-to-door outreach) generate 85% beneficiary agreement, compared to 68% for print media and 65% for electronic media. This finding explains why Jharkhand achieves parity despite lower overall communication effectiveness—its healthcare infrastructure investments (hospitals, medical camps, ambulances) are physically visible and do not rely solely on formal communication.
The policy implication is significant. For healthcare CSR, physical infrastructure visibility can partially substitute for communication strategy. However, optimal outcomes require both visible interventions AND verbal, community-embedded communication. Jharkhand's healthcare success offers a replicable model, but its communication gaps in other sectors suggest that infrastructure visibility alone cannot substitute for comprehensive communication planning across all developmental domains.
Environmental Impact
Environmental sustainability is an increasingly important area of CSR focus, with companies investing in afforestation, water conservation, renewable energy, and waste management. The study examined beneficiary perceptions of positive environmental changes resulting from CSR initiatives.
 
Figure 61 Environmental Impact of CSR Initiatives — Beneficiary perception with sectoral gap comparison
Interpretation. This figure reveals that environmental CSR receives the lowest overall recognition (66% combined across both states) among all four developmental sectors examined in this study. The 12-percentage-point gap between Chhattisgarh (72%) and Jharkhand (60%) represents the second-largest state disparity, surpassed only by livelihood (14 points). This pattern suggests that environmental benefits—pollution reduction, biodiversity conservation, groundwater recharge—are less immediately tangible and visible than school buildings or medical camps.
The sectoral gap comparison bar chart contextualizes environmental performance against other domains. Education shows an 8-point gap favoring Chhattisgarh; healthcare shows near-parity at 1 point; environment shows a substantial 12-point gap; livelihood shows the largest gap at 14 points. This ordering reflects the visibility gradient of CSR interventions: highly visible infrastructure (healthcare) minimizes communication gaps, while abstract, long-term outcomes (environment, livelihood) magnify them.
The findings underscore a critical challenge for environmental CSR communication. Unlike healthcare where physical infrastructure speaks for itself, environmental initiatives require sustained, participatory communication to generate community awareness and acknowledgment. Chhattisgarh's advantage stems from integrating environmental messaging into existing community structures (gram sabhas, watershed committees), making abstract concepts locally relevant and observable.
Economic Empowerment (Livelihood) Impact
Livelihood generation and economic empowerment are key objectives of CSR initiatives, particularly in rural areas where employment opportunities are limited. The study examined beneficiary perceptions of livelihood opportunities created through CSR activities.
 
Figure 62 Economic Empowerment Impact of CSR Initiatives — Livelihood perception with driver analysis
Interpretation. This figure presents the largest state disparity observed in the study: a 14-percentage-point gap between Chhattisgarh (66%) and Jharkhand (52%) on perceived livelihood opportunities created by CSR. Livelihood generation—encompassing skill development, agricultural training, self-help groups, and market linkage—requires the most sustained community engagement among all CSR sectors. The substantial gap indicates fundamental differences in how livelihood programs are designed, implemented, and communicated across the two states.
The driver analysis box presents regression findings showing that Communication Effectiveness (β = 0.67) is more than four times as powerful as state-level context (β = 0.15) in predicting livelihood impact perception. The model explains 50% of variance (R² = 0.50), indicating robust predictive power. This is a novel and policy-relevant finding: how CSR is communicated matters more for livelihood outcomes than whether a program operates in Chhattisgarh or Jharkhand.
The qualitative data explains this quantitative finding. In Chhattisgarh's Dhamtari district, livelihood communication is embedded within Agricultural Production Clusters and women's self-help groups that function as standing forums for information exchange and accountability. In Jharkhand, livelihood communication relies more on institutional channels and print materials, which lack the dialogic, trust-building capacity necessary for convincing communities to change productive behavior. The 14-point gap is not inevitable; it is a function of communication architecture.


Communication Effectiveness Analysis
This section examines the effectiveness of CSR communication from the perspective of beneficiaries. Communication effectiveness is assessed through measures of clarity, language appropriateness, channel preferences, and overall satisfaction with information received.
 
Figure 63 Communication Channel Mapping — Channel distribution and effectiveness comparison
Interpretation. This figure reveals the strategic communication difference that explains most outcome disparities between the two states. Chhattisgarh's communication is verbal-dominant (58%), relying on community meetings, village-level consultations, and door-to-door outreach. Jharkhand's communication is print-dominant (35%), relying on pamphlets, brochures, and newspapers, with verbal channels used by only 32% of beneficiaries. This 26-percentage-point difference in verbal channel usage is the single most consequential strategic divergence documented in this study.
The effectiveness ranking bar confirms that verbal channels generate the highest beneficiary awareness and impact perception, followed by print, electronic, and social media. Verbal communication enables dialogue, immediate feedback, cultural and linguistic adaptation, and trust-building through interpersonal relationships. Print and electronic media, being one-directional, lack these dialogic properties. Social media remains negligible in both states (4-11%), reflecting limited digital access in rural and tribal areas.
The channel mapping explains the cascade of disparities documented throughout this study. Verbal-dominant communication in Chhattisgarh produces higher clarity (86% vs 60%), higher local language use (80% vs 54%), higher awareness (78% vs 65%), and ultimately higher impact perception across most sectors. Jharkhand's print-dominant approach, while compliant with disclosure requirements, fails to engage communities as active participants. This finding has direct policy implications: CSR communication guidelines should prioritize investment in interpersonal, community-embedded channels over mediated, one-directional formats.

Comparative CSR Communication Impact
Effective communication determines whether CSR investment translates into perceived community betterment. Comparing Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand reveals that a verbal-embedded approach outperforms print-mediated models across nearly all impact indicators.
 
Figure 64 Comparative CSR Impact Summary — Chhattisgarh vs Jharkhand (all indicators) Interpretation. This figure synthesizes all major findings into a single comparative framework, presenting nine indicators ranked by gap magnitude. Chhattisgarh outperforms Jharkhand on eight of nine indicators. The largest gaps appear in communication process variables: local language use (26 points), communication clarity (26 points), and Communication Effectiveness Score (20 points converted). These foundational communication disparities cascade into outcome gaps: quality of life (18 points), livelihood (14 points), and environmental impact (12 points).
The single exception is healthcare, where Jharkhand (80%) marginally outperforms Chhattisgarh (79%). This anomaly confirms that healthcare infrastructure possesses inherent visibility that partially compensates for weaker communication. However, even healthcare shows channel effects: verbal communication generates 85% agreement regardless of state. The pattern across all nine indicators demonstrates that communication effectiveness is not ancillary to CSR but constitutive of it—how CSR is communicated determines whether social investment translates into perceived betterment.
The summary box captures the central policy implication. The disparities are not attributable to differential expenditure (both states show comparable per-capita CSR investment) but to systematic differences in communication architecture. Chhattisgarh's verbal-dominant, community-embedded approach produces superior outcomes across nearly every dimension. Jharkhand's print-dominant, institutionally-mediated approach, while compliant with disclosure requirements, fails to generate equivalent community awareness, trust, or impact perception. The evidence supports reorienting CSR governance toward communication accountability alongside financial compliance.
Local Language Usage
The use of local language in CSR communication is critical for ensuring comprehension, particularly in areas where beneficiaries may not be fluent in Hindi or English. The study examined whether CSR communication was provided in local languages understood by beneficiaries.
  Communication Channels
The choice of communication channels significantly influences the reach and effectiveness of CSR communication. The study examined the primary channels through which beneficiaries received information about CSR initiatives.

Figure 65 Primary CSR Communication Channels by State
 Interpretation. Chhattisgarh relies on verbal-dominant communication (58% via meetings and outreach), while Jharkhand is print-dominant (35% via pamphlets), creating a 26-point gap. This difference drives the study's key findings: verbal channels enable dialogue, feedback, and trust, whereas print channels are one-way and literacy-dependent.
The channel split reflects opposing philosophies—Chhattisgarh sees beneficiaries as active participants; Jharkhand as passive recipients. Electronic media use is modest (18–22%), and social media is negligible (4–11%), confirming that digital platforms cannot replace face-to-face engagement in rural and tribal areas.
Thus, to be effective in tribal and rural regions, investment must prioritize verbal, community-embedded channels over mediated, one-directional formats. Jharkhand's print-dominant approach, while compliant with disclosure requirements, fails to generate the awareness, clarity, and trust necessary for beneficiaries to recognize and engage with CSR initiatives. Rebalancing channel strategy toward verbal communication—training field staff, organizing regular village meetings, integrating with existing community structures—would likely close the outcome gaps documented across education, environment, and livelihood sectors.


Communication Effectiveness Score
A composite Communication Effectiveness Score (CES) was calculated for each respondent based on their responses to questions about awareness, clarity, language, and satisfaction with CSR communication. The score ranges from 0 to 4, with higher scores indicating more effective communication.
 
Figure 66 Communication Effectiveness Score (CES) — Chhattisgarh vs Jharkhand
Interpretation. This figure presents the most foundational statistical finding of the study: the Communication Effectiveness Score (CES) differential between Chhattisgarh (M = 2.67, SD = 0.52) and Jharkhand (M = 1.89, SD = 0.71). The 0.78-point difference, while appearing modest on a 0-4 scale, represents a Cohen's d of 1.24—a very large effect size in social science research. The independent samples t-test yields t(398) = 12.41, p < 0.001, confirming statistical significance at the highest confidence level.
The CES is a composite measure aggregating beneficiary responses on three communication dimensions: clarity of information (Q23), use of local language (Q24), and primary communication channel (Q25, weighted by effectiveness ranking). This composite approach captures communication effectiveness holistically rather than through any single metric. The large effect size indicates that the communication gap between states is not merely statistically detectable but practically substantial—a difference that meaningfully affects community outcomes.
The standard deviations reveal another important pattern: Jharkhand (SD = 0.71) shows greater variability than Chhattisgarh (SD = 0.52), suggesting that communication experiences in Jharkhand are less consistent across districts, communities, and beneficiaries. This variability reflects the fragmented, institutionally-mediated communication architecture in Jharkhand compared to the systematic, community-embedded approach in Chhattisgarh. The CES difference forms the explanatory foundation for all subsequent outcome disparities documented in this study.

PART B - QUALITATIVE DATA ANALYSIS
Introduction to the Qualitative Analysis
The qualitative component of this study constitutes a critical dimension of its mixed-methods architecture. While the quantitative strand, presented in Section A, established statistically significant patterns in CSR communication effectiveness across Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand, the qualitative strand interrogates the processes, meanings, and contextual factors that underlie those patterns. This section presents findings from forty in-depth semi-structured interviews conducted with key stakeholders directly engaged in CSR implementation and its communication across both states during the study period of FY 2018–19 to FY 2022–23. 
It is essential to restate the disciplinary orientation of this research at the outset. This study does not evaluate CSR interventions as development projects per se; rather, it investigates how CSR activities are communicated to communities, how that communication is received and interpreted, and how it contributes to social development outcomes. CSR communication is treated here as a distinct, analytically separable process that mediates between corporate intent and community benefit. The qualitative analysis is therefore attentive to the communicative dimensions of every stakeholder account: how information flows, through which channels, in what language, with what degree of participation, and with what observable effect.
The analysis proceeds through a structured coding and thematic synthesis procedure, drawing on the NVivo-informed framework of manual thematic coding. Four primary thematic domains — Education, Health, Livelihood, and Environment — anchor the analytical structure, corresponding to the sector-specific dimensions examined in the quantitative analysis. These are enriched by cross-cutting sub-themes including communication channels, transparency, community participation, and state-level variation. The chapter concludes with a comparative discussion that situates qualitative findings within the broader context of the research objectives.
Sampling Description and Interview Profile
Purposive sampling was employed to identify informants who possessed direct, substantive knowledge of CSR communication processes in the study areas. The target sample of forty interviews was distributed equally between Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand, with twenty interviews conducted per state. Within each state, the sample was further stratified across five districts in a pre-determined chronological order, ensuring systematic geographical coverage of both urban-industrial centres and remote tribal areas.
The district-wise sequence for Chhattisgarh was: Raipur District, Dhamtari District, Balodabazar District, Kanker District, and Bastar District. For Jharkhand, the sequence followed was: Ranchi District, East Singhbhum District, West Singhbhum District, Hazaribagh District, and Ramgarh District. This ordering reflects a movement from more urbanised, commercially active districts towards more remote, tribal-majority areas, allowing the analysis to capture the full range of CSR communication contexts.
Stakeholder categories included: Corporate CSR Heads, NGO and Implementing Agency Representatives, CSR Field Coordinators, Village-Level Beneficiaries, Community Leaders, and Local Administrative Representatives. The selection of individual interviewees prioritised those with the longest direct engagement with the CSR programme under study, and those capable of providing substantive commentary on communication practices rather than merely on project outputs.
In cases where the interview pool exceeded the required sample for a given district, interviews were filtered for inclusion on the basis of informational richness, thematic relevance to CSR communication, and stakeholder diversity. Community-level interviews were weighted in selection to ensure that the beneficiary voice — most at risk of marginalisation in institutional accounts — is adequately represented. All personal names have been replaced with anonymous role-based identifiers throughout the analysis, in conformity with the ethical protocol of this research.
To provide a structured overview of the qualitative sample composition, the below table presents the district-wise distribution of interviews across both states, stakeholder categories, CSR sector focus areas, and interview counts. The table demonstrates the geographical spread and stakeholder diversity incorporated into the purposive sampling design.

Interpretation. The qualitative sample comprises 40 in-depth interviews distributed equally across Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand (20 each). Within each state, interviews were stratified across five districts, moving from urban-industrial centers to remote tribal areas. Chhattisgarh districts included Raipur, Dhamtari, Balodabazar, Kanker, and Bastar. Jharkhand districts included Ranchi, East Singhbhum, West Singhbhum, Hazaribagh, and Ramgarh.
Stakeholder categories ensured diverse perspectives: CSR Heads, Implementing Agency Representatives (NGOs), Government Officials, Community Leaders, and direct Beneficiaries. Dhamtari (CG) had the highest number of interviews (6) due to the extensive HDFC Parivartan livelihood project requiring multiple stakeholder levels. West Singhbhum (JH) had the lowest (1) due to limited CSR activity and accessibility constraints.
The purposive sampling strategy prioritized informants with the longest direct engagement with CSR programs and those capable of providing substantive commentary on communication practices. Community-level interviews were weighted to ensure beneficiary voices—most at risk of marginalization—are adequately represented. All personal names have been replaced with anonymous role-based identifiers.
Interview Methodology and Coding Procedure
All forty interviews were conducted by the researcher (Sayed Amir Mustafa Hashmi) in the field, using a semi-structured interview guide developed in alignment with the study's research objectives. Interviews were conducted in Hindi, Chhattisgarhi, and in some cases with the assistance of a local interpreter for Gondi-speaking communities in Bastar. Responses were recorded with participant consent and subsequently transcribed in full. Transcripts were then rendered into English for analytical purposes.
The coding procedure followed a systematic qualitative analysis approach consistent with NVivo-based thematic analysis. In the first phase, data familiarisation was achieved through multiple close readings of all forty transcripts. In the second phase, initial codes were generated inductively from the data, without reference to pre-existing theoretical frameworks, in keeping with the instruction to conduct straightforward data-driven coding rather than theoretically-oriented interpretation. In the third phase, codes were organised into themes and sub-themes corresponding to the four mandated sectoral domains: Education, Health, Livelihood, and Environment.
The coding procedure identified recurring patterns across stakeholder responses, distinguishing between descriptions of CSR activities (what was done) and accounts of CSR communication (how it was communicated, through which channels, with what clarity, and with what response from communities). The distinction between CSR implementation and CSR communication was applied rigorously throughout the coding process. Codes related to communication channels, language appropriateness, information clarity, community participation, and trust were treated as primary analytical nodes.

Figure 68 Thematic Coding Framework Applied Across All Forty Interviews

Interpretation. The thematic coding framework was organized around four primary sectoral domains corresponding to the quantitative analysis: Education, Health, Livelihood, and Environment. Within each domain, sub-themes were derived inductively from interview transcripts, focusing specifically on communication processes rather than program implementation. Education generated four sub-themes (School Infrastructure, Smart Classrooms, Scholarship Communication, Enrolment & Retention); Health generated four (Medical Camps, Cancer Care, Maternal & Child Health, Preventive Health); Livelihood generated four (Agricultural Training, Market Linkage, Women's SHG, Skill Development); Environment generated three (Water Conservation, Solar Energy, Sanitation).
Four cross-cutting themes were identified that apply across all sectoral domains: Communication Channels (distinguishing verbal, print, electronic, and social media), Language and Cultural Sensitivity (use of local languages and culturally appropriate messaging), Transparency and Feedback (whether communities could ask questions and receive responses), and Community Participation (degree of community involvement in planning and implementation). These cross-cutting themes enabled comparative analysis between states.
The coding procedure followed NVivo-informed thematic analysis: first-phase data familiarization through multiple readings of all forty transcripts; second-phase inductive coding without pre-existing frameworks; third-phase organization into themes and sub-themes. The distinction between CSR implementation (what was done) and CSR communication (how it was conveyed) was applied rigorously throughout, ensuring that codes captured communicative dimensions rather than program outputs.
 RESULTS: THE THEMATIC FINDINGS
Theme 1: Education
Education emerged as the most consistently discussed thematic domain across all ten districts, reflecting the primacy of educational CSR expenditure observed in the quantitative data. Stakeholders across both states described substantial investments in school infrastructure, digital learning resources, library development, and scholarship programmes. Critically, the qualitative data makes apparent that the effectiveness of educational CSR was closely correlated with how well these interventions were communicated to communities prior to implementation, during execution, and in terms of ongoing visibility.
Chhattisgarh: Balodabazar District
The most detailed and analytically rich account of education-focused CSR communication in Chhattisgarh was provided by a CSR Head from the Balodabazar District, representing a cement company with a large operational footprint in the area. This interviewee described a structured school development programme spanning fifty government schools, encompassing BALA painting, science laboratories, smart classrooms with projectors and invertors, libraries, WASH facilities, and drinking water infrastructure. What distinguished this programme in communicative terms was the systematic baseline assessment conducted at each school prior to intervention, which was followed by gap-identification and community communication before work commenced.
“This was not here before. Our school was very backward. There was nothing to do. Earlier we did not have clean drinking water. The condition of the school was very bad. But this company brought waves of happiness to these dull faces.”  — CSR Head – Chhattisgarh
Community leaders in Balodabazar confirmed that digital learning infrastructure specifically motivated renewed student engagement. Multiple community respondents noted that smart classroom technology had transformed the experience of attending school, with students expressing interest in subjects they had previously found inaccessible. Significantly, community voices confirmed awareness of the CSR origin of these improvements, suggesting that attribution communication was successfully executed in this district.
“Since smart class came, with big pictures and zooming, it was explained well, so we have benefited a lot. Earlier, when we used to study, we didn’t feel like it that much.”  — Community Leader – Balodabazar, Chhattisgarh
Chhattisgarh: Raipur District
In Raipur, educational CSR communication centred on library services, scholarship programmes, and school infrastructure. Community leaders described the establishment of a public library in the city centre, scholarship disbursements to economically disadvantaged students, and distribution of stationery across schools in Chhattisgarh and Madhya Pradesh. These activities were communicated through a combination of direct community engagement by the implementing company and word-of-mouth diffusion within residential areas. The scholarship programme was particularly noted for its broad reach and clear communication of eligibility criteria.
“My daughter received a scholarship from this company. She can now continue her education without financial burden. Many poor students from our village who want quality education get scholarships every year.”  — Community Leader – Raipur, Chhattisgarh
Jharkhand: Hazaribagh District
In Hazaribagh, the District Collector provided a comprehensive account of educational interventions supported by a major industrial group. The government official highlighted renovation of government schools, establishment of smart classrooms, construction of girl-friendly sanitation facilities, provision of vocational training for older students, and identification and re-integration of school dropouts through bridge courses. From a communication perspective, the District Collector's active involvement in CSR planning and evaluation created a three-tier communication architecture — corporate, government, and community — that appeared to enhance both the reach and the credibility of educational messaging.
“School dropouts have been identified and brought back to mainstream education through bridge courses. Libraries have been established and stocked with books. For girl students, they have provided separate toilet facilities which has helped in improving attendance.”  — Government Official – Hazaribagh, Jharkhand

Figure 69 Education — Thematic Reference Frequency from Qualitative Interviews (n=40)
 Interpretation. The radar chart reveals that education-related CSR communication was discussed more frequently in Chhattisgarh (total 30 references) than in Jharkhand (total 24 references). The largest state disparities appear in School Infrastructure (CG: 8, JH: 6) and Smart Classrooms (CG: 7, JH: 5), indicating that Chhattisgarh's educational CSR places greater emphasis on physical infrastructure and digital learning resources. Scholarship communication shows the most dramatic gap (CG: 5, JH: 2), suggesting that Jharkhand beneficiaries are less aware of financial assistance programs.
In Jharkhand, the radar pattern is more balanced across sub-themes, with Enrolment & Retention (JH: 4, CG: 3) and Vocational Training (JH: 4, CG: 3) showing slightly higher frequency. This suggests that Jharkhand's educational CSR communication focuses more on bringing children into schools and skill development, while Chhattisgarh emphasizes infrastructure and technology. The qualitative data confirms that Chhattisgarh's community meetings explicitly discuss school upgrades, whereas Jharkhand's print materials focus on enrollment drives.
The overall shape difference is significant. Chhattisgarh's polygon is larger and skewed toward infrastructure sub-themes; Jharkhand's polygon is smaller and more evenly distributed. This pattern aligns with the quantitative finding that Chhattisgarh (82%) outperforms Jharkhand (74%) in perceived educational improvement. The infrastructure focus in Chhattisgarh—combined with verbal communication—creates visible, attributable outcomes. Jharkhand's broader but shallower approach may dilute the impact of individual interventions.
 Theme 2: Health
Healthcare communication constituted the second major thematic cluster, and the data revealed a more balanced pattern across the two states compared to education. This finding aligns with the quantitative result showing near-parity in healthcare access perception between Chhattisgarh (79%) and Jharkhand (80%). The qualitative evidence suggests that healthcare CSR is distinguished by its typically high visibility — through medical camps, ambulances, hospital infrastructure, and cancer care facilities — which partly compensates for weaker communication planning in certain districts.
Jharkhand: East Singhbhum District
The most substantively rich health-related interview was conducted with a hospital medical superintendent at a specialised cancer hospital in Tatanagar, East Singhbhum. This hospital, established as a CSR legacy institution by a major industrial group, provides comprehensive oncological care to patients from Jharkhand, Bihar, Odisha, and West Bengal. The medical superintendent described an elaborate communication architecture: outreach camps in rural and tribal areas, a mobile medical unit for awareness and early detection, training of ASHA workers to identify symptoms, telemedicine links planned with district hospitals, and a community health worker training programme.
“About 60% of our patients come from below the poverty line. The fact that we can provide them with world-class cancer care without pushing them into bankruptcy is what makes our work meaningful.”  — Hospital Administrator – East Singhbhum, Jharkhand
This interviewee's account illustrates an important dimension of health CSR communication: the hospital's not-for-profit ethos and its subsidy structures were actively communicated to prospective patients through social workers, community health workers, and media. The communication of affordability and accessibility — rather than solely clinical excellence — was central to how the institution positioned itself vis-à-vis its target beneficiary population.
Chhattisgarh: Raipur District
In Raipur, health-related CSR was described by community leaders in terms of ambulance services, mega medical camps, and subsidised operations for underprivileged patients. One community leader described how a company-donated ambulance to a local hospital had fundamentally altered emergency response capacity. Another account described a mega medical camp at which thousands of community members received free check-ups, with information about the camp disseminated through direct community visits by company representatives.
“They also run an ambulance service for various villages. When critical medical care is needed, they sponsor operations for underprivileged people. This has saved many lives in our community.”  — Community Leader – Raipur, Chhattisgarh
Jharkhand: Hazaribagh District
The District Collector of Hazaribagh provided a multi-sector account of health-related CSR, with particular emphasis on the upgrading of primary health centres and community health centres in mining-affected areas, provision of ambulances for remote villages, regular specialised health camps, and renovation of anganwadi centres with improved nutrition supplies. The official's framing of healthcare communication was notable: government-corporate partnership was presented not merely as a coordination mechanism but as a joint communicative strategy in which both parties actively informed communities about available services.
“Several community health centres and primary health centres in mining-affected areas have been renovated and equipped with modern equipment. They have provided ambulances for remote areas so that patients can reach hospitals in time.”  — Government Official – Hazaribagh, Jharkhand

 Interpretation. The radar chart reveals a striking divergence in health-related CSR communication between the two states. Chhattisgarh's references are concentrated heavily on Medical Camp Communication (CG: 7, JH: 5), reflecting its community-based health outreach model where village meetings announce and discuss medical camps. Jharkhand, conversely, shows exceptional emphasis on Cancer/Specialist Care (JH: 6, CG: 1), driven by the Tata Steel Foundation's cancer hospital in Jamshedpur, which dominates health discourse in East Singhbhum district. This single-institution effect fundamentally shapes Jharkhand's health communication landscape.
Maternal and Child Health shows moderate frequency in both states (CG: 2, JH: 4), with Jharkhand's higher score reflecting ASHA worker-led immunization drives. Ambulance Services (CG: 4, JH: 3) and Preventive Health/WASH (CG: 3, JH: 4) show near-parity, suggesting these basic health services are communicated similarly across states. Notably, Chhattisgarh's radar shape is asymmetrical, dominated by one sub-theme; Jharkhand's shape is more balanced, reflecting a diversified health communication portfolio.
The contrasting patterns explain the quantitative finding of healthcare near-parity (79% CG vs 80% JH). Chhattisgarh's community-based medical camp communication effectively reaches rural populations, but Jharkhand's specialist cancer care creates high-visibility impact that compensates for weaker general health communication. However, Jharkhand's reliance on institutional channels for cancer care may not translate to other health domains. The radar reveals that neither state has an optimally balanced health communication strategy.

Theme 3: Livelihood
Livelihood-oriented CSR generated the highest volume of thematically rich interview data across all ten districts, and it was in this domain that the qualitative analysis revealed the most analytically significant differences in communication approaches between the two states. Livelihood CSR in this study encompasses agricultural development, skill-based training, market linkage facilitation, women’s self-help group organisation, and income diversification through activities such as fish farming, poultry, mushroom cultivation, and tamarind processing. The communicative challenge in this domain is considerably greater than in education or health, because the intended beneficiary must not only be informed of the intervention but must also be persuaded to change existing productive behaviour.
Chhattisgarh: Dhamtari District
The qualitative data from Dhamtari was the most extensive in the study, encompassing interviews with implementing agency professionals, field coordinators, agricultural entrepreneurs, and community beneficiaries engaged in vegetable farming, fish farming, lift irrigation, and women’s empowerment programmes. These interviews, conducted with stakeholders from HDFC Bank’s Parivartan project implemented through a major NGO, consistently emphasised that CSR communication in Dhamtari was primarily face-to-face, community-embedded, and ongoing rather than episodic.
A State Coordinator from the implementing agency described the fundamental challenge of motivating tribal farmers to transition from subsistence to market-oriented agriculture, and identified consistent village-level engagement meetings, collective planning sessions, and capacity-building demonstrations as the primary communication instruments. The organising of women into producer groups was explicitly framed as a communication strategy: it created institutionalised channels through which information could be delivered and feedback received on a structured basis.
“We organised women and farmers at the village level. We will train them to practice farming that aligns with market demands, enabling them to produce according to local needs. Our goal is to collectively make agriculture a profitable activity.”  — Implementing Agency Coordinator – Dhamtari, Chhattisgarh
Community voices from Dhamtari confirmed the communicative significance of this approach. Multiple beneficiaries described the moment at which a village meeting changed their agricultural practice, noting that the group forum created both information access and social accountability. Beneficiaries working in fish farming, vegetable cultivation, and lift irrigation all described the formation of group structures as integral to how they received, processed, and acted upon CSR communication.
“We formed a group, and all the women together created a green pit in a planned manner. This has given us good and healthy compost, and we all work together.”  — Community Leader – Dhamtari, Chhattisgarh
Chhattisgarh: Kanker and Bastar Districts
In Kanker and Bastar, livelihood CSR communication involved agricultural cluster development, tamarind processing, and agri-entrepreneurship facilitation, implemented through NGO partnerships supported by HDFC Bank and other CSR funders. An implementing agency representative in Kanker described the creation of Agriculture Production Clusters as a structural communication mechanism: by bringing together farmers from multiple villages into a single productive group, the cluster created a standing forum for information exchange, collective decision-making, and accountability. The representative noted that modern techniques including trellising systems, drip irrigation, and multi-layer farming were communicated through in-field demonstrations rather than printed materials.
“We brought together male and female farmers through the Agriculture Production Cluster and held detailed discussions with them. During the meeting, we collectively selected certain crops and ensured complete support throughout the farming process.”  — Implementing Agency Coordinator – Kanker, Chhattisgarh
In Bastar, a field coordinator described the specific challenge of introducing tamarind processing machinery to a community of tribal women, and the multi-stage communication process required: initial awareness meetings to explain the concept, demonstration sessions on machine operation, training on quality grading standards, and ongoing market linkage communication. The coordinator emphasised that communication cost was itself a project component that had been budgeted for, rather than an afterthought.
Jharkhand: Ranchi District
The Ranchi district interviews, conducted with implementing agency representatives of a major livelihood-focused NGO funded through Ramkrishna Forgings Limited, presented a philosophically richer account of CSR communication than any other district in the study. Multiple representatives of this organisation articulated a vision of livelihood CSR as fundamentally about enhancing the communicative and decision-making capacity of communities, rather than delivering services to passive recipients. This framing — in which ‘development is a complex process requiring the best and brightest minds to engage with vulnerable communities’ — positioned communication as the core activity, not an auxiliary one.
“Resources are not a challenge. To my mind, the biggest challenge today in India is ideas — ideas that work at a scale which can solve problems.”  — Implementing Agency Representative – Ranchi, Jharkhand
“Each professional, when he engages with the community, identifies the challenges there, understands the context, and designs the engagement with that set of community that he works with. It is not that he is following a blueprint given to him.”  — Implementing Agency Representative – Ranchi, Jharkhand
Jharkhand: Ramgarh District
In Ramgarh, the Walmart-supported livelihood projects demonstrated a particularly significant dimension of livelihood CSR communication: the deliberate redefinition of women’s identity as farmers. Implementing agency representatives described how communication was designed not merely to inform women of training opportunities, but to reshape the social category through which they understood their own agricultural labour. Community leader testimonials confirmed that this communicative reframing had tangible effects: women who had previously not identified publicly as farmers were asserting that identity in community spaces, with positive consequences for their economic agency and family status.
“And here if we see our village women — earlier when talking about farmers, only a man would be referred to. Today, a woman can also call herself a farmer. Today, when given the name of farmer, she herself steps forward and says I am a farmer.”  — Community Leader – Ramgarh, Jharkhand

Figure 71 Livelihood — Thematic Reference Frequency from Qualitative Interviews (n=40) Interpretation. The radar chart reveals that livelihood-related CSR communication generated the highest volume of qualitative references among all sectors (CG total 40, JH total 36). Chhattisgarh shows overwhelming emphasis on Agricultural Training (CG: 12, JH: 7), reflecting the Dhamtari district's extensive Agriculture Production Cluster program where village-level demonstrations and field trainings dominate communication. This single sub-theme accounts for nearly one-third of Chhattisgarh's livelihood references, indicating a concentrated, systematic approach to agricultural extension communication.
Jharkhand shows a distinct pattern with Women's Self-Help Groups (JH: 9, CG: 8) as the dominant sub-theme, followed by Market Linkage (JH: 7, CG: 6) and Skill Development (JH: 6, CG: 5). This reflects Jharkhand's focus on livelihood programs that emphasize collective enterprise and market access, particularly the NGO-implemented Walmart project in Ramgarh district. The gender dimension is stronger in Jharkhand's livelihood communication, with women's farmer identity explicitly reframed through participatory messaging.
The shape difference is analytically significant. Chhattisgarh's radar is lopsided, dominated by Agricultural Training, suggesting a specialized but narrow livelihood communication strategy. Jharkhand's radar is more balanced, indicating diversified livelihood messaging across multiple sub-themes. However, Chhattisgarh's concentrated approach may explain its higher perceived livelihood impact (66% vs 52%), as deep, sustained communication on a few key interventions may be more effective than broad but shallow messaging on many topics.

Theme 4: Environment
Environmental CSR communication was the least frequently cited thematic domain in the qualitative data, consistent with the quantitative finding that environmental CSR received the lowest overall recognition (66%) and showed the greatest state-level disparity (Chhattisgarh 72%, Jharkhand 60%). The qualitative analysis reveals that this gap is partly explained by the structural characteristics of environmental communication: unlike education or health, where visible physical outputs facilitate stakeholder recognition, environmental interventions such as watershed management, groundwater recharge, and solar energy installation are often diffuse in their effects and require more sustained communication to generate community awareness.
Chhattisgarh: Dhamtari District – Watershed Management
The most substantive environmental CSR communication data came from Dhamtari, where a watershed management project funded by Axis Bank, Yes Bank, and Ford Foundation had been implemented across the Magarlod and Kurud blocks. Implementing agency representatives described the construction of 213 low-cost bunding structures, 132 new farm ponds, and 54 new ponds across seventy gram panchayats, within a ‘ridge to valley’ watershed management framework. The communication of this project to village communities was described as a multi-stage process: awareness campaigns to explain the watershed concept, capacity building of Panchayati Raj institution members, and formation of village-level water management groups.
“By running a campaign of alertness, continuous awareness, and training, we connected the villagers to the High Impact Mega Watershed project. The capacity of frontline workers, people associated with Panchayati Raj institutions, and people associated with group and circle level organisations at the village level was developed.”  — Implementing Agency Coordinator – Dhamtari, Chhattisgarh
A Government Official from Magarlod block confirmed that the integration of Panchayati Raj institutions into the communication architecture had been critical to community acceptance of the project. The representative described how gram sabha-level discussions had been used to explain the ridge-to-valley concept, and how the visual mapping of water flows through village territory had made an abstract environmental concept tangible and comprehensible to non-specialist audiences.
Chhattisgarh: Dhamtari District – Solar Irrigation
A team coordinator from the implementing agency in Nagri block, Dhamtari, described an additional environmental dimension: the installation of 416 solar lights across fifty villages, supported by HDFC Bank. The communication significance of this intervention was highlighted in the coordinator’s account, which noted that solar lighting had unlocked new communication possibilities by enabling women’s groups to hold meetings in the evenings in previously dark villages. In this analysis, the solar energy installation functioned simultaneously as an environmental intervention and as a communication infrastructure that extended the reach of all other CSR communication activities.
“In collaboration with HDFC, we have installed 416 lights in all 50 villages. The biggest benefit of this is that the women can now sit and conduct meetings in the evenings. Even in those interior areas which are considered vulnerable, light has now reached — this is a great improvement.”  — Implementing Agency Coordinator – Dhamtari, Chhattisgarh
Jharkhand: Hazaribagh District
In Hazaribagh, the District Collector described water conservation structures including check dams and farm ponds as a component of environmental CSR communication, noting that these interventions were particularly important in water-scarce tribal areas. Street lighting was described as serving both an environmental and a safety communication function, by reducing women’s and children’s exposure to risk in after-dark movement. However, the District Collector’s account also revealed a communication gap: while infrastructure was delivered, formal communication campaigns explaining the environmental rationale were less systematically organised than in Chhattisgarh’s watershed programme.

Figure 72 Environment — Thematic Reference Frequency from Qualitative Interviews (n=40)
 Interpretation. The radar chart reveals that environmental CSR communication received the lowest overall frequency among all four sectors, consistent with quantitative findings showing environmental impact perception at 66% (lowest among sectors). Chhattisgarh substantially outperforms Jharkhand across all five sub-themes, with total references of 26 compared to 15. The largest gap appears in Watershed & Water Conservation (CG: 8, JH: 4), reflecting Chhattisgarh's extensive watershed management project in Dhamtari, where gram sabha meetings and visual mapping of water flows made abstract environmental concepts tangible to communities.
Chhattisgarh also shows stronger emphasis on Farm Ponds & Irrigation (CG: 6, JH: 3) and Solar Energy (CG: 5, JH: 2), indicating a multi-pronged environmental communication strategy. The solar lighting intervention in Dhamtari was explicitly framed by implementing agencies as both an environmental initiative and a communication enabler—allowing women's groups to hold evening meetings. Jharkhand's radar is notably smaller and flatter, with only Sanitation/ODF showing parity (CG: 4, JH: 4), likely due to government-led sanitation campaigns rather than CSR-specific communication.
The pattern explains the 12-percentage-point gap in environmental impact perception (CG 72%, JH 60%). Environmental CSR communication requires sustained, participatory dialogue to make long-term, diffuse benefits visible to communities. Chhattisgarh's integration of environmental messaging into existing community governance structures (gram sabhas, water committees) enables this sustained engagement. Jharkhand's institutional, print-based communication fails to build the community understanding necessary for recognizing environmental outcomes, resulting in significantly lower impact perception. Comparative Discussion: Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand
The preceding quantitative analysis established statistically significant differences in CSR communication effectiveness between Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand. However, numbers alone cannot fully explain why these disparities exist. This section moves beyond numerical patterns to explore the underlying qualitative dynamics, drawing directly from the voices and experiences of beneficiaries, field workers, and implementing agency representatives.
Based on 40 in-depth interviews conducted across both states, the comparative discussion examines five analytically salient dimensions: communication channel preference, the role of implementing agencies, community participation structures, language and cultural adaptation, and the visibility and attribution of CSR activities. Together, these five dimensions provide a comprehensive framework for understanding why Chhattisgarh's community-embedded approach generates higher perceived impact than Jharkhand's compliance-driven model.
The combined radar graph presented below aggregates thematic references across four sectors—Livelihood, Education, Environment, and Health. Livelihood emerges as the most discussed sector in both states, reflecting the intensive engagement required for behavior-change interventions. The shape and size differences between the two states provide visual confirmation of Chhattisgarh's systematic communication advantage, as interpreted in the following analysis.

 

Figure 73 Combined Qualitative Analysis — Total Thematic References by Sector (n=40 interviews)
 Interpretation. The combined radar graph reveals that Livelihood generated the highest volume of qualitative references in both states (CG: 40, JH: 36), confirming that livelihood-oriented CSR requires the most sustained community engagement and communication. This sector's dominance across both states reflects the intensive, ongoing dialogue necessary for agricultural training, self-help groups, and market linkages—interventions that require behavior change rather than one-time service delivery. Chhattisgarh's higher livelihood score aligns with its verbal-dominant communication architecture.
Education shows the second-highest frequency (CG: 30, JH: 24), followed by Environment (CG: 26, JH: 15) and Health (CG: 19, JH: 20). Notably, Health is the only sector where Jharkhand (20) slightly exceeds Chhattisgarh (19), consistent with the quantitative finding of near-parity in healthcare impact perception. This reflects Jharkhand's specialist cancer care discourse in East Singhbhum, which generated substantial qualitative references despite limited general health communication.
The shape difference is analytically significant. Chhattisgarh's radar is larger across three of four sectors, with Environment showing the widest gap (11 points). This explains the quantitative pattern where environmental impact perception (72% vs 60%) shows the largest state disparity after livelihood. The combined radar demonstrates that Chhattisgarh's qualitative communication advantage is systematic rather than sector-specific—its community-embedded, verbal approach generates more discussion across all developmental domains, translating into higher perceived impact.

Communication Channel Preference
Across all four thematic domains, Chhattisgarh’s CSR communication was distinguished by its systematic preference for interpersonal, face-to-face communication channels. Village meetings, Agricultural Production Cluster forums, women’s group sessions, and field-level demonstrations were the dominant instruments through which information was conveyed. These channels are characterised by high interactivity, immediate feedback mechanisms, the possibility of local language use, and strong social accountability dynamics. When a group of farmers collectively discusses a new farming technique in a village meeting, the communicative transaction is simultaneously informational, motivational, and socially binding.
In Jharkhand, the qualitative data reveals a more varied communication channel landscape. While implementing agencies in Ranchi and Ramgarh also employed face-to-face communication, the accounts from East Singhbhum and Hazaribagh suggest a greater reliance on institutional channels — hospitals, government departments, and print media — rather than direct community-embedded dialogue. This finding is consistent with the quantitative data showing lower verbal channel use (32%) and higher print media reliance (35%) in Jharkhand compared to Chhattisgarh (58% verbal).
Role of Implementing Agencies as Communication Mediators
A central finding of the qualitative analysis is the communicative role played by NGO implementing agencies in both states, and the differential quality of this mediation between Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand. In Chhattisgarh, particularly in Dhamtari, Kanker, and Bastar, implementing agencies were described as the primary communication architects: they organised village meetings, trained community-level communicators, facilitated group formation, and created structured feedback channels. The implementing agency’s role was not limited to programme delivery but encompassed the full communicative lifecycle of a CSR intervention.
In Jharkhand, while implementing agencies in Ranchi articulated a sophisticated philosophy of community engagement, the practical depth of community-level communication described was less consistently rich. Several accounts from East Singhbhum and West Singhbhum described CSR communication as largely institution-initiated and one-directional: the hospital communicated to patients; the company communicated through branding; the government communicated through official channels. The dialogic quality of communication — in which community members actively participated in shaping the information they received — was more frequently described in Chhattisgarh than in Jharkhand.
Community Participation in Communication
The qualitative data consistently shows that CSR communication in Chhattisgarh incorporated participatory mechanisms that positioned community members as active agents in the communication process rather than passive recipients of information. The formation of water management committees, agricultural producer groups, women’s self-help groups, and village planning committees all served communicative functions in addition to their organisational purposes. These structures created institutionalised channels for ongoing dialogue between implementing agencies and communities, enabling two-way information flow, needs assessment, and adaptive communication.
This participatory communication architecture was less systematically described in Jharkhand, where accounts more frequently positioned community members as recipients of well-intentioned CSR services. Notably, however, the accounts from Ranchi and Ramgarh reveal genuine innovations in participatory communication: the identity-focused messaging around women’s farmer identity in Ramgarh, and the philosophy of citizen-centred development described by the Ranchi implementing agency, both represent sophisticated approaches to community participation that could serve as models for broader CSR communication practice in Jharkhand.
Language and Cultural Adaptation
Language emerged as a significant variable in the comparative analysis, consistent with the quantitative finding of a 26-percentage-point gap in local language communication between the two states. The qualitative data illuminates why this gap exists: Chhattisgarh’s implementing agencies operated with staff who were socially proximate to the communities they served — often drawn from the same linguistic and cultural background as beneficiaries. In Dhamtari, field coordinators conducted meetings in Chhattisgarhi and in local tribal dialects; in Kanker and Bastar, the presence of Gondi-speaking communities necessitated active language adaptation.
In Jharkhand, the greater linguistic diversity — encompassing Santali, Mundari, Kurukh, and Hindi across different districts — created a more complex communication environment. The qualitative data suggests that this complexity was not uniformly addressed: while the Ranchi and Ramgarh implementing agencies appeared linguistically competent and culturally sensitive, accounts from East Singhbhum and Hazaribagh were less specific about language adaptation strategies, suggesting that institutional communication channels may have predominated over vernacular dialogue in these districts.
Visibility, Branding, and Attribution
One dimension of CSR communication that showed interesting variation between the two states was the role of corporate branding and physical visibility in creating community awareness of CSR activities. In Chhattisgarh, particularly in Raipur, multiple community leaders explicitly referenced corporate branding — school buildings with company names, gardens with company logos, statues and beautification projects attributed to specific companies — as a significant communication mechanism. This ‘visibility as communication’ approach appeared to reinforce the face-to-face communication strategies, creating a built environment that continuously attributed development outcomes to specific corporate actors.
In Jharkhand, the East Singhbhum interviews also described prominent corporate branding by the dominant industrial group in that district — parks, roads, hospitals, and civic amenities all bearing visible corporate attribution. In West Singhbhum, a community leader described how the logo of a mining company displayed at a renovated cultural hall ‘reminds everyone of their contribution.’ However, in the more rural districts of Hazaribagh and Ramgarh, physical branding was less prominent, and the communication of corporate identity relied more heavily on implementing agency reputation than on visible corporate attribution.

Table 23 Comparative qualitative summary of CSR communication dimensions

  
Figure 74 Comparative Qualitative Summary — CSR Communication Dimensions Interpretation. The comparative analysis reveals systematic differences across seven dimensions of CSR communication. Chhattisgarh demonstrates a verbally dominant, face-to-face communication channel preference, while Jharkhand employs a mixed approach combining institutional, print, and verbal channels. Implementing agencies in Chhattisgarh function as primary communication architects, organizing village meetings and creating structured feedback channels; in Jharkhand, agencies focus more on programme delivery with some facilitation. Community participation in Chhattisgarh is institutionalized through structured forums such as Self-Help Groups and Agriculture Production Clusters; Jharkhand shows variable participation, stronger only in Ranchi and Ramgarh districts.
Language adaptation shows the most dramatic difference. Chhattisgarh achieves high local dialect use systemically, with field staff drawn from local communities; Jharkhand's greater linguistic diversity (Santhali, Mundari, Kurukh, Ho) is not uniformly addressed, resulting in moderate and district-variable adaptation. Corporate visibility and branding are high in both states in urban areas, but Chhattisgarh maintains prominent attributed infrastructure even in rural districts, while Jharkhand's branding is moderate outside urban centers.
Feedback mechanisms are institutionalized through group structures in Chhattisgarh, enabling ongoing two-way communication; Jharkhand's feedback systems are less systematically organized. Communication of attribution—the ability of beneficiaries to connect outcomes to specific corporate actors—is strong and clear in Chhattisgarh but variable and weaker in Jharkhand's remote districts. These seven dimensions collectively explain why Chhattisgarh's community-embedded approach generates higher perceived impact than Jharkhand's institutionally-mediated model.
Barriers in CSR Communication at the Village Level
The qualitative data identified several recurring barriers to effective CSR communication at the village level, with some barriers common to both states and others more pronounced in one state than the other.
Geographic and infrastructural barriers were cited across all districts. The remoteness of many beneficiary villages in Bastar, Kanker, Hazaribagh, and West Singhbhum created physical obstacles to regular communication visits. In Dhamtari, the implementing agency coordinator acknowledged that the construction of solar lighting was partly motivated by the need to enable evening meetings in villages that were otherwise dark, suggesting that communication infrastructure itself required investment. In Hazaribagh, the District Collector noted that several mining-affected villages were accessible only by unpaved roads that became impassable during the monsoon, limiting the frequency of in-person communication during part of the year.
Social and cultural barriers were more pronounced in Jharkhand. The interviews from East Singhbhum and Hazaribagh noted the role of traditional authority structures in mediating community access to information, sometimes creating hierarchical filters that prevented information from reaching the most marginalised sub-groups. In Chhattisgarh, the formation of women’s producer groups and water management committees partly addressed this barrier by creating parallel communication channels that bypassed traditional gatekeepers.
Linguistic barriers, as discussed in the comparative section, were more significant in Jharkhand than in Chhattisgarh. However, both states shared the challenge of communicating complex technical information — about watershed management, agricultural techniques, or medical procedures — to communities with limited formal education. The most successful communication strategies identified in the qualitative data addressed this by using visual aids, field demonstrations, and participatory exercises rather than text-based or verbal-only communication.
An additional barrier, identified specifically in accounts from Jharkhand, related to low initial trust in corporate entities. Several implementing agency representatives in Ranchi noted that communities in Jharkhand had long experience of industrial operations that had displaced communities, degraded environments, or failed to deliver promised benefits. Rebuilding trust sufficient to enable effective CSR communication in this context required sustained relationship investment over multiple years, rather than the campaign-style communication approaches that may be adequate in more trust-rich environments.
Integrated Interpretation of Qualitative Findings
The qualitative analysis of forty stakeholder interviews across ten districts of Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand yields a coherent and analytically robust account of how CSR communication functions in rural and tribal development contexts. Four integrated conclusions emerge from the thematic analysis and comparative discussion.
First, the quality of CSR communication is primarily determined by channel choice and community embeddedness. Across all four thematic domains and across both states, the most effective CSR communication was consistently characterised by face-to-face dialogue embedded within the social structures of beneficiary communities. The formation of agricultural producer groups, women’s self-help groups, and village-level water management committees served not merely as organisational mechanisms but as structured communication channels through which information could be delivered, questions could be asked, and feedback could be incorporated. Where these structures were systematically developed — as in Dhamtari and Kanker in Chhattisgarh, and in Ranchi and Ramgarh in Jharkhand — CSR communication was demonstrably more effective.
Second, NGO implementing agencies function as the critical communicative intermediary between corporate CSR intentions and community-level outcomes. The quality of this intermediation varies considerably, and the data suggests that agencies with deep community roots, local language competence, and genuine participatory commitments are significantly more effective communicators than agencies that function primarily as programme delivery mechanisms. The investment in implementing agency capacity and in the relationship between agencies and communities is therefore an investment in communication quality as much as in programme quality.
Third, the gender dimension of CSR communication is strategically significant and under-utilised in Jharkhand. The qualitative data from Chhattisgarh’s Dhamtari, Kanker, and Bastar districts, and from Jharkhand’s Ramgarh district, consistently showed that women’s group formation created the most effective communication channels for livelihood and health-related CSR. The communicative reframing of women’s identity as farmers — as documented in Ramgarh — represents an innovative approach to CSR communication that deserves wider recognition and adoption.
Fourth, the attribution gap identified in the quantitative data — wherein Jharkhand beneficiaries are less likely to accurately identify the CSR company responsible for interventions in their area — is explained qualitatively by the greater reliance on institutional communication channels and the lesser development of community-embedded communication structures. Where companies communicate primarily through hospitals, government channels, and print media, the community’s awareness of the corporate actor behind the intervention is structurally weaker than where companies communicate through face-to-face meetings and community engagement activities that explicitly connect the company name with the development outcome.
