STAKEHOLDER PERSPECTIVES ON CSR COMMUNICATION
The qualitative component of the study involved in-depth interviews with 40 key informants (20 from each state) to gain deeper insights into CSR communication practices, challenges, and best practices. Thematic analysis of interview transcripts revealed several key themes.
Theme 1: Importance of Community Engagement
Interview respondents emphasized the critical importance of community engagement in effective CSR communication. Community leaders and beneficiaries in both states highlighted that CSR initiatives are more successful when companies and implementing agencies engage with communities from the planning stage rather than simply informing them about pre-determined projects.
Quote from a community leader in Chhattisgarh: "When the company comes and asks us what we need, we feel respected. We tell them about our problems, and they design projects accordingly. This is much better than when they just come and tell us what they are going to do.
Theme 2: Language and Cultural Sensitivity
Respondents consistently highlighted the importance of using local languages and being culturally sensitive in CSR communication. In areas where tribal languages are predominant, communication in Hindi alone was found to be insufficient for ensuring comprehension and engagement.
Quote from a beneficiary in Jharkhand: "Many people in our village do not understand Hindi well. When the company people come and speak in our language, we understand better and can ask questions. This makes a big difference.
Theme 3: Role of Local Leaders
The findings confirmed the importance of local leaders and opinion makers in facilitating CSR communication. Respondents reported that they often first learn about CSR initiatives from community leaders, who serve as intermediaries between companies and beneficiaries.
Quote from an NGO representative in Chhattisgarh: "We work closely with the village head and other respected community members. They help us communicate with the people and build trust. Without their support, it is very difficult to implement any project successfully.
Theme 4: Challenges in CSR Communication
Respondents identified several challenges in CSR communication, including limited reach of communication channels, lack of feedback mechanisms, insufficient use of local languages, and limited engagement with marginalized groups. These challenges were found to be more pronounced in Jharkhand compared to Chhattisgarh.
Quote from a company official in Jharkhand: "We face challenges in reaching remote villages where infrastructure is poor. Sometimes our messages do not reach the intended beneficiaries, or they get distorted as they pass through multiple channels. We are working on improving our communication strategies.
Theme 5: Recommendations for Improvement
Respondents offered several recommendations for improving CSR communication, including: (1) increasing the use of local languages in all communication materials; (2) establishing regular community meetings for information sharing and feedback collection; (3) leveraging local media and community radio for wider reach; (4) involving community leaders more actively in communication processes; and (5) creating accessible grievance redressal mechanisms.
The qualitative strand of this research has generated a rich, contextualised account of CSR communication in Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand that both substantiates and nuances the patterns identified in the quantitative analysis. The analysis demonstrates that the communication advantage observed in Chhattisgarh is not primarily a function of greater CSR expenditure or more visible infrastructure, but rather a function of systematically superior communication architecture: face-to-face channels embedded in community social structures, NGO intermediaries with genuine participatory commitments, active language and cultural adaptation, and structured feedback mechanisms that make communities active participants in CSR communication rather than passive recipients.
Jharkhand’s CSR communication landscape is characterised by genuine innovations — particularly in the Ranchi and Ramgarh districts — but also by greater variability across districts and a structural tendency towards institutional, one-directional communication channels. The trust deficit created by historical patterns of industrial-community relations in Jharkhand represents an additional communicative challenge that requires sustained relationship investment before conventional communication strategies can be effective.
Across both states, the qualitative analysis confirms that livelihood-oriented CSR generates the most sustained community engagement precisely because it requires ongoing communication and adaptive facilitation, rather than the one-time information delivery that can characterise education or health infrastructure projects. The community voices captured in this analysis — from farmers in Dhamtari who discovered the economic potential of their backyard gardens, to women in Ramgarh who claimed their identity as farmers, to beneficiaries in Hazaribagh who were brought back into mainstream education — collectively affirm that CSR communication, when effectively executed, is a transformative social process with tangible developmental consequences.
 PART C - HYPOTHESIS TESTING
Following the descriptive analysis of beneficiary responses across Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand, this section applies inferential statistical tests to validate whether the observed differences in CSR communication and impact are statistically significant. The analysis addresses the five research hypotheses and examines the relationships between communication effectiveness, beneficiary awareness, and perceived societal betterment.
This section presents the results of hypothesis testing to examine the relationships between CSR communication effectiveness and perceived impact across different domains. Independent samples t-tests were used to compare means between Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand, and to examine the relationship between communication effectiveness and impact perception.
H No.	Statement	Test Used	Result	p-value	Decision
H₁	Significant difference in CSR communication effectiveness between CG and JH	Independent t-test	t(398) = 12.41, d = 1.24	< 0.001	SUPPORTED
H₂	CSR communication positively associated with educational development	Binary Logistic Regression (Nagelkerke R² = 0.31)	χ²(2) = 118.65	< 0.001	SUPPORTED
H₃	CSR communication influences healthcare awareness with interstate variation	Two-way ANOVA (State × Channel)	F(7,392) = 8.45; Interaction: F(3,389) = 5.14	0.002	PARTIALLY SUPPORTED
H₄	CSR communication related to environmental sustainability uptake	Chi-square test	χ²(3) = 24.88, Cramér's V = 0.25	< 0.001	SUPPORTED
H₅	CSR communication contributes to livelihood enhancement	Multiple Linear Regression (R² = 0.50)	F(2,397) = 397.65; β(CES) = 0.67	< 0.001	SUPPORTED
Table 25 Hypothesis Testing Results

 
Figure 77 Hypothesis Testing Results Summary
 Interpretation. H1: There is a significant difference in CSR communication effectiveness between Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand. The independent samples t-test yielded a t-value of 12.41 (df=398, p<0.001), indicating a statistically significant difference in communication effectiveness scores between the two states. The mean CES for Chhattisgarh (M=2.67, SD=0.52) was significantly higher than for Jharkhand (M=1.89, SD=0.71). This hypothesis is supported.
H2: There is a significant positive relationship between CSR communication effectiveness and the perceived impact of CSR initiatives on education. The t-test comparing beneficiaries with high and low CES on education impact perception yielded t=14.23 (df=398, p<0.001), indicating a strong positive relationship. This hypothesis is supported.
H3: There is a significant positive relationship between CSR communication effectiveness and the perceived impact of CSR initiatives on healthcare. The t-test yielded t=0.25 (df=398, p=0.802), which is not statistically significant. This suggests that healthcare impact perceptions are similar across different levels of communication effectiveness. This hypothesis is not supported.
H4: There is a significant positive relationship between CSR communication effectiveness and the perceived impact of CSR initiatives on environmental sustainability. The t-test yielded t=9.87 (df=398, p<0.001), indicating a significant positive relationship. This hypothesis is supported.
H5: There is a significant positive relationship between CSR communication effectiveness and the perceived impact of CSR initiatives on livelihood generation. The t-test yielded t=7.54 (df=398, p<0.001), indicating a significant positive relationship. This hypothesis is supported.
ANOVA Results
Company sector significantly shapes CSR outcomes independent of geographic context. This analysis reveals banking/finance sectors outperform industrial sectors in beneficiary satisfaction, suggesting participatory livelihood models generate stronger community engagement than physical infrastructure alone.
 
Table 26 ANOVA Results - Overall Satisfaction by Company Sector
Interpretation. This figure presents a sectoral analysis of beneficiary satisfaction, revealing that company sector significantly influences CSR outcomes independent of state. The Banking/Finance sector generates the highest satisfaction scores (CG: M = 2.60, JH: M = 2.30), followed by Metal, Energy, and Cement sectors in descending order. The one-way ANOVA confirms these differences are statistically significant (F(3, 396) = 12.33, p < 0.001, η² = 0.09), with post-hoc Tukey HSD tests showing Banking/Finance sector satisfaction significantly higher than all industrial sectors.
The finding is notable because Banking/Finance companies (represented in this study primarily by HDFC Bank and Axis Bank) operate through extensive implementing agency networks and focus heavily on livelihood and agricultural development, sectors where communication effectiveness most strongly predicts outcomes. Industrial sector companies (Metal, Energy, Cement) often focus on physical infrastructure—school buildings, hospitals, roads—which, while visible, may not generate the same level of sustained community engagement and satisfaction as participatory livelihood programs.
The sectoral difference persists across both states, with Chhattisgarh consistently outperforming Jharkhand within each sector. This suggests that while sectoral approach matters, state-level communication infrastructure remains an independent contributor to satisfaction. The banking sector model—characterized by community-embedded implementing agencies, participatory needs assessment, and structured feedback mechanisms—offers replicable best practices for industrial sector CSR. Companies across all sectors should consider adopting banking sector communication strategies to enhance beneficiary satisfaction.
B.1 Hypothesis Testing Framework
The statistical significance of differences between Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand was tested using Chi-square (χ²) tests of independence for categorical variables and independent samples t-tests for composite scores. All tests were conducted at a 95% confidence level (α = 0.05), with p-values <0.05 indicating statistical significance.
 
Figure 78 Hypothesis Testing Framework — Statistical methods applied to categorical and continuous variables
Interpretation. All hypothesis tests were conducted at a 95% confidence level (α = 0.05), with p-values below 0.05 indicating statistical significance. The framework distinguishes two variable types requiring different analytical approaches. For categorical variables (nominal or ordinal data), the Chi-square (χ²) test of independence was used to examine associations—such as between communication channel choice and environmental impact belief (H₄). For continuous or composite variables (interval or ratio data), the independent samples t-test compared means between Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand, as in the Communication Effectiveness Score comparison (H₁).
The Chi-square test measures whether observed frequencies differ significantly from expected frequencies under the null hypothesis of independence. In this study, it was applied to state-wise awareness comparisons, sectoral distributions, and the channel-perception relationship (H₄: χ²(3) = 24.88, p < 0.001). The independent samples t-test compares continuous variable means across two groups, assuming approximately normal distributions and equal variances (or using Welch's correction when unequal). For composite measures like CES, effect sizes (Cohen's d) are also reported to indicate the magnitude of differences beyond mere statistical significance.
The decision rule remains consistent across all tests: reject the null hypothesis when p < 0.05, confirming that observed differences are unlikely due to random chance alone. This framework ensures methodological rigor and reproducibility, with every inferential conclusion grounded in established statistical principles appropriate to each hypothesis's variable structure.

B.2 Testing Hypothesis 1 (H₁): Difference in Perceived Effectiveness of CSR Communication
Method	A composite Communication Effectiveness Score (CES) was created by summing responses to Q23 (clarity), Q24 (local language), and Q25 (communication channel), each coded on a 3-point scale. An independent samples t-test was performed.
H₁	There is a statistically significant difference in the perceived effectiveness of CSR communication between Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand.
Data Table	State	N	Mean (CES)	Std. Deviation	t-value	p-value	Cohen’s d
1.		Chhattisgarh	200	2.67	0.52	12.41	<0.001	1.24
2.		Jharkhand	200	1.89	0.71			
Model Summary	t(398) = 12.41, p <0.001, Cohen's d = 1.24
Table 27 Testing Hypothesis 1 (H₁)
 
Figure 79 H₁: Communication Effectiveness Score (CES) — Chhattisgarh vs Jharkhand
 Interpretation. This figure presents the statistical testing of Hypothesis 1, which posited a significant difference in perceived CSR communication effectiveness between Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand. The independent samples t-test confirms this hypothesis with strong statistical support. Chhattisgarh's mean CES of 2.67 (SD = 0.52) is significantly higher than Jharkhand's 1.89 (SD = 0.71), yielding a mean difference of 0.78 on the 0-4 scale. The t-statistic of 12.41 with 398 degrees of freedom produces a p-value less than 0.001, far below the α = 0.05 significance threshold, indicating that the observed difference is extremely unlikely to have occurred by random chance.
The effect size, measured by Cohen's d, is 1.24—conventionally interpreted as a large effect in social science research (where d = 0.2 is small, 0.5 is medium, and 0.8 is large). A d of 1.24 indicates that the magnitude of the communication gap is not merely statistically detectable but practically substantial. The 95% confidence interval [0.65, 0.91] does not include zero, further confirming the reliability of the difference. The standard deviations reveal greater variability in Jharkhand (SD = 0.71 vs 0.52), suggesting less consistent communication experiences across districts and communities.
The CES is a composite measure aggregating three foundational communication dimensions: clarity of information local language use, and channel effectiveness. Chhattisgarh's advantage across all three components—particularly its verbal-dominant channel strategy and 80% local language usage—explains the large effect size. This finding forms the statistical foundation for understanding why Chhattisgarh outperforms Jharkhand across most developmental outcome measures examined in subsequent hypotheses.

B.3 Testing Hypothesis 2 (H₂): CSR Communication and Educational Development

Method	A composite Communication Effectiveness Score (CES) was created by summing responses to Q23 (clarity), Q24 (local language), and Q25 (communication channel), each coded on a 3-point scale. An independent samples t-test was performed.
H₁	There is a statistically significant difference in the perceived effectiveness of CSR communication between Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand.
Data Table	State	N	Mean (CES)	Std. Deviation	t-value	p-value	Cohen’s d

Table 28 Testing Hypothesis 2 (H₂)
 
Figure 80 H₂: Communication Effectiveness and Educational Development — Logistic regression odds ratios
 Interpretation. This figure presents a forest plot showing the odds ratios for perceiving educational improvement based on combinations of Communication Effectiveness Score (high/low) and state (CG/JH). Beneficiaries with High CES in Chhattisgarh have 4.8 times higher odds of perceiving educational improvement compared to the reference category (Low CES in Jharkhand). High CES in Jharkhand yields 3.1 times higher odds, while Low CES in Chhattisgarh yields 1.6 times higher odds. The 95% confidence intervals for all predictors do not cross 1.0, indicating statistical significance.
The ordering of effect sizes reveals an additive relationship: both state context and individual communication effectiveness contribute independently to educational perception. High CES in Chhattisgarh produces the largest effect; High CES in Jharkhand produces a substantial but smaller effect; Low CES in Chhattisgarh produces a modest effect; and Low CES in Jharkhand serves as the reference. This pattern confirms that communication effectiveness is not merely a proxy for state-level differences but an independent predictor that operates within both states.
The Nagelkerke R² value of 0.31 indicates that the model explains 31% of variance in educational impact perception—substantial for binary outcome models in social science research. The significant chi-square statistic (χ²(2) = 118.65, p < 0.001) confirms model fit. The practical implication is clear: improving communication effectiveness in Jharkhand to Chhattisgarh's levels could substantially increase educational impact perception, potentially closing the observed gap. Communication investment is not ancillary to CSR but a primary determinant of perceived outcomes.

Figure 81 Interaction Effect of State and Communication Channel on Healthcare Perception
 Interpretation. This figure presents an interaction plot revealing that the effectiveness of communication channels on healthcare perception depends significantly on state context (F(3,389) = 5.14, p = 0.002). In Chhattisgarh (solid line), verbal channels achieve 86% agreement, with modest declines for print (78%), electronic (75%), and social media (72%). In Jharkhand (dashed line), verbal channels achieve 84%—comparable to Chhattisgarh—but then decline steeply to print (68%), electronic (65%), and social media (60%).
The diverging lines indicate that Jharkhand's healthcare perception is disproportionately dependent on verbal channels. Post-hoc Tukey HSD tests confirm that verbal channels in Jharkhand produce significantly higher healthcare perception than print (p = 0.003) or electronic media (p = 0.008). This finding explains the apparent paradox of healthcare near-parity despite Jharkhand's lower overall communication effectiveness: where verbal channels are used, Jharkhand performs nearly as well as Chhattisgarh; where mediated channels dominate, Jharkhand underperforms substantially.
The interaction effect has direct policy implications. For healthcare CSR, investment in verbal, community-embedded communication channels can partially compensate for state-level contextual disadvantages. Jharkhand's healthcare programs that utilize community health workers, ASHA volunteers, and village meetings achieve outcomes comparable to Chhattisgarh. The challenge is scaling this verbal-dominant approach beyond pilot projects. The interaction plot demonstrates that channel choice is not neutral—its effectiveness varies by context, and optimal strategies must be state-specific rather than uniform.

Environmental CSR presents the greatest communication challenge among all sectors because its benefits (biodiversity conservation, groundwater recharge, air quality improvement) are often diffuse and long-term rather than immediate and tangible. Verbal channels address this challenge by enabling two-way explanation, visual demonstration (e.g., watershed mapping), and collective decision-making. The findings indicate that improving environmental communication in Jharkhand requires not just increasing verbal channel usage but enhancing the quality of community dialogue about environmental outcomes.

The practical implication is transformative for CSR policy and practice. Livelihood programs—agricultural training, self-help groups, market linkages, skill development—require ongoing communication, dialogue, and trust-building. Their success depends more on how they are communicated than on where they are implemented. For Jharkhand, closing the livelihood perception gap with Chhattisgarh requires not state-level reforms but targeted investment in communication infrastructure: training implementing agencies in participatory communication, embedding programs within existing community structures, and using local languages consistently.

The CES-OSS correlation (r = 0.66) confirms that communication effectiveness strongly influences overall satisfaction with CSR programs. These relationships are not merely statistically significant but practically substantial, with effect sizes approaching Cohen's conventions for large effects.
State correlates positively with all outcome variables, indicating that Chhattisgarh beneficiaries consistently report higher scores than Jharkhand beneficiaries. However, the moderate magnitude of state correlations (0.28 to 0.53) compared to the strong CES correlations (0.45 to 0.71) confirms the regression finding: communication effectiveness is a more powerful predictor than state context. This implies that improving communication effectiveness in Jharkhand could substantially close the outcome gap with Chhattisgarh, offering a clear policy lever for enhancing CSR impact across both states.
 All five research hypotheses were supported by statistical tests:
•	H₁: Confirmed—communication effectiveness differs significantly between states.
•	H₂: Confirmed—communication strongly predicts educational improvement.
•	H₃: Partially confirmed—channel choice interacts with state for healthcare perception.
•	H₄: Confirmed—communication channel affects environmental impact belief.
•	H₅: Confirmed—communication is the primary driver of overall sectoral impact.
The analysis confirms that CSR communication is not merely ancillary but a statistically significant determinant of perceived societal betterment. The consistent advantage of verbal, community-based communication—particularly prevalent in Chhattisgarh—emerges as a key factor in enhancing awareness, trust, and impact recognition.
 INTEGRATED DISCUSSION OF STATISTICAL FINDINGS
The inferential statistical analysis provides robust, quantitative validation for the patterns described in the descriptive section and directly tests the study's hypotheses.
1.	Confirmation of a Communication Chasm (H₁): The very large effect size (d=1.24) in communication effectiveness between states is not just perceptible but statistically monumental. This forms the bedrock for explaining all subsequent outcome differences.
2.	Communication as the Key Driver (H₂, H₄, H₅): Regression and correlation analyses consistently position Communication Effectiveness as the strongest predictor (β = 0.67) of perceived impact across education, environment, and livelihood. The odds of seeing educational improvement nearly quintuple with effective communication.
3.	The Channel is the Message (H₃, H₄): The ANOVA and Chi-square tests reveal that the choice of communication channel is not neutral. Verbal, interpersonal channels are statistically superior for generating positive perceptions, particularly for complex sectors like healthcare and the environment. Jharkhand's underutilization of this channel statistically explains its lower scores.
4.	The Chhattisgarh Advantage is Quantifiable: The state variable remained a significant predictor in all multivariate models, even after accounting for communication. This suggests that, beyond communication, other systemic factors—governance, corporate culture, and project design—intrinsic to Chhattisgarh's CSR ecosystem contribute to better outcomes.
5.	The Banking Sector Model Emerges: The significant ANOVA result highlights that CSR from the Banking/Other sector generates statistically higher satisfaction than traditional industrial CSR. This quantifies the observed pattern, suggesting that the sectoral approach is a critical variable for impact.
The null hypotheses H₁ through H₅ are rejected. The quantitative evidence confirms that CSR communication is not merely a supportive activity but a statistically significant determinant of developmental outcomes. The gap between Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand is rooted in measurable differences in communication strategy, which interact with state-level and sectoral factors to produce markedly different beneficiary experiences and perceptions of societal betterment.
Summary of Statistical Conclusions
The inferential statistical analysis presented above leads to five robust conclusions that collectively validate the central argument of this thesis. First, the confirmation of a communication chasm between Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand (H₁) with a Cohen's d of 1.24 represents not merely a statistically detectable difference but a substantively monumental gap in how CSR information reaches and resonates with beneficiary communities. This foundational disparity explains the cascade of subsequent outcome differences documented across education, environment, and livelihood sectors. Second, communication effectiveness has been established as the key driver of perceived impact (H₂, H₄, H₅), with regression analyses consistently positioning Communication Effectiveness as the strongest predictor (β = 0.67) of sectoral outcomes. The finding that effective communication nearly quintuples the odds of perceiving educational improvement (odds ratio = 4.8) demonstrates that how CSR is communicated matters as much as—and in some domains more than—what is implemented. Third, the principle that "the channel is the message" (H₃, H₄) has been empirically validated: verbal, interpersonal, community-embedded channels consistently outperform print, electronic, and social media in generating positive beneficiary perceptions, particularly for complex sectors such as healthcare and environmental sustainability where outcomes are not immediately self-evident.
Fourth, the quantifiable Chhattisgarh advantage persists even after accounting for communication effectiveness, indicating that systemic factors beyond messaging—including governance quality, corporate culture, project design, and historical patterns of industrial-community relations—contribute independently to superior outcomes in that state. This finding suggests that while communication strategy can be improved in Jharkhand, closing the inter-state gap requires attention to the broader institutional ecosystem within which CSR operates. Fifth, the emergence of the banking sector as a statistically superior model (ANOVA, p < 0.001) challenges the traditional dominance of industrial sector CSR in resource-rich states. Banking and financial services companies, represented in this study by HDFC Bank, Axis Bank, and Yes Bank, generate higher beneficiary satisfaction than metal, energy, and cement sectors, likely due to their focus on livelihood-oriented, participatory programs implemented through extensive NGO networks rather than physical infrastructure projects. Collectively, these five conclusions confirm that the null hypotheses H₁ through H₅ are rejected with high statistical confidence.
 Theoretical and Policy Implications of the Statistical Findings
The statistical rejection of all five null hypotheses carries profound implications for both CSR theory and policy. Theoretically, these findings extend Stakeholder Theory beyond its traditional focus on identifying stakeholder groups to demonstrating empirically that the quality of communication with primary stakeholders—beneficiary communities—directly determines the perceived legitimacy and effectiveness of corporate social investments. The large effect sizes documented (Cohen's d = 1.24, R² = 0.50 for livelihood outcomes) suggest that communication is not merely an ancillary function but a constitutive dimension of CSR that deserves theoretical recognition equal to financial expenditure and program design. Furthermore, the interaction effect documented in H₃ (State × Channel, p = 0.002) reveals that communication effectiveness is not universal but context-dependent: verbal channels produce high healthcare perception in both states, but Jharkhand's performance degrades sharply when mediated channels are used. This finding supports a contingency approach to CSR communication theory, wherein channel selection must be tailored to state-level factors including literacy rates, linguistic diversity, trust levels, and existing community institutional structures. The strong predictive power of communication effectiveness across diverse sectors (education, environment, livelihood) also suggests that the Diffusion of Innovations framework requires greater attention to the communication strategies that move communities from awareness to adoption, rather than focusing primarily on innovation attributes.
From a policy perspective, the statistical findings provide an evidence-based mandate for regulatory reform. The current CSR governance framework under Section 135 of the Companies Act, 2013, focuses overwhelmingly on financial compliance—whether companies have spent the mandated 2 percent—rather than on whether beneficiaries have received, understood, and acted upon information about available programs. The finding that 40 percent of Jharkhand beneficiaries find CSR communication unclear or inaccessible (Q23) and 46 percent receive no information in their local language (Q24) represents a systemic governance failure that statistical evidence can no longer ignore. The Ministry of Corporate Affairs should consider amending Schedule VII to explicitly recognize "CSR communication and community engagement" as an eligible CSR activity, allowing companies to allocate funds specifically for participatory communication infrastructure. Additionally, the Companies (CSR Policy) Rules should be amended to require reporting on Communication Effectiveness Scores, local language usage, and structured feedback mechanisms as mandatory Key Performance Indicators alongside expenditure disclosures. Without such reforms, CSR in India will remain what the statistical evidence suggests it largely is today: a compliance exercise that generates substantial financial flows but systematically fails to reach, inform, and engage substantial portions of its intended beneficiary population, particularly in linguistically diverse and historically marginalized regions such as Jharkhand.
 CHAPTER 07: RECOMMENDATIONS, AND CONCLUSIONS
Recommendations
Based on the findings and critical reflections of this study, the following recommendations are proposed for companies, implementing agencies, and policymakers to enhance the effectiveness of CSR communication and maximize social impact.
Recommendations for Companies
1.	Adopt the Five-Pillar Framework: Companies should institutionalize the Five-Pillar Framework—distinguishing clearly between the corporate entity (Pillar 1) and the implementation agency (Pillar 2)—to ensure that legal accountability is not conflated with operational delegation.
2.	Prioritize Involvement Communication over Information Communication: Companies must shift from one-way informational communication (press releases, annual reports, pamphlets) to two-way involvement communication (community meetings, participatory planning, feedback mechanisms).
3.	Invest in Local Language and Cultural Adaptation: Communication materials and field staff must be proficient in local languages (Chhattisgarhi, Gondi, Halbi, Santhali, Mundari, Kurukh, Ho) to ensure comprehension and trust.
4.	Establish Structured Community Engagement Forums: Companies should support the formation of Agriculture Production Clusters, Women's Self-Help Groups, Water Management Committees, and similar structures that function as standing communication channels.
5.	Integrate Communication Metrics into CSR Evaluation: Communication Effectiveness Score (CES) should become a standard Key Performance Indicator alongside financial expenditure and project outputs.
Recommendations for Implementing Agencies
1.	Function as Communication Architects, Not Just Program Deliverers: NGOs and implementation partners must prioritize community dialogue, trust-building, and participatory needs assessment as core activities, not ancillary tasks.
2.	Develop Context-Specific Communication Strategies: Agencies must adapt communication approaches to local literacy levels, media access, cultural norms, and linguistic diversity rather than applying uniform templates.
3.	Build Local Staff Capacity: Field staff should be recruited from local communities wherever possible, trained in participatory communication techniques, and empowered to adapt messaging based on community feedback.
4.	Document and Share Best Practices: Agencies should systematically document successful communication strategies and contribute to cross-learning platforms for CSR practitioners.
Recommendations for Policymakers
1.	Amend Schedule VII to Include Communication as a Mandatory CSR Activity: The Ministry of Corporate Affairs should amend Schedule VII of the Companies Act to explicitly recognize "CSR communication and community engagement" as an eligible CSR activity, allowing companies to allocate funds specifically for participatory communication infrastructure.
2.	Mandate Communication Effectiveness Reporting: The Companies (CSR Policy) Rules should be amended to require companies to report on Communication Effectiveness Scores (CES), local language usage, community feedback mechanisms, and beneficiary awareness levels as part of the annual CSR report.
3.	Establish State-Level CSR Communication Guidelines for Tribal Districts: State governments in Chhattisgarh, Jharkhand, and other Schedule V areas should issue operational guidelines requiring companies to develop CSR communication plans in local languages using culturally appropriate channels (community radio, folk media, gram sabha engagement).
4.	Strengthen Implementing Agency Accreditation on Communication Competency: The National CSR Portal should develop accreditation standards for implementing agencies that include documented capacity in local language communication, participatory needs assessment, and community feedback collection.
5.	Institutionalize Cross-State Learning on Communication Best Practices: MCA and NITI Aayog should facilitate structured cross-state learning programs between Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand, recognizing that Chhattisgarh's community-embedded communication model yields superior outcomes.
6.	Reconsider the Infrastructure-Centric Bias in CSR Approval: Policymakers should critically evaluate whether CSR funds for infrastructure projects (roads, buildings) that duplicate government schemes represent optimal use of mandatory CSR expenditure, and instead incentivize micro-level, participatory, communication-intensive interventions.
Conclusions
Restatement of the Research Problem and Approach
This study set out to comparatively examine how Corporate Social Responsibility (CSR) communication influences societal betterment in the states of Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand, with particular reference to educational development, healthcare accessibility, environmental sustainability, and livelihood enhancement among tribal and rural beneficiary communities. The integration of quantitative surveys (n=400) with qualitative stakeholder interviews (n=40) across a retrospective period (FY 2018–19 to FY 2022–23) yielded a body of empirical evidence that is analytically rich, theoretically grounded, and practically significant.
Principal Empirical Conclusions
First, the study concludes unequivocally that CSR communication in Chhattisgarh is significantly more effective than in Jharkhand. The Communication Effectiveness Score differential of 0.78 points (CES: CG=2.67 vs. JH=1.89; Cohen's d=1.24) represents one of the most substantial inter-state gaps identified in this study. This conclusion is not attributable to differences in CSR expenditure magnitude but rather to differences in communication strategy—specifically, in the choice of channels, the degree of local language adaptation, and the architecture of community participation.
Second, CSR communication effectiveness is a statistically significant and practically substantive predictor of impact perception across education (H₂ supported), environment (H₄ supported), and livelihood (H₅ supported; β=0.67, explaining 50% of outcome variance). The finding that communication effectiveness is the strongest predictor of livelihood impact perception—more powerful than state-level contextual differences—represents a novel and policy-relevant finding.
Third, healthcare alone demonstrated near-parity across states (CG:79%, JH:80%), indicating that the inherent visibility of healthcare infrastructure partly compensates for weaker communication planning. However, even for healthcare, communication channel choice significantly moderated outcomes (H₃ partially supported), with verbal community-based channels producing substantially higher impact perceptions than print or electronic media in both states.
Fourth, the qualitative analysis reveals that CSR communication in Chhattisgarh is distinguished by its systematic embedding within community social structures—through agricultural producer clusters, women's self-help groups, and village-level water management committees—that function simultaneously as organizational forms and communication channels. In Jharkhand, a structural tendency toward institutional, one-directional communication channels constrains overall effectiveness, compounded by a historically rooted trust deficit in industrial-community relations.
Theoretical Contributions
This study advances theoretical understanding at the intersection of Stakeholder Theory, the Two-Way Symmetrical Communication Model, Agenda-Setting Theory, and the Diffusion of Innovations framework. Communities that receive CSR communication through face-to-face, dialogic, culturally adapted channels are more likely to become informed, engaged, and convinced stakeholders who move along the adoption continuum from awareness to confirmed benefit. The study provides rare empirical grounding for the application of communication theory in the Indian tribal CSR context—a contribution identified as a significant gap in the existing literature.
The original Five-Pillar Framework developed in this thesis—distinguishing between the Corporate Entity (Pillar 1) and Implementation Agency (Pillar 2), and positioning Communication Strategy (Pillar 5) as the central mediating variable—offers an analytical architecture that addresses the conceptual conflation prevalent in existing CSR scholarship.
The Central Argument: Communication as the Missing Pillar
The central argument of this thesis is that the manner in which CSR activities are communicated is, in contexts of structural informational inequality such as those prevailing in tribal and rural communities of Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand, at least as consequential as the nature of the activities themselves in determining the extent to which corporate social investment translates into genuine community awareness, trust, and participatory engagement.
The evidence from 400 beneficiaries and 40 stakeholders across ten districts demonstrates that CSR communication is not merely ancillary to CSR implementation but constitutive of it. Where communication is verbal, participatory, linguistically appropriate, and embedded within community structures, CSR outcomes improve across all developmental domains. Where communication is print-based, one-directional, institutionally mediated, and linguistically inaccessible, CSR outcomes diminish correspondingly.
Policy Implications
From a policy perspective, this study concludes that CSR governance in India must move beyond expenditure compliance towards communication accountability. The Ministry of Corporate Affairs, state CSR cells, and corporate CSR committees should collectively establish minimum standards for beneficiary-facing communication: mandatory local-language material development, structured community feedback mechanisms, and the inclusion of communication effectiveness metrics in CSR impact assessment frameworks.
The Five-Pillar Framework offers a practical policy architecture for achieving this shift. By distinguishing clearly between the corporate entity (which holds legal accountability) and the implementation agency (which executes communication and programming), the framework enables precise allocation of responsibility. By positioning Communication Strategy as a distinct pillar rather than an implicit activity, the framework makes visible what current law renders invisible.
Final Reflection: From Hypothesis to Reality
The researcher concludes with a critical observation: Corporate Social Responsibility in India's current legal framework remains a hypothesis—an assumption that mandatory expenditure will automatically translate into social benefit. This study demonstrates that the hypothesis is only validated when communication is participatory, when communities are involved, when local languages are used, and when implementing agencies act as genuine intermediaries rather than passive delivery mechanisms.
Bhaskar Chatterjee's framework, while groundbreaking in establishing India's mandatory CSR regime, is fundamentally incomplete because it does not articulate how CSR should be implemented and how it should be communicated. The 2021 amendments represent progress, but they do not yet mandate the involvement-oriented, two-way communication that this study has demonstrated as the primary determinant of perceived social impact.
The Five-Pillar Framework proposed in this thesis—alongside the empirical evidence from Chhattisgarh and Jharkhand—offers a path forward. Working universally, working according to people's needs, working with people's participation, and working through people's trust: this is the only and the best way to fulfill Corporate Social Responsibility. Until the law mandates not just how much to spend but how to communicate, how to engage, and how to involve, CSR in India will remain what it largely is today: a compliance exercise, not a transformative social practice.
Corporate Social Responsibility in the current legal framework remains a hypothesis requiring reform. This study provides the evidence, the framework, and the direction for that reform.
Suggestions for Future Research
This study opens several avenues for future research on CSR communication and its impact on society:
1.	Longitudinal Studies: Research examining the long-term impact of CSR communication on beneficiary awareness, behavior change, and community development outcomes over extended time periods (5-10 years).
2.	Comparative International Studies: Cross-country comparisons to identify context-specific factors that influence CSR communication effectiveness across different regulatory and cultural environments.
3.	Digital and Technology-Mediated CSR Communication: Investigation of the role of mobile technology, community radio, and emerging digital platforms in enhancing CSR communication reach and effectiveness in rural areas.
4.	Cost-Effectiveness Analysis of Communication Channels: Systematic evaluation of the cost-effectiveness of different CSR communication channels (verbal, print, electronic, social media) relative to their impact outcomes.
5.	Marginalized Group Communication Needs: Dedicated studies on the communication needs and preferences of specific marginalized groups, including women, tribal communities, persons with disabilities, and extremely poor households.
6.	Instrument Development for Communication Effectiveness: Further validation and refinement of standardized instruments for measuring CSR communication effectiveness across diverse Indian contexts.
7.	Comparative Analysis of PSU and Private Sector CSR Communication: Research comparing communication strategies and outcomes between Public Sector Undertakings and private companies operating under the same statutory mandate.
8.	Implementation Agency Capacity Building: Action research on effective models for building communication competency among NGO implementing agencies in tribal and rural contexts.
9.	Policy Implementation Research: Studies examining how state-level CSR cells interpret and implement national CSR guidelines, and how policy variations influence communication outcomes at the district level.
